   <TITLE>Of Custom AND EDUCATION</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Custom AND EDUCATION</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MEN'S thoughts, are much according to their



<P>inclination; their discourse and speeches,



<P>according to their learning and infused opinions;



<P>but their deeds, are after as they have been accus-



<P>tomed.&nbsp; And therefore, as Machiavel well noteth



<P>(though in an evil-favored instance), there is no



<P>trusting to the force of nature, nor to the bravery



<P>of words, except it be corroborate by custom.&nbsp; His



<P>instance is, that for the achieving of a desperate



<P>conspiracy, a man should not rest upon the fierce-



<P>ness of any man's nature, or his resolute under-



<P>takings; but take such an one, as hath had his



<P>hands formerly in blood.&nbsp; But Machiavel knew not



<P>of a Friar Clement, nor a Ravillac, nor a Jaureguy,



<P>nor a Baltazar Gerard; yet his rule holdeth still,



<P>that nature, nor the engagement of words, are not



<P>so forcible, as custom.&nbsp; Only superstition is now so



<P>well advanced, that men of the first blood, are as



<P>firm as butchers by occupation; and votary reso-



<P>lution, is made equipollent to custom, even in mat-



<P>ter of blood.&nbsp; In other things, the predominancy of



<P>custom is everywhere visible; insomuch as a man



<P>would wonder, to hear men profess, protest, en-



<P>gage, give great words, and then do, just as they



<P>have done before; as if they were dead images,



<P>and engines moved only by the wheels of custom.



<P>We see also the reign or tyranny of custom, what



<P>it is.&nbsp; The Indians (I mean the sect of their wise men)



<P>lay themselves quietly upon a stock of wood, and



<P>so sacrifice themselves by fire.&nbsp; Nay, the wives



<P>strive to be burned, with the corpses of their hus-



<P>bands.&nbsp; The lads of Sparta, of ancient time, were



<P>wont to be scourged upon the altar of Diana, with-



<P>out so much as queching.&nbsp; I remember, in the be-



<P>ginning of Queen Elizabeth's time of England, an



<P>Irish rebel condemned, put up a petition to the



<P>deputy, that he might be hanged in a withe, and



<P>not in an halter; because it had been so used, with



<P>former rebels.&nbsp; There be monks in Russia, for pen-



<P>ance, that will sit a whole night in a vessel of water,



<P>till they be engaged with hard ice.&nbsp; Many examples



<P>may be put of the force of custom, both upon mind



<P>and body.&nbsp; Therefore, since custom is the principal



<P>magistrate of man's life, let men by all means en-



<P>deavor, to obtain good customs.&nbsp; Certainly custom



<P>is most perfect, when it beginneth in young years:



<P>this we call education; which is, in effect, but an



<P>early custom.&nbsp; So we see, in languages, the tongue



<P>is more pliant to all expressions and sounds, the



<P>joints are more supple, to all feats of activity and



<P>motions,&nbsp; in youth than afterwards.&nbsp; For it is true,



<P>that late learners cannot so well take the ply; ex-



<P>cept it be in some minds, that have not suffered



<P>themselves to fix, but have kept themselves open,



<P>and prepared to receive continual amendment,



<P>which is exceeding rare.&nbsp; But if the force of cus-



<P>tom simple and separate, be great, the force of



<P>custom copulate and conjoined and collegiate, is



<P>far greater.&nbsp; For there example teacheth, company



<P>comforteth, emulation quickeneth, glory raiseth:



<P>so as in such places the force of custom is in his



<P>exaltation.&nbsp; Certainly the great multiplication of



<P>virtues upon human nature, resteth upon socie-



<P>ties well ordained and disciplined.&nbsp; For common-



<P>wealths, and good governments, do nourish virtue



<P>grown but do not much mend the deeds.&nbsp; But the



<P>misery is, that the most effectual means, are now



<P>applied to the ends, least to be desired.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Usury</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Usury</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MANY have made witty invectives against



<P>usury.&nbsp; They say that it is a pity, the devil



<P>should have God's part, which is the tithe.&nbsp; That the



<P>usurer is the greatest Sabbath-breaker, because his



<P>plough goeth every Sunday.&nbsp; That the usurer is the



<P>drone, that Virgil speaketh of;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Ignavum fucos pecus a praesepibus arcent.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>That the usurer breaketh the first law, that was



<P>made for mankind after the fall, which was, in



<P>sudore vultus tui comedes panem tuum; not, in



<P>sudore vultus alieni.&nbsp; That usurers should have



<P>orange-tawny bonnets, because they do judaize.



<P>That it is against nature for money to beget money;



<P>and the like.&nbsp; I say this only, that usury is a conces-



<P>sum propter duritiem cordis; for since there must



<P>be borrowing and lending, and men are so hard



<P>of heart, as they will not lend freely, usury must



<P>be permitted.&nbsp; Some others, have made suspicious



<P>and cunning propositions of banks, discovery of



<P>men's estates, and other inventions.&nbsp; But few have



<P>spoken of usury usefully.&nbsp; It is good to set before us,



<P>the incommodities and commodities of usury, that



<P>the good, may be either weighed out or culled out;



<P>and warily to provide, that while we make forth



<P>to that which is better, we meet not with that



<P>which is worse.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The discommodities of usury are, First, that it



<P>makes fewer merchants.&nbsp; For were it not for this



<P>lazy trade of usury, money would not he still, but



<P>would in great part be employed upon merchan-



<P>dizing; which is the vena porta of wealth in a state.



<P>The second, that it makes poor merchants.&nbsp; For, as



<P>a farmer cannot husband his ground so well, if he



<P>sit at a great rent; so the merchant cannot drive



<P>his trade so well, if he sit at great usury.&nbsp; The third



<P>is incident to the other two; and that is the decay of



<P>customs of kings or states, which ebb or flow, with



<P>merchandizing.&nbsp; The fourth, that it bringeth the



<P>treasure of a realm, or state, into a few hands.&nbsp; For



<P>the usurer being at certainties, and others at uncer-



<P>tainties, at the end of the game, most of the money



<P>will be in the box; and ever a state flourisheth,



<P>when wealth is more equally spread.&nbsp; The fifth,



<P>that it beats down the price of land; for the em-



<P>ployment of money, is chiefly either merchandiz-



<P>ing or purchasing; and usury waylays both.&nbsp; The



<P>sixth, that it doth dull and damp all industries, im-



<P>provements, and new inventions, wherein money



<P>would be stirring, if it were not for this slug.&nbsp; The



<P>last, that it is the canker and ruin of many men's



<P>estates; which, in process of time, breeds a public



<P>poverty.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the other side, the commodities of usury are,



<P>first, that howsoever usury in some respect hinder-



<P>eth merchandizing, yet in some other it advanceth



<P>it; for it is certain that the greatest part of trade is



<P>driven by young merchants, upon borrowing at



<P>interest; so as if the usurer either call in, or keep



<P>back, his money, there will ensue, presently, a



<P>great stand of trade.&nbsp; The second is, that were it not



<P>for this easy borrowing upon interest, men's neces-



<P>sities would draw upon them a most sudden un-



<P>doing; in that they would be forced to sell their



<P>means (be it lands or goods) far under foot; and so,



<P>whereas usury doth but gnaw upon them, bad



<P>markets would swallow them quite up.&nbsp; As for



<P>mortgaging or pawning, it will little mend the



<P>matter: for either men will not take pawns with-



<P>out use; or if they do, they will look precisely for



<P>the forfeiture.&nbsp; I remember a cruel moneyed man



<P>in the country, that would say, The devil take this



<P>usury, it keeps us from forfeitures, of mortgages



<P>and bonds.&nbsp; The third and last is, that it is a vanity



<P>to conceive, that there would be ordinary borrow-



<P>ing without profit; and it is impossible to conceive,



<P>the number of inconveniences that will ensue, if



<P>borrowing be cramped.&nbsp; Therefore to speak of the



<P>abolishing of usury is idle.&nbsp; All states have ever had



<P>it, in one kind or rate, or other.&nbsp; So as that opinion



<P>must be sent to Utopia.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To speak now of the reformation, and reigle-



<P>ment, of usury; how the discommodities of it may



<P>be best avoided, and the commodities retained.&nbsp; It



<P>appears, by the balance of commodities and dis-



<P>commodities of usury, two things are to be recon-



<P>ciled.&nbsp; The one, that the tooth of usury be grinded,



<P>that it bite not too much; the other, that there be



<P>left open a means, to invite moneyed men to lend



<P>to the merchants, for the continuing and quicken-



<P>ing of trade.&nbsp; This cannot be done, except you intro-



<P>duce two several sorts of usury, a less and a greater.



<P>For if you reduce usury to one low rate, it will ease



<P>the common borrower, but the merchant will be



<P>to seek for money.&nbsp; And it is to be noted, that the



<P>trade of merchandize, being the most lucrative,



<P>may bear usury at a good rate; other contracts



<P>not so.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To serve both intentions, the way would be



<P>briefly thus.&nbsp; That there be two rates of usury:



<P>the one free, and general for all; the other under



<P>license only, to certain persons, and in certain



<P>places of merchandizing.&nbsp; First, therefore, let usury



<P>in general, be reduced to five in the hundred; and



<P>let that rate be proclaimed, to be free and current;



<P>and let the state shut itself out, to take any penalty



<P>for the same.&nbsp; This will preserve borrowing, from



<P>any general stop or dryness.&nbsp; This will ease infinite



<P>borrowers in the country.&nbsp; This will, in good part,



<P>raise the price of land, because land purchased



<P>at sixteen years' purchase will yield six in the



<P>hundred, and somewhat more; whereas this rate



<P>of interest, yields but five.&nbsp; This by like reason



<P>will encourage, and edge, industrious and profit-



<P>able improvements; because many will rather



<P>venture in that kind, than take five in the hun-



<P>dred, especially having been used to greater profit.



<P>Secondly, let there be certain persons licensed,



<P>to lend to known merchants, upon usury at a



<P>higher rate; and let it be with the cautions fol-



<P>lowing.&nbsp; Let the rate be, even with the merchant



<P>himself, somewhat more easy than that he used



<P>formerly to pay; for by that means, all bor-



<P>rowers, shall have some ease by this reformation,



<P>be he merchant, or whosoever.&nbsp; Let it be no



<P>bank or common stock, but every man be master



<P>of his own money.&nbsp; Not that I altogether mis-



<P>like banks, but they will hardly be brooked, in



<P>regard of certain suspicions.&nbsp; Let the state be



<P>answered some small matter for the license, and



<P>the rest left to the lender; for if the abatement be



<P>but small, it will no whit discourage the lender.



<P>For he, for example, that took before ten or nine in



<P>the hundred, will sooner descend to eight in the



<P>hundred than give over his trade of usury, and go



<P>from certain gains, to gains of hazard.&nbsp; Let these



<P>licensed lenders be in number indefinite, but re-



<P>strained to certain principal cities and towns of



<P>merchandizing; for then they will be hardly able



<P>to color other men's moneys in the country: so as



<P>the license of nine will not suck away the current



<P>rate of five; for no man will send his moneys far



<P>off, nor put them into unknown hands.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If it be objected that this doth in a sort authorize



<P>usury, which before, was in some places but per-



<P>missive; the answer is, that it is better to mitigate



<P>usury, by declaration, than to suffer it to rage, by



<P>connivance.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <TITLE>Of Judicature</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Judicature</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>JUDGES ought to remember, that their office is



<P>jus dicere, and not jus dare; to interpret law,



<P>and not to make law, or give law.&nbsp; Else will it be



<P>like the authority, claimed by the Church of Rome,



<P>which under pretext of exposition of Scripture,



<P>doth not stick to add and alter; and to pronounce



<P>that which they do not find; and by show of an-



<P>tiquity, to introduce novelty.&nbsp; Judges ought to be



<P>more learned, than witty, more reverend, than



<P>plausible,and more advised, than confident.&nbsp; Above



<P>all things, integrity is their portion and proper



<P>virtue.&nbsp; Cursed (saith the law) is he that removeth



<P>the landmark.&nbsp; The mislayer of a mere-stone is to



<P>blame.&nbsp; But it is the unjust judge, that is the capital



<P>remover of landmarks, when he defineth amiss, of



<P>lands and property.&nbsp; One foul sentence doth more



<P>hurt, than many foul examples.&nbsp; For these do but



<P>corrupt the stream, the other corrupteth the foun-



<P>tain.&nbsp; So with Solomon, Fons turbatus, et vena



<P>corrupta, est justus cadens in causa sua coram



<P>adversario.&nbsp; The office of judges may have reference



<P>unto the parties that use, unto the advocates that



<P>plead, unto the clerks and ministers of justice



<P>underneath them, and to the sovereign or state



<P>above them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>First, for the causes or parties that sue.&nbsp; There be



<P>(saith the Scripture) that turn judgment, into



<P>wormwood; and surely there be also, that turn it



<P>into vinegar; for injustice maketh it bitter, and



<P>delays make it sour.&nbsp; The principal duty of a judge,



<P>is to suppress force and fraud; whereof force is the



<P>more pernicious, when it is open, and fraud, when



<P>it is close and disguised.&nbsp; Add thereto contentious



<P>suits, which ought to be spewed out, as the surfeit



<P>of courts.&nbsp; A judge ought to prepare his way to a



<P>just sentence, as God useth to prepare his way, by



<P>raising valleys and taking down hills: so when



<P>there appeareth on either side an high hand, vio-



<P>lent prosecution, cunning advantages taken, com-



<P>bination, power, great counsel, then is the virtue



<P>of a judge seen, to make inequality equal; that he



<P>may plant his judgment as upon an even ground.



<P>Qui fortiter emungit, elicit sanguinem; and where



<P>the wine-press is hard wrought, it yields a harsh



<P>wine, that tastes of the grape-stone.&nbsp; Judges must



<P>beware of hard constructions, and strained infer-



<P>ences; for there is no worse torture, than the tor-



<P>ture of laws.&nbsp; Specially in case of laws penal, they



<P>ought to have care, that that which was meant for



<P>terror, be not turned into rigor; and that they



<P>bring not upon the people, that shower whereof



<P>the Scripture speaketh, Pluet super eos laqueos;



<P>for penal laws pressed, are a shower of snares upon



<P>the people.&nbsp; Therefore let penal laws, if they have



<P>been sleepers of long, or if they be grown unfit for



<P>the present time, be by wise judges confined in the



<P>execution: Judicis officium est, ut res, ita tempora



<P>rerum, etc.&nbsp; In causes of life and death, judges ought



<P>(as far as the law permitteth) in justice to remem-



<P>ber mercy; and to cast a severe eye upon the



<P>example, but a merciful eye upon the person.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Secondly, for the advocates and counsel that



<P>plead.&nbsp; Patience and gravity of hearing, is an essen-



<P>tial part of justice; and an overspeaking judge is no



<P>well-tuned cymbal.&nbsp; It is no grace to a judge, first



<P>to find that, which he might have heard in due



<P>time from the bar; or to show quickness of conceit,



<P>in cutting off evidence or counsel too short; or to



<P>prevent information by questions, though perti-



<P>nent.&nbsp; The parts of a judge in hearing, are four: to



<P>direct the evidence; to moderate length, repetition,



<P>or impertinency of speech; to recapitulate, select,



<P>and collate the material points, of that which hath



<P>been said; and to give the rule or sentence.&nbsp; What-



<P>soever is above these is too much; and proceedeth



<P>either of glory, and willingness to speak, or of im-



<P>patience to hear, or of shortness of memory, or of



<P>want of a staid and equal attention.&nbsp; It is a strange



<P>thing to see, that the boldness of advocates should



<P>prevail with judges; whereas they should imitate



<P>God, in whose seat they sit; who represseth the pre-



<P>sumptuous, and giveth grace to the modest.&nbsp; But it



<P>is more strange, that judges should have noted



<P>favorites; which cannot but cause multiplication



<P>of fees, and suspicion of by-ways.&nbsp; There is due from



<P>the judge to the advocate, some commendation



<P>and gracing, where causes are well handled and



<P>fair pleaded; especially towards the side which



<P>obtaineth not; for that upholds in the client, the



<P>reputation of his counsel, and beats down in him



<P>the conceit of his cause.&nbsp; There is likewise due to the



<P>public, a civil reprehension of advocates, where



<P>there appeareth cunning counsel, gross neglect,



<P>slight information, indiscreet pressing, or an over-



<P>bold defence.&nbsp; And let not the counsel at the bar,



<P>chop with the judge, nor wind himself into the



<P>handling of the cause anew, after the judge hath



<P>declared his sentence; but, on the other side, let



<P>not the judge meet the cause half way, nor give



<P>occasion to the party, to say his counsel or proofs



<P>were not heard.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Thirdly, for that that concerns clerks and minis-



<P>ters.&nbsp; The place of justice is an hallowed place; and



<P>therefore not only the bench, but the foot-place;



<P>and precincts and purprise thereof, ought to be



<P>preserved without scandal and corruption.&nbsp; For



<P>certainly grapes (as the Scripture saith) will not



<P>be gathered of thorns or thistles; either can justice



<P>yield her fruit with sweetness, amongst the briars



<P>and brambles of catching and polling clerks, and



<P>ministers.&nbsp; The attendance of courts, is subject to



<P>four bad instruments.&nbsp; First, certain persons that



<P>are sowers of suits; which make the court swell,



<P>and the country pine.&nbsp; The second sort is of those,



<P>that engage courts in quarrels of jurisdiction, and



<P>are not truly amici curiae, but parasiti curiae, in



<P>puffing a court up beyond her bounds, for their



<P>own scraps and advantage.&nbsp; The third sort, is of



<P>those that may be accounted the left hands of



<P>courts; persons that are full of nimble and sinister



<P>tricks and shifts, whereby they pervert the plain



<P>and direct courses of courts, and bring justice into



<P>oblique lines and labyrinths.&nbsp; And the fourth, is the



<P>poller and exacter of fees; which justifies the com-



<P>mon resemblance of the courts of justice, to the



<P>bush whereunto, while the sheep flies for defence



<P>in weather, he is sure to lose part of his fleece.&nbsp; On



<P>the other side, an ancient clerk, skilful in prece-



<P>dents, wary in proceeding, and understanding in



<P>the business of the court, is an excellent finger of



<P>a court; and doth many times point the way to the



<P>judge himself.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Fourthly, for that which may concern the sov-



<P>ereign and estate.&nbsp; Judges ought above all to re-



<P>member the conclusion of the Roman Twelve



<P>Tables; Salus populi suprema lex; and to know



<P>that laws, except they be in order to that end, are



<P>but things captious, and oracles not well inspired.



<P>Therefore it is an happy thing in a state, when



<P>kings and states do often consult with judges; and



<P>again, when judges do often consult with the king



<P>and state: the one, when there is matter of law,



<P>intervenient in business of state; the other, when



<P>there is some consideration of state, intervenient



<P>in matter of law.&nbsp; For many times the things de-



<P>duced to judgment may be meum and tuum, when



<P>the reason and consequence thereof may trench to



<P>point of estate: I call matter of estate, not only the



<P>parts of sovereignty, but whatsoever introduceth



<P>any great alteration, or dangerous precedent; or



<P>concerneth manifestly any great portion of peo-



<P>ple.&nbsp; And let no man weakly conceive, that just



<P>laws and true policy have any antipathy; for they



<P>are like the spirits and sinews, that one moves with



<P>the other.&nbsp; Let judges also remember, that Solo-



<P>mon's throne was supported by lions on both sides:



<P>let them be lions, but yet lions under the throne;



<P>being circumspect that they do not check or oppose



<P>any points of sovereignty.&nbsp; Let not judges also be



<P>ignorant of their own right, as to think there is not



<P>left to them, as a principal part of their office, a



<P>wise use and application of laws.&nbsp; For they may



<P>remember, what the apostle saith of a greater law



<P>than theirs; Nos scimus quia lex bona est, modo



<P>quis ea utatur legitime.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Building</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Building</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>HOUSES are built to live in, and not to look on;



<P>therefore let use be preferred before uni-



<P>formity, except where both may be had.&nbsp; Leave



<P>the goodly fabrics of houses, for beauty only, to



<P>the enchanted palaces of the poets; who build them



<P>with small cost.&nbsp; He that builds a fair house, upon



<P>an ill seat, committeth himself to prison.&nbsp; Neither



<P>do I reckon it an ill seat, only where the air is un-



<P>wholesome; but likewise where the air is unequal;



<P>as you shall see many fine seats set upon a knap of



<P>ground, environed with higher hills round about



<P>it; whereby the heat of the sun is pent in, and the



<P>wind gathereth as in troughs; so as you shall have,



<P>and that suddenly, as great diversity of heat and



<P>cold as if you dwelt in several places.&nbsp; Neither is it



<P>ill air only that maketh an ill seat, but ill ways, ill



<P>markets; and, if you will consult with Momus, ill



<P>neighbors.&nbsp; I speak not of many more; want of



<P>water; want of wood, shade, and shelter; want of



<P>fruitfulness, and mixture of grounds of several



<P>natures; want of prospect; want of level grounds;



<P>want of places at some near distance for sports of



<P>hunting, hawking, and races; too near the sea, too



<P>remote; having the commodity of navigable rivers,



<P>or the discommodity of their overflowing; too far



<P>off from great cities, which may hinder business,



<P>or too near them, which lurcheth all provisions,



<P>and maketh everything dear; where a man hath



<P>a great living laid together, and where he is



<P>scanted: all which, as it is impossible perhaps to



<P>find together, so it is good to know them, and think



<P>of them, that a man may take as many as he can;



<P>and if he have several dwellings, that he sort them



<P>so&nbsp; that what he wanteth in the one, he may find in



<P>the other.&nbsp; Lucullus answered Pompey well; who,



<P>when he saw his stately galleries, and rooms so



<P>large and lightsome, in one of his houses, said,



<P>Surely an excellent place for summer, but how do



<P>you in winter? Lucullus answered, Why, do you



<P>not think me as wise as some fowl are, that ever



<P>change their abode towards the winter?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To pass from the seat, to the house itself; we will



<P>do as Cicero doth in the orator's art; who writes



<P>books De Oratore, and a book he entitles Orator;



<P>whereof the former, delivers the precepts of the



<P>art, and the latter, the perfection.&nbsp; We will there-



<P>fore describe a princely palace, making a brief



<P>model thereof.&nbsp; For it is strange to see, now in



<P>Europe, such huge buildings as the Vatican and



<P>Escurial and some others be, and yet scarce a very



<P>fair room in them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>First, therefore, I say you cannot have a perfect



<P>palace except you have two several sides; a side for



<P>the banquet, as it is spoken of in the book of Hester,



<P>and a side for the household; the one for feasts and



<P>triumphs, and the other for dwelling.&nbsp; I understand



<P>both these sides to be not only returns, but parts



<P>of the front; and to be uniform without, though



<P>severally partitioned within; and to be on both



<P>sides of a great and stately tower, in the midst of



<P>the front, that, as it were, joineth them together



<P>on either hand.&nbsp; I would have on the side of the ban-



<P>quet, in front, one only goodly room above stairs,



<P>of some forty foot high; and under it a room for a



<P>dressing, or preparing place, at times of triumphs.



<P>On the other side, which is the household side, I



<P>wish it divided at the first, into a hall and a chapel



<P>(with a partition between); both of good state and



<P>bigness; and those not to go all the length, but to



<P>have at the further end, a winter and a summer



<P>parlor, both fair.&nbsp; And under these rooms, a fair



<P>and large cellar, sunk under ground; and likewise



<P>some privy kitchens, with butteries and pantries,



<P>and the like.&nbsp; As for the tower, I would have it two



<P>stories, of eighteen foot high apiece, above the two



<P>wings; and a goodly leads upon the top,railed with



<P>statuas interposed; and the same tower to be di-



<P>vided into rooms, as shall be thought fit.&nbsp; The stairs



<P>likewise to the upper rooms, let them be upon a



<P>fair open newel, and finely railed in, with images



<P>of wood, cast into a brass color; and a very fair



<P>landing-place at the top.&nbsp; But this to be, if you do



<P>not point any of the lower rooms, for a dining place



<P>of servants.&nbsp; For otherwise, you shall have the ser-



<P>vants' dinner after your own: for the steam of it,



<P>will come up as in a tunnel.&nbsp; And so much for the



<P>front.&nbsp; Only I understand the height of the first



<P>stairs to be sixteen foot, which is the height of the



<P>lower room.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Beyond this front, is there to be a fair court, but



<P>three sides of it, of a far lower building than the



<P>front.&nbsp; And in all the four corners of that court, fair



<P>staircases, cast into turrets, on the outside, and not



<P>within the row of buildings themselves.&nbsp; But those



<P>towers, are not to be of the height of the front, but



<P>rather proportionable to the lower building.&nbsp; Let



<P>the court not be paved, for that striketh up a great



<P>heat in summer, and much cold in winter.&nbsp; But



<P>only some side alleys, with a cross, and the quar-



<P>ters to graze, being kept shorn, but not too near



<P>shorn.&nbsp; The row of return on the banquet side, let it



<P>be all stately galleries: in which galleries let there



<P>be three, or five, fine cupolas in the length of it,



<P>placed at equal distance; and fine colored windows



<P>of several works.&nbsp; On the household side, chambers



<P>of presence and ordinary entertainments, with



<P>some bed-chambers; and let all three sides be a



<P>double house, without thorough lights on the sides,



<P>that you may have rooms from the sun, both for



<P>forenoon and afternoon.&nbsp; Cast it also, that you may



<P>have rooms, both for summer and winter; shady



<P>for summer, and warm for winter.&nbsp; You shall have



<P>sometimes fair houses so full of glass, that one can-



<P>not tell where to become, to be out of the sun or



<P>cold.&nbsp; For inbowed windows, I hold them of good



<P>use (in cities, indeed, upright do better, in respect



<P>of the uniformity towards the street); for they be



<P>pretty retiring places for conference; and besides,



<P>they keep both the wind and sun off; for that



<P>which would strike almost through the room, doth



<P>scarce pass the window.&nbsp; But let them be but few,



<P>four in the court, on the sides only.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Beyond this court, let there be an inward court,



<P>of the same square and height; which is to be en-



<P>vironed with the garden on all sides; and in the



<P>inside, cloistered on all sides, upon decent and



<P>beautiful arches, as high as the first story.&nbsp; On the



<P>under story, towards the garden, let it be turned



<P>to a grotto, or a place of shade, or estivation.&nbsp; And



<P>only have opening and windows towards the gar-



<P>den; and be level upon the floor, no whit sunken



<P>under ground, to avoid all dampishness.&nbsp; And let



<P>there be a fountain, or some fair work of statuas, in



<P>the midst of this court; and to be paved as the other



<P>court was.&nbsp; These buildings to be for privy lodgings



<P>on both sides; and the end for privy galleries.



<P>Whereof you must foresee that one of them be for



<P>an infirmary, if the prince or any special person



<P>should be sick, with chambers, bed-chamber, ante-



<P>camera, and recamera joining to it.&nbsp; This upon the



<P>second story.&nbsp; Upon the ground story, a fair gallery,



<P>open, upon pillars; and upon the third story like-



<P>wise, an open gallery, upon pillars, to take the



<P>prospect and freshness of the garden.&nbsp; At both cor-



<P>ners of the further side, by way of return, let there



<P>be two delicate or rich cabinets, daintily paved,



<P>richly hanged, glazed with crystalline glass, and



<P>a rich cupola in the midst; and all other elegancy



<P>that may be thought upon.&nbsp; In the upper gallery



<P>too, I wish that there may be, if the place will yield



<P>it, some fountains running in divers places from



<P>the wall, with some fine avoidances.&nbsp; And thus



<P>much for the model of the palace; save that you



<P>must have, before you come to the front, three



<P>courts.&nbsp; A green court plain, with a wall about it;



<P>a second court of the same, but more garnished,



<P>with little turrets, or rather embellishments, upon



<P>the wall; and a third court, to make a square with



<P>the front, but not to be built, nor yet enclosed with



<P>a naked wall, but enclosed with terraces, leaded



<P>aloft, and fairly garnished, on the three sides; and



<P>cloistered on the inside, with pillars, and not with



<P>arches below.&nbsp; As for offices, let them stand at dis-



<P>tance, with some low galleries, to pass from them



<P>to the palace itself.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Adversity</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Adversity</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>IT WAS an high speech of Seneca (after the



<P>manner of the Stoics), that the good things,



<P>which belong to prosperity, are to be wished; but



<P>the good things, that belong to adversity, are to be



<P>admired.&nbsp; Bona rerum secundarum optabilia; ad-



<P>versarum mirabilia.&nbsp; Certainly if miracles be the



<P>command over nature, they appear most in adver-



<P>sity.&nbsp; It is yet a higher speech of his, than the other



<P>(much too high for a heathen), It is true greatness,



<P>to have in one the frailty of a man, and the security



<P>of a God.&nbsp; Vere magnum habere fragilitatem homi-



<P>nis, securitatem Dei.&nbsp; This would have done better



<P>in poesy, where transcendences are more allowed.



<P>And the poets indeed have been busy with it; for



<P>it is in effect the thing, which figured in that



<P>strange fiction of the ancient poets, which seemeth



<P>not to be without mystery; nay, and to have some



<P>approach to the state of a Christian; that Hercules,



<P>when he went to unbind Prometheus (by whom



<P>human nature is represented), sailed the length of



<P>the great ocean, in an earthen pot or pitcher; lively



<P>describing Christian resolution, that saileth in the



<P>frail bark of the flesh, through the waves of the



<P>world.&nbsp; But to speak in a mean.&nbsp; The virtue of pros-



<P>perity, is temperance; the virtue of adversity, is



<P>fortitude; which in morals is the more heroical



<P>virtue.&nbsp; Prosperity is the blessing of the Old Testa-



<P>ment; adversity is the blessing of the New; which



<P>carrieth the greater benediction, and the clearer



<P>revelation of God's favor.&nbsp; Yet even in the Old



<P>Testament, if you listen to David's harp, you shall



<P>hear as many hearse-like airs as carols; and the



<P>pencil of the Holy Ghost hath labored more in de-



<P>scribing the afflictions of Job, than the felicities of



<P>Solomon.&nbsp; Prosperity is not without many fears



<P>and distastes; and adversity is not without com-



<P>forts and hopes.&nbsp; We see in needle-works and em-



<P>broideries, it is more pleasing to have a lively work,



<P>upon a sad and solemn ground, than to have a dark



<P>and melancholy work, upon a lightsome ground:



<P>judge therefore of the pleasure of the heart, by the



<P>pleasure of the eye.&nbsp; Certainly virtue is like precious



<P>odors, most fragrant when they are incensed, or



<P>crushed: for prosperity doth best discover vice, but



<P>adversity doth best discover virtue.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Gardens</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Gardens</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>G0D Almighty first planted a garden.&nbsp; And



<P>indeed it is the purest of human pleasures.



<P>It is the greatest refreshment to the spirits of man;



<P>without which, buildings and palaces are but



<P>gross handiworks; and a man shall ever see, that



<P>when ages grow to civility and elegancy, men



<P>come to build stately sooner than to garden finely;



<P>as if gardening were the greater perfection.&nbsp; I do



<P>hold it, in the royal ordering of gardens, there



<P>ought to be gardens, for all the months in the year;



<P>in which severally things of beauty may be then



<P>in season.&nbsp; For December, and January, and the



<P>latter part of November, you must take such things



<P>as are green all winter: holly; ivy; bays; juniper;



<P>cypress-trees; yew; pine-apple-trees; fir-trees;



<P>rosemary; lavender; periwinkle, the white, the



<P>purple, and the blue; germander; flags; orange-



<P>trees; lemon-trees; and myrtles, if they be stoved;



<P>and sweet marjoram, warm set.&nbsp; There followeth,



<P>for the latter part of January and February, the



<P>mezereon-tree, which then blossoms; crocus ver-



<P>nus, both the yellow and the grey; primroses,



<P>anemones; the early tulippa; hyacinthus orien-



<P>talis; chamairis; fritellaria.&nbsp; For March, there



<P>come violets, specially the single blue, which are



<P>the earliest; the yellow daffodil; the daisy; the



<P>almond-tree in blossom; the peach-tree in blos-



<P>som; the cornelian-tree in blossom; sweet-briar.



<P>In April follow the double white violet; the wall-



<P>flower; the stock-gilliflower; the cowslip; flower-



<P>delices, and lilies of all natures; rosemary-flowers;



<P>the tulippa; the double peony; the pale daffodil;



<P>the French honeysuckle; the cherry-tree in blos-



<P>som; the damson and plum-trees in blossom; the



<P>white thorn in leaf; the lilac-tree.&nbsp; In May and



<P>June come pinks of all sorts, specially the blush-



<P>pink; roses of all kinds, except the musk, which



<P>comes later; honeysuckles; strawberries; bugloss;



<P>columbine; the French marigold, flos Africanus;



<P>cherry-tree in fruit; ribes; figs in fruit; rasps; vine-



<P>flowers; lavender in flowers; the sweet satyrian,



<P>with the white flower; herba muscaria; lilium



<P>convallium; the apple-tree in blossom.&nbsp; In July



<P>come gilliflowers of all varieties; musk-roses; the



<P>lime-tree in blossom; early pears and plums in



<P>fruit; jennetings, codlins.&nbsp; In August come plums



<P>of all sorts in fruit; pears; apricocks; berberries;



<P>filberds; musk-melons; monks-hoods, of all colors.



<P>In September come grapes; apples; poppies of



<P>all colors; peaches; melocotones; nectarines; cor-



<P>nelians; wardens; quinces.&nbsp; In October and the



<P>beginning of November come services; medlars;



<P>bullaces; roses cut or removed to come late; holly-



<P>hocks; and such like.&nbsp; These particulars are for the



<P>climate of London; but my meaning is perceived,



<P>that you may have ver perpetuum, as the place



<P>affords.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And because the breath of flowers is far sweeter



<P>in the air (where it comes and goes like the warb-



<P>ling of music) than in the hand, therefore nothing



<P>is more fit for that delight, than to know what be



<P>the flowers and plants that do best perfume the air.



<P>Roses, damask and red, are fast flowers of their



<P>smells; so that you may walk by a whole row of



<P>them, and find nothing of their sweetness; yea



<P>though it be in a morning's dew.&nbsp; Bays likewise



<P>yield no smell as they grow.&nbsp; Rosemary little; nor



<P>sweet marjoram.&nbsp; That which above all others



<P>yields the sweetest smell in the air is the violet,



<P>specially the white double violet, which comes



<P>twice a year; about the middle of April, and about



<P>Bartholomew-tide.&nbsp; Next to that is the musk-rose.



<P>Then the strawberry-leaves dying, which yield a



<P>most excellent cordial smell.&nbsp; Then the flower of



<P>vines; it is a little dust, like the dust of a bent, which



<P>grows upon the cluster in the first coming forth.



<P>Then sweet-briar.&nbsp; Then wall-flowers, which are



<P>very delightful to be set under a parlor or lower



<P>chamber window.&nbsp; Then pinks and gilliflowers,



<P>especially the matted pink and clove gilliflower.



<P>Then the flowers of the lime-tree.&nbsp; Then the honey-



<P>suckles, so they be somewhat afar off.&nbsp; Of bean-



<P>flowers I speak not, because they are field flowers.



<P>But those which perfume the air most delightfully,



<P>not passed by as the rest, but being trodden upon



<P>and crushed, are three; that is, burnet, wild-



<P>thyme, and watermints.&nbsp; Therefore you are to set



<P>whole alleys of them, to have the pleasure when



<P>you walk or tread.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For gardens (speaking of those which are indeed



<P>princelike, as we have done of buildings), the con-



<P>tents ought not well to be under thirty acres of



<P>ground; and to be divided into three parts; a green



<P>in the entrance; a heath or desert in the going



<P>forth; and the main garden in the midst; besides



<P>alleys on both sides.&nbsp; And I like well that four acres



<P>of ground be assigned to the green; six to the



<P>heath; four and four to either side; and twelve to



<P>the main garden.&nbsp; The green hath two pleasures:



<P>the one, because nothing is more pleasant to the



<P>eye than green grass kept finely shorn; the other,



<P>because it will give you a fair alley in the midst, by



<P>which you may go in front upon a stately hedge,



<P>which is to enclose the garden.&nbsp; But because the



<P>alley will be long, and, in great heat of the year or



<P>day, you ought not to buy the shade in the garden,



<P>by going in the sun through the green, therefore



<P>you are, of either side the green, to plant a covert



<P>alley upon carpenter's work, about twelve foot in



<P>height, by which you may go in shade into the



<P>garden.&nbsp; As for the making of knots or figures, with



<P>divers colored earths,&nbsp; that they may lie under the



<P>windows of the house on that side which the gar-



<P>den stands, they be but toys; you may see as good



<P>sights, many times, in tarts.&nbsp; The garden is best to



<P>be square, encompassed on all the four sides with



<P>a stately arched hedge.&nbsp; The arches to be upon pil-



<P>lars of carpenter's work, of some ten foot high, and



<P>six foot broad; and the spaces between of the same



<P>dimension with the breadth of the arch.&nbsp; Over the



<P>arches let there be an entire hedge of some four



<P>foot high, framed also upon carpenter's work; and



<P>upon the upper hedge, over every arch, a little tur-



<P>ret, with a belly, enough to receive a cage of birds:



<P>and over every space between the arches some



<P>other little figure, with broad plates of round col-



<P>ored glass gilt, for the sun to play upon.&nbsp; But this



<P>hedge I intend to be raised upon a bank, not steep,



<P>but gently slope, of some six foot, set all with



<P>flowers.&nbsp; Also I understand, that this square of the



<P>garden, should not be the whole breadth of the



<P>ground, but to leave on either side, ground enough



<P>for diversity of side alleys; unto which the two



<P>covert alleys of the green, may deliver you.&nbsp; But



<P>there must be no alleys with hedges, at either end



<P>of this great enclosure; not at the hither end, for



<P>letting your prospect upon this fair hedge from



<P>the green; nor at the further end, for letting your



<P>prospect from the hedge, through the arches upon



<P>the heath.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the ordering of the ground, within the great



<P>hedge, I leave it to variety of device; advising



<P>nevertheless, that whatsoever form you cast it into,



<P>first, it be not too busy, or full of work.&nbsp; Wherein I,



<P>for my part, do not like images cut out in juniper



<P>or other garden stuff; they be for children.&nbsp; Little



<P>low hedges, round, like welts, with some pretty



<P>pyramids, I like well; and in some places, fair



<P>columns upon frames of carpenter's work.&nbsp; I would



<P>also have the alleys, spacious and fair.&nbsp; You may



<P>have closer alleys, upon the side grounds, but none



<P>in the main garden.&nbsp; I wish also, in the very middle,



<P>a fair mount, with three ascents, and alleys,



<P>enough for four to walk abreast; which I would



<P>have to be perfect circles, without any bulwarks



<P>or embossments; and the whole mount to be thirty



<P>foot high; and some fine banqueting-house, with



<P>some chimneys neatly cast, and without too much



<P>glass.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For fountains, they are a great beauty and re-



<P>freshment; but pools mar all, and make the garden



<P>unwholesome, and full of flies and frogs.&nbsp; Foun-



<P>tains I intend to be of two natures: the one that



<P>sprinkleth or spouteth water; the other a fair re-



<P>ceipt of water, of some thirty or forty foot square,



<P>but without fish, or slime, or mud.&nbsp; For the first,



<P>the ornaments of images gilt, or of marble, which



<P>are in use, do well: but the main matter is so to



<P>convey the water, as it never stay, either in the



<P>bowls or in the cistern; that the water be never by



<P>rest discolored, green or red or the like; or gather



<P>any mossiness or putrefaction.&nbsp; Besides that, it is to



<P>be cleansed every day by the hand.&nbsp; Also some



<P>steps up to it, and some fine pavement about it,



<P>doth well.&nbsp; As for the other kind of fountain, which



<P>we may call a bathing pool, it may admit much



<P>curiosity and beauty; wherewith we will not



<P>trouble ourselves: as, that the bottom be finely



<P>paved, and with images; the sides likewise; and



<P>withal embellished with colored glass, and such



<P>things of lustre; encompassed also with fine rails



<P>of low statuas.&nbsp; But the main point is the same



<P>which we mentioned in the former kind of foun-



<P>tain; which is, that the water be in perpetual



<P>motion, fed by a water higher than the pool, and



<P>delivered into it by fair spouts, and then dis-



<P>charged away under ground, by some equality of



<P>bores, that it stay little.&nbsp; And for fine devices, of



<P>arching water without spilling, and making it rise



<P>in several forms (of feathers, drinking glasses,



<P>canopies, and the like), they be pretty things to



<P>look on, but nothing to health and sweetness.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the heath, which was the third part of our



<P>plot, I wish it to be framed, as much as may be, to



<P>a natural wildness.&nbsp; Trees I would have none in it,



<P>but some thickets made only of sweet-briar and



<P>honeysuckle, and some wild vine amongst; and



<P>the ground set with violets, strawberries, and



<P>primroses.&nbsp; For these are sweet, and prosper in the



<P>shade.&nbsp; And these to be in the heath, here and there,



<P>not in any order.&nbsp; I like also little heaps, in the na-



<P>ture of mole-hills (such as are in wild heaths), to



<P>be set, some with wild thyme; some with pinks;



<P>some with germander, that gives a good flower to



<P>the eye; some with periwinkle; some with violets;



<P>some with strawberries; some&nbsp; with cowslips; some



<P>with daisies; some with red roses; some with lilium



<P>convallium; some with sweet-williams red; some



<P>with bear's-foot: and the like low flowers, being



<P>withal sweet and sightly.&nbsp; Part of which heaps, are



<P>to be with standards of little bushes pricked upon



<P>their top, and part without.&nbsp; The standards to be



<P>roses; juniper; holly; berberries (but here and



<P>there, because of the smell of their blossoms); red



<P>currants; gooseberries; rosemary; bays; sweet-



<P>briar; and such like.&nbsp; But these standards to be kept



<P>with cutting, that they grow not out of course.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the side grounds, you are to fill them with



<P>variety of alleys, private, to give a full shade, some



<P>of them, wheresoever the sun be.&nbsp; You are to frame



<P>some of them, likewise, for shelter, that when the



<P>wind blows sharp you may walk as in a gallery.



<P>And those alleys must be likewise hedged at both



<P>ends, to keep out the wind; and these closer alleys



<P>must be ever finely gravelled, and no grass, be-



<P>cause of going wet.&nbsp; In many of these alleys, like-



<P>wise, you are to set fruit-trees of all sorts; as well



<P>upon the walls, as in ranges.&nbsp; And this would be



<P>generally observed, that the borders wherein you



<P>plant your fruit-trees, be fair and large, and low,



<P>and not steep; and set with fine flowers, but thin



<P>and sparingly, lest they deceive the trees.&nbsp; At the



<P>end of both the side grounds, I would have a mount



<P>of some pretty height, leaving the wall of the en-



<P>closure breast high, to look abroad into the fields.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the main garden, I do not deny, but there



<P>should be some fair alleys ranged on both sides,



<P>with fruit-trees; and some pretty tufts of fruit-



<P>trees, and arbors with seats, set in some decent



<P>order; but these to be by no means set too thick; but



<P>to leave the main garden so as it be not close, but



<P>the air open and free.&nbsp; For as for shade, I would



<P>have you rest upon the alleys of the side grounds,



<P>there to walk, if you be disposed, in the heat of the



<P>year or day; but to make account, that the main



<P>garden is for the more temperate parts of the year;



<P>and in the heat of summer, for the morning and



<P>the evening, or overcast days.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For aviaries, I like them not, except they be of



<P>that largeness as they may be turfed, and have



<P>living plants and bushes set in them; that the birds



<P>may have more scope, and natural nesting, and



<P>that no foulness appear in the floor of the aviary.



<P>So I have made a platform of a princely garden,



<P>partly by precept, partly by drawing, not a model,



<P>but some general lines of it; and in this I have



<P>spared for no cost.&nbsp; But it is nothing for great



<P>princes, that for the most part taking advice with



<P>workmen, with no less cost set their things to-



<P>gether; and sometimes add statuas and such things



<P>for state and magnificence, but nothing to the true



<P>pleasure of a garden.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Superstition</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Superstition</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>IT WERE better to have no opinion of God at all,



<P>than such an opinion, as is unworthy of him.



<P>For the one is unbelief, the other is contumely;



<P>and certainly superstition is the reproach of the



<P>Deity.&nbsp; Plutarch saith well to that purpose: Surely



<P>(saith he) I had rather a great deal, men should



<P>say, there was no such man at all, as Plutarch,



<P>than that they should say, that there was one Plu-



<P>tarch, that would eat his children as soon as they



<P>were born; as the poets speak of Saturn.&nbsp; And as the



<P>contumely is greater towards God, so the danger is



<P>greater towards men.&nbsp; Atheism leaves a man to



<P>sense, to philosophy, to natural piety, to laws, to



<P>reputation; all which may be guides to an outward



<P>moral virtue, though religion were not; but super-



<P>stition dismounts all these, and erecteth an abso-



<P>lute monarchy, in the minds of men.&nbsp; Therefore



<P>theism did never perturb states; for it makes men



<P>wary of themselves, as looking no further: and we



<P>see the times inclined to atheism (as the time of



<P>Augustus Caesar) were civil times.&nbsp; But supersti-



<P>tion hath been the confusion of many states, and



<P>bringeth in a new primum mobile, that ravisheth



<P>all the spheres of government.The master of super-



<P>stition, is the people; and in all superstition, wise



<P>men follow fools; and arguments are fitted to prac-



<P>tice, in a reversed order.&nbsp; It was gravely said by



<P>some of the prelates in the Council of Trent, where



<P>the doctrine of the Schoolmen bare great sway,



<P>that the Schoolmen were like astronomers, which



<P>did feign eccentrics and epicycles, and such en-



<P>gines of orbs, to save the phenomena; though they



<P>knew there were no such things; and in like man-



<P>ner, that the Schoolmen had framed a number of



<P>subtle and intricate axioms, and theorems, to save



<P>the practice of the church.&nbsp; The causes of supersti-



<P>tion are: pleasing and sensual rites and ceremonies;



<P>excess of outward and pharisaical holiness; over-



<P>great reverence of traditions, which cannot but



<P>load the church; the stratagems of prelates, for



<P>their own ambition and lucre; the favoring too



<P>much of good intentions, which openeth the gate



<P>to conceits and novelties; the taking an aim at



<P>divine matters, by human, which cannot but



<P>breed mixture of imaginations: and, lastly, bar-



<P>barous times, especially joined with calamities



<P>and disasters.&nbsp; Superstition, without a veil, is a de-



<P>formed thing; for, as it addeth deformity to an



<P>ape, to be so like a man, so the similitude of super-



<P>stition to religion, makes it the more deformed.



<P>And as wholesome meat corrupteth to little worms,



<P>so good forms and orders corrupt, into a number of



<P>petty observances.&nbsp; There is a superstition in avoid-



<P>ing superstition, when men think to do best, if they



<P>go furthest from the superstition, formerly re-



<P>ceived; therefore care would be had that (as it



<P>fareth in ill purgings) the good be not taken away



<P>with the bad; which commonly is done, when the



<P>people is the reformer.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Deformity</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Deformity</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>DEFORMED persons are commonly even with



<P>nature; for as nature hath done ill by them,



<P>so do they by nature; being for the most part (as



<P>the Scripture saith) void of natural affection; and



<P>so they have their revenge of nature.&nbsp; Certainly



<P>there is a consent, between the body and the mind;



<P>and where nature erreth in the one, she ventureth



<P>in the other.&nbsp; Ubi peccat in uno, periclitatur in al-



<P>tero.&nbsp; But because there is, in man, an election



<P>touching the frame of his mind, and a necessity in



<P>the frame of his body, the stars of natural inclina-



<P>tion are sometimes obscured, by the sun of disci-



<P>pline and virtue.&nbsp; Therefore it is good to consider of



<P>deformity, not as a sign, which is more deceivable;



<P>but as a cause, which seldom faileth of the effect.



<P>Whosoever hath anything fixed in his person, that



<P>doth induce contempt, hath also a perpetual spur



<P>in himself, to rescue and deliver himself from



<P>scorn.&nbsp; Therefore all deformed persons, are extreme



<P>bold.&nbsp; First, as in their own defence, as being ex-



<P>posed to scorn; but in process of time, by a general



<P>habit.&nbsp; Also it stirreth in them industry, and espe-



<P>cially of this kind, to watch and observe the weak-



<P>ness of others, that they may have somewhat to



<P>repay.&nbsp; Again, in their superiors, it quencheth



<P>jealousy towards them, as persons that they think



<P>they may, at pleasure, despise: and it layeth their



<P>competitors and emulators asleep; as never believ-



<P>ing they should be in possibility of advancement,



<P>till they see them in possession.&nbsp; So that upon the



<P>matter, in a great wit, deformity is an advantage



<P>to rising.&nbsp; Kings in ancient times (and at this pres-



<P>ent in some countries) were wont to put great trust



<P>in eunuchs; because they that are envious towards



<P>all are more obnoxious and officious, towards one.



<P>But yet their trust towards them, hath rather



<P>been as to good spials, and good wbisperers, than



<P>good magistrates and officers.&nbsp; And much like is



<P>the reason of deformed persons.&nbsp; Still the ground



<P>is, they will, if they be of spirit, seek to free them-



<P>selves from scorn; which must be either by virtue



<P>or malice; and therefore let it not be marvelled, if



<P>sometimes they prove excellent persons; as was



<P>Agesilaus, Zanger the son of Solyman, AEsop,



<P>Gasca, President of Peru; and Socrates may go



<P>likewise amongst them; with others.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Masques AND TRIUMPHS</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Masques AND TRIUMPHS</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THESE things are but toys, to come amongst



<P>such serious observations.&nbsp; But yet, since



<P>princes will have such things, it is better they



<P>should be graced with elegancy, than daubed with



<P>cost.&nbsp; Dancing to song, is a thing of great state and



<P>pleasure.&nbsp; I understand it, that the song be in quire,



<P>placed aloft, and accompanied with some broken



<P>music; and the ditty fitted to the device.&nbsp; Acting in



<P>song, especially in dialogues, hath an extreme



<P>good grace; I say acting, not dancing (for that is a



<P>mean and vulgar thing); and the voices of the dia-



<P>logue would be strong and manly (a base and a



<P>tenor; no treble); and the ditty high and tragical;



<P>not nice or dainty.&nbsp; Several quires, placed one over



<P>against another, and taking the voice by catches,



<P>anthem-wise, give great pleasure.&nbsp; Turning dances



<P>into figure, is a childish curiosity.&nbsp; And generally



<P>let it be noted, that those things which I here set



<P>down, are such as do naturally take the sense, and



<P>not respect petty wonderments.&nbsp; It is true, the al-



<P>terations of scenes, so it be quietly and without



<P>noise, are things of great beauty and pleasure; for



<P>they feed and relieve the eye, before it be full of



<P>the same object.&nbsp; Let the scenes abound with light,



<P>specially colored and varied; and let the masquers,



<P>or any other, that are to come down from the



<P>scene, have some motions upon the scene itself,



<P>before their coming down; for it draws the eye



<P>strangely, and makes it, with great pleasure, to



<P>desire to see, that it cannot perfectly discern.&nbsp; Let



<P>the songs be loud and cheerful, and not chirpings



<P>or pulings.&nbsp; Let the music likewise be sharp and



<P>loud, and well placed.&nbsp; The colors that show best by



<P>candle-light are white, carnation, and a kind of



<P>sea-water-green; and oes, or spangs, as they are of



<P>no great cost, so they are of most glory.&nbsp; As for rich



<P>embroidery, it is lost and not discerned.&nbsp; Let the



<P>suits of the masquers be graceful, and such as be-



<P>come the person, when the vizors are off; not after



<P>examples of known attires; Turke, soldiers, mari-



<P>ners', and the like.&nbsp; Let anti-masques not be long;



<P>they have been commonly of fools, satyrs, baboons,



<P>wild-men, antics, beasts, sprites, witches, Ethiops,



<P>pigmies, turquets, nymphs, rustics, Cupids, statuas



<P>moving, and the like.&nbsp; As for angels, it is not comi-



<P>cal enough, to put them in anti-masques; and



<P>anything that is hideous, as devils, giants, is on



<P>the other side as unfit.&nbsp; But chiefly, let the music



<P>of them be recreative, and with some strange



<P>changes.&nbsp; Some sweet odors suddenly coming forth,



<P>without any drops falling, are, in such a company



<P>as there is steam and heat, things of great pleasure



<P>and refreshment.&nbsp; Double masques, one of men,



<P>another of ladies, addeth state and variety.&nbsp; But all



<P>is nothing except the room be kept clear and neat.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For justs, and tourneys, and barriers; the glories



<P>of them are chiefly in the chariots, wherein the



<P>challengers make their entry; especially if they



<P>be drawn with strange beasts: as lions, bears,



<P>camels, and the like; or in the devices of their en-



<P>trance; or in the bravery of their liveries; or in the



<P>goodly furniture of their horses and armor.&nbsp; But



<P>enough of these toys.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Suspicion</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Suspicion</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>SUSPICIONS amongst thoughts, are like bats



<P>amongst birds, they ever fly by twilight.&nbsp; Cer-



<P>tainly they are to be repressed, or at least well



<P>guarded: for they cloud the mind; they leese



<P>friends; and they check with business, whereby



<P>business cannot go on currently and constantly.



<P>They dispose kings to tyranny, husbands to jeal-



<P>ousy, wise men to irresolution and melancholy.



<P>They are defects, not in the heart, but in the brain;



<P>for they take place in the stoutest natures; as in the



<P>example of Henry the Seventh of England.&nbsp; There



<P>was not a more suspicious man, nor a more stout.



<P>And in such a composition they do small hurt.&nbsp; For



<P>commonly they are not admitted, but with exami-



<P>nation, whether they be likely or no.&nbsp; But in fearful



<P>natures they gain ground too fast.&nbsp; There is nothing



<P>makes a man suspect much, more than to know



<P>little; and therefore men should remedy suspicion,



<P>by procuring to know more, and not to keep their



<P>suspicions in smother.&nbsp; What would men have? Do



<P>they think, those they&nbsp; employ and deal with, are



<P>saints? Do they not think, they will have their own



<P>ends, and be truer to themselves, than to them?



<P>Therefore there is no better way, to moderate sus-



<P>picions, than to account upon such suspicions as



<P>true, and yet to bridle them as false.&nbsp; For so far a



<P>man ought to make use of suspicions, as to provide,



<P>as if that should be true, that he suspects, yet it



<P>may do him no hurt.&nbsp; Suspicions that the mind of



<P>itself gathers, are but buzzes; but suspicions that



<P>are artificially nourished, and put into men's



<P>heads, by the tales and whisperings of others, have



<P>stings.&nbsp; Certainly, the best mean, to clear the way



<P>in this same wood of suspicions, is frankly to com-



<P>municate them with the party, that he suspects;



<P>for thereby he shall be sure to know more of the



<P>truth of them, than he did before; and withal shall



<P>make that party more circumspect, not to give



<P>further cause of suspicion.&nbsp; But this would not be



<P>done to men of base natures; for they, if they find



<P>themselves once suspected, will never be true.&nbsp; The



<P>Italian says, Sospetto licentia fede; as if suspicion,



<P>did give a passport to faith; but it ought, rather, to



<P>kindle it to discharge itself.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Fame</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Fame</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE poets make Fame a monster.&nbsp; They de-



<P>scribe her in part finely and elegantly, and



<P>in part gravely and sententiously.&nbsp; They say, look



<P>how many feathers she hath, so many eyes she



<P>hath underneath; so many tongues; so many



<P>voices; she pricks up so many ears.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This is a flourish.&nbsp; There follow excellent par-



<P>ables; as that, she gathereth strength in going;



<P>that she goeth upon the ground, and yet hideth her



<P>head in the clouds; that in the daytime she sitteth



<P>in a watch tower, and flieth most by night; that



<P>she mingleth things done, with things not done;



<P>and that she is a terror to great cities.&nbsp; But that



<P>which passeth all the rest is: They do recount that



<P>the Earth, mother of the giants that made war



<P>against Jupiter, and were by him destroyed, there-



<P>upon in an anger brought forth Fame.&nbsp; For certain



<P>it is, that rebels, figured by the giants, and seditious



<P>fames and libels, are but brothers and sisters, mas-



<P>culine and feminine.&nbsp; But now, if a man can tame



<P>this monster, and bring her to feed at the hand,



<P>and govern her, and with her fly other ravening



<P>fowl and kill them, it is somewhat worth.&nbsp; But we



<P>are infected with the style of the poets.&nbsp; To speak



<P>now in a sad and serious manner: There is not, in



<P>all the politics, a place less handled and more



<P>worthy to be handled, than this of fame.&nbsp; We will



<P>therefore speak of these points: What are false



<P>fames; and what are true fames; and how they



<P>may be best discerned; how fames may be sown,



<P>and raised; how they may be spread, and multi-



<P>plied; and how they may be checked, and laid



<P>dead.&nbsp; And other things concerning the nature of



<P>fame.&nbsp; Fame is of that force, as there is scarcely any



<P>great action, wherein it hath not a great part; es-



<P>pecially in the war.&nbsp; Mucianus undid Vitellius, by



<P>a fame that he scattered, that Vitellius had in pur-



<P>pose to remove the legions of Syria into Germany,



<P>and the legions of Germany into Syria; where-



<P>upon the legions of Syria were infinitely inflamed.



<P>Julius Caesar took Pompey unprovided, and laid



<P>asleep his industry and preparations, by a fame



<P>that he cunningly gave out: Caesar's own soldiers



<P>loved him not, and being wearied with the wars,



<P>and laden with the spoils of Gaul, would forsake



<P>him, as soon as he came into Italy.&nbsp; Livia settled



<P>all things for the succession of her son Tiberius, by



<P>continual giving out, that her husband Augustus



<P>was upon recovery and amendment, and it is an



<P>usual thing with the pashas, to conceal the death



<P>of the Great Turk from the janizaries and men of



<P>war, to save the sacking of Constantinople and



<P>other towns, as their manner is.&nbsp; Themistocles made



<P>Xerxes, king of Persia, post apace out of Grecia, by



<P>giving out, that the Grecians had a purpose to



<P>break his bridge of ships, which he had made ath-



<P>wart Hellespont.&nbsp; There be a thousand such like



<P>examples; and the more they are, the less they



<P>need to be repeated; because a man meeteth with



<P>them everywhere.&nbsp; Therefore let all wise governors



<P>have as great a watch and care over fames, as they



<P>have of the actions and designs themselves.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>[This essay was not finished]

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Great Place</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Great Place</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MEN in great place are thrice servants: ser-



<P>vants of the sovereign or state; servants of



<P>fame; and servants of business.&nbsp; So as they have no



<P>freedom; neither in their persons, nor in their ac-



<P>tions, nor in their times.&nbsp; It is a strange desire, to



<P>seek power and to lose liberty: or to seek power



<P>over others, and to lose power over a man's self.



<P>The rising unto place is laborious; and by pains,



<P>men come to greater pains; and it is sometimes



<P>base; and by indignities, men come to dignities.



<P>The standing is slippery, and the regress is either



<P>a downfall, or at least an eclipse, which is a melan-



<P>choly thing.&nbsp; Cum non sis qui fueris, non esse cur



<P>velis vivere.&nbsp; Nay, retire men cannot when they



<P>would, neither will they, when it were reason; but



<P>are impatient of privateness, even in age and sick-



<P>ness, which require the shadow; like old towns-



<P>men, that will be still sitting at their street door,



<P>though thereby they offer age to scom.&nbsp; Certainly



<P>great persons had need to borrow other men's



<P>opinions, to think themselves happy; for if they



<P>judge by their own feeling, they cannot find it; but



<P>if they think with themselves, what other men



<P>think of them, and that other men would fain be,



<P>as they are, then they are happy, as it were, by



<P>report; when perhaps they find the contrary



<P>within.&nbsp; For they are the first, that find their own



<P>griefs, though they be the last, that find their



<P>own faults.&nbsp; Certainly men in great fortunes are



<P>strangers to themselves, and while they are in the



<P>puzzle of business, they have no time to tend their



<P>health, either of body or mind.&nbsp; Illi mors gravis



<P>incubat, qui notus nimis omnibus, ignotus moritur



<P>sibi.&nbsp; In place, there is license to do good, and evil;



<P>whereof the latter is a curse: for in evil, the best



<P>condition is not to win; the second, not to can.&nbsp; But



<P>power to do good, is the true and lawful end of



<P>aspiring.&nbsp; For good thoughts (though God accept



<P>them) yet, towards men, are little better than good



<P>dreams, except they be put in act; and that cannot



<P>be, without power and place, as the vantage, and



<P>commanding ground.&nbsp; Merit and good works, is



<P>the end of man's motion; and conscience of the



<P>same is the accomplishment of man's rest.&nbsp; For if a



<P>man can be partaker of God's theatre, he shall like-



<P>wise be partaker of God's rest.&nbsp; Et conversus Deus,



<P>ut aspiceret opera quae fecerunt manus suae, vidit



<P>quod omnia essent bona nimis; and then the sab-



<P>bath.&nbsp; In the discharge of thy place, set before thee



<P>the best examples; for imitation is a globe of pre-



<P>cepts.&nbsp; And after a time, set before thee thine own



<P>example; and examine thyself strictly, whether



<P>thou didst not best at first.&nbsp; Neglect not also the



<P>examples, of those that have carried themselves



<P>ill, in the same place; not to set off thyself, by tax-



<P>ing their memory, but to direct thyself, what to



<P>avoid.&nbsp; Reform therefore, without bravery, or scan-



<P>dal of former times and persons; but yet set it down



<P>to thyself, as well to create good precedents, as to



<P>follow them.&nbsp; Reduce things to the first institution,



<P>and observe wherein, and how, they have degen-



<P>erate; but yet ask counsel of both times; of the



<P>ancient time, what is best; and of the latter time,



<P>what is fittest.&nbsp; Seek to make thy course regular,



<P>that men may know beforehand, what they may



<P>expect; but be not too positive and peremptory;



<P>and express thyself well, when thou digressest



<P>from thy rule.&nbsp; Preserve the right of thy place; but



<P>stir not questions of jurisdiction; and rather as-



<P>sume thy right, in silence and de facto, than voice



<P>it with claims, and challenges.&nbsp; Preserve likewise



<P>the rights of inferior places; and think it more



<P>honor, to direct in chief, than to be busy in all.



<P>Embrace and invite helps, and advices, touching



<P>the execution of thy place; and do not drive away



<P>such, as bring thee information, as meddlers; but



<P>accept of them in good part.&nbsp; The vices of authority



<P>are chiefly four: delays, corruption, roughness,



<P>and facility.&nbsp; For delays: give easy access; keep



<P>times appointed; go through with that which is in



<P>hand, and interlace not business, but of necessity.



<P>For corruption: do not only bind thine own hands,



<P>or thy servants' hands, from taking, but bind the



<P>hands of suitors also, from offering.&nbsp; For integrity



<P>used doth the one; but integrity professed, and



<P>with a manifest detestation of bribery, doth the



<P>other.&nbsp; And avoid not only the fault, but the sus-



<P>picion.&nbsp; Whosoever is found variable, and changeth



<P>manifestly without manifest cause, giveth sus-



<P>picion of corruption.&nbsp; Therefore always, when thou



<P>changest thine opinion or course, profess it plainly,



<P>and declare it, together with the reasons that move



<P>thee to change; and do not think to steal it.&nbsp; A



<P>servant or a favorite, if he be inward, and no



<P>other apparent cause of esteem, is commonly



<P>thought, but a by-way to close corruption.&nbsp; For



<P>roughness: it is a needless cause of discontent:



<P>severity breedeth fear, but roughness breedeth



<P>hate.&nbsp; Even reproofs from authority, ought to be



<P>grave, and not taunting.&nbsp; As for facility: it is worse



<P>than bribery.&nbsp; For bribes come but now and then;



<P>but if importunity, or idle respects, lead a man, he



<P>shall never be without.&nbsp; As Solomon saith, To re-



<P>spect persons is not good; for such a man will



<P>transgress for a piece of bread.&nbsp; It is most true, that



<P>was anciently spoken, A place showeth the man.



<P>And it showeth some to the better, and some to the



<P>worse.&nbsp; Omnium consensu capax imperii, nisi im-



<P>perasset, saith Tacitus of Galba; but of Vespasian



<P>he saith, Solus imperantium, Vespasianus mutatus



<P>in melius; though the one was meant of sufficiency,



<P>the other of manners, and affection.&nbsp; It is an assured



<P>sign of a worthy and generous spirit, whom honor



<P>amends.&nbsp; For honor is, or should be, the place of



<P>virtue; and as in nature, things move violently to



<P>their place, and calmly in their place, so virtue in



<P>ambition is violent, in authority settled and calm.



<P>All rising to great place is by a winding star; and



<P>if there be factions, it is good to side a man's self,



<P>whilst he is in the rising, and to balance himself



<P>when he is placed.&nbsp; Use the memory of thy prede-



<P>cessor, fairly and tenderly; for if thou dost not, it is



<P>a debt will sure be paid when thou art gone.&nbsp; If



<P>thou have colleagues, respect them, and rather call



<P>them, when they look not for it, than exclude



<P>them , when they have reason to look to be called.



<P>Be not too sensible, or too remembering, of thy



<P>place in conversation, and private answers to



<P>suitors; but let it rather be said, When he sits in



<P>place, he is another man.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>0f Followers AND FRIENDS</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>0f Followers AND FRIENDS</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>COSTLY followers are not to be liked; lest



<P>while a man maketh his train longer, he



<P>make his wings shorter.&nbsp; I reckon to be costly, not



<P>them alone which charge the purse, but which are



<P>wearisome, and importune in suits.&nbsp; Ordinary fol-



<P>lowers ought to challenge no higher conditions,



<P>than countenance, recommendation, and protec-



<P>tion from wrongs.&nbsp; Factious followers are worse to



<P>be liked, which follow not upon affection to him,



<P>with whom they range themselves, but upon



<P>discontentment conceived against some other;



<P>whereupon commonly ensueth that ill intelli-



<P>gence, that we many times see between great per-



<P>sonages.&nbsp; Likewise glorious followers, who make



<P>themselves as trumpets of the commendation of



<P>those they follow, are full of inconvenience; for



<P>they taint business through want of secrecy; and



<P>they export honor from a man, and make him a



<P>return in envy.&nbsp; There is a kind of followers like-



<P>wise, which are dangerous, being indeed espials;



<P>which inquire the secrets of the house, and bear



<P>tales of them, to others.&nbsp; Yet such men, many times,



<P>are in great favor; for they are officious, and com-



<P>monly exchange tales.&nbsp; The following by certain



<P>estates of men, answerable to that, which a great



<P>person himself professeth (as of soldiers, to him



<P>that hath been employed in the wars, and the like),



<P>hath ever been a thing civil, and well taken, even



<P>in monarchies; so it be without too much pomp



<P>or popularity.&nbsp; But the most honorable kind of fol-



<P>lowing, is to be followed as one, that apprehendeth



<P>to advance virtue, and desert, in all sorts of per-



<P>sons.&nbsp; And yet, where there is no eminent odds in



<P>sufficiency, it is better to take with the more pass-



<P>able, than with the more able.&nbsp; And besides, to



<P>speak truth, in base times, active men are of more



<P>use than virtuous.&nbsp; It is true that in government, it



<P>is good to use men of one rank equally: for to coun-



<P>tenance some extraordinarily, is to make them



<P>insolent, and the rest discontent; because they



<P>may claim a due.&nbsp; But contrariwise, in favor, to



<P>use men with much difference and election is



<P>good; for it maketh the persons preferred more



<P>thankful, and the rest more officious: because all is



<P>of favor.&nbsp; It is good discretion, not to make too much



<P>of any man at the first; because one cannot hold



<P>out that proportion.&nbsp; To be governed (as we call it)



<P>by one is not safe; for it shows softness, and gives



<P>a freedom, to scandal and disreputation; for those,



<P>that would not censure or speak ill of a man imme-



<P>diately, will talk more boldly of those that are so



<P>great with them, and thereby wound their honor.



<P>Yet to be distracted with many is worse; for it



<P>makes men to be of the last impression, and full of



<P>change.&nbsp; To take advice of some few friends, is ever



<P>honorable; for lookers-on many times see more



<P>than gamesters; and the vale best discovereth the



<P>hill.&nbsp; There is little friendship in the world, and least



<P>of all between equals, which was wont to be mag-
