<P>nified.&nbsp; That that is, is between superior and in-



<P>ferior, whose fortunes may comprehend the one



<P>the other.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Praise</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Praise</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>PRAISE is the reflection of virtue; but it is as



<P>the glass or body, which giveth the reflec-



<P>tion.&nbsp; If it be from the common people, it is com-



<P>monly false and naught; and rather followeth vain



<P>persons, than virtuous.&nbsp; For the common people



<P>understand not many excellent virtues.&nbsp; The lowest



<P>virtues draw praise from them; the middle virtues



<P>work in them astonishment or admiration; but of



<P>the highest virtues, they have no sense of perceiv-



<P>ing at all.&nbsp; But shows, and species virtutibus similes,



<P>serve best with them.&nbsp; Certainly fame is like a river,



<P>that beareth up things light and swoln, and drowns



<P>things weighty and solid.&nbsp; But if persons of quality



<P>and judgment concur, then it is (as the Scripture



<P>saith) nomen bonum instar unguenti fragrantis.



<P>It fireth all round about, and will not easily away.



<P>For the odors of ointments are more durable, than



<P>those of flowers.&nbsp; There be so many false points of



<P>praise, that a man may justly hold it a suspect.



<P>Some praises proceed merely of flattery; and if he



<P>be an ordinary flatterer, he will have certain com-



<P>mon attributes, which may serve every man; if he



<P>be a cunning flatterer, he will follow the arch-



<P>flatterer, which is a man's self; and wherein a man



<P>thinketh best of himself, therein the flatterer will



<P>uphold him most: but if he be an impudent flat-



<P>terer, look wherein a man is conscious to himself,



<P>that he is most defective, and is most out of counte-



<P>nance in himself, that will the flatterer entitle him



<P>to perforce, spreta conscientia.&nbsp; Some praises come



<P>of good wishes and respects, which is a form due, in



<P>civility, to kings and great persons, laudando



<P>praecipere, when by telling men what they are,



<P>they represent to them, what they should be.&nbsp; Some



<P>men are praised maliciously, to their hurt, thereby



<P>to stir envy and jealousy towards them: pessimum



<P>genus inimicorum laudantium; insomuch as it



<P>was a proverb, amongst the Grecians, that he that



<P>was praised to his hurt, should have a push rise



<P>upon his nose; as we say, that a blister will rise



<P>upon one's tongue, that tells a lie.&nbsp; Certainly mod-



<P>erate praise, used with opportunity, and not vul-



<P>gar, is that which doth the good.&nbsp; Solomon saith,



<P>He that praiseth his friend aloud, rising early, it



<P>shall be to him no better than a curse.&nbsp; Too much



<P>magnifying of man or matter, doth irritate con-



<P>tradiction, and procure envy and scorn.&nbsp; To praise



<P>a man's self, cannot be decent, except it be in rare



<P>cases; but to praise a man's office or profession, he



<P>may do it with good grace, and with a kind of mag-



<P>nanimity.&nbsp; The cardinals of Rome, which are theo-



<P>logues, and friars, and Schoolmen, have a phrase



<P>of notable contempt and scorn towards civil busi-



<P>ness: for they call all temporal business of wars,



<P>embassages, judicature, and other employments,



<P>sbirrerie, which is under-sheriffries; as if they



<P>were but matters, for under-sheriffs and catch-



<P>poles: though many times those under-sheriffries



<P>do more good, than their high speculations.&nbsp; St.



<P>Paul, when he boasts of himself, he doth oft inter-



<P>lace, I speak like a fool; but speaking of his calling,



<P>he saith, magnificabo apostolatum meum.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Expense</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Expense</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>RICHES are for spending, and spending for



<P>honor and good actions.&nbsp; Therefore extra-



<P>ordinary expense must be limited by the worth of



<P>the occasion; for voluntary undoing, may be as



<P>well for a man's country, as for the kingdom of



<P>heaven.&nbsp; But ordinary expense, ought to be limited



<P>by a man's estate; and governed with such regard,



<P>as it be within his compass; and not subject to de-



<P>ceit and abuse of servants; and ordered to the best



<P>show, that the bills may be less than the estima-



<P>tion abroad.&nbsp; Certainly, if a man will keep but of



<P>even hand, his ordinary expenses ought to be but



<P>to the half of his receipts; and if he think to wax



<P>rich, but to the third part.&nbsp; It is no baseness, for the



<P>greatest to descend and look into their own estate.



<P>Some forbear it, not upon negligence alone, but



<P>doubting to bring themselves into melancholy, in



<P>respect they shall find it broken.&nbsp; But wounds can-



<P>not be cured without searching.&nbsp; He that cannot



<P>look into his own estate at all, had need both choose



<P>well those whom he employeth, and change them



<P>often; for new are more timorous and less subtle.



<P>He that can look into his estate but seldom, it be-



<P>hooveth him to turn all to certainties.&nbsp; A man had



<P>need, if he be plentiful in some kind of expense, to



<P>be as saving again in some other.&nbsp; As if he be plenti-



<P>ful in diet, to be saving in apparel; if he be plenti-



<P>ful in the hall, to be saving in the stable; and the



<P>like.&nbsp; For he that is plentiful in expenses of all kinds,



<P>will hardly be preserved from decay.&nbsp; In clearing



<P>of a man's estate, he may as well hurt himself in



<P>being too sudden, as in letting it run on too long.



<P>For hasty selling, is commonly as disadvantage-



<P>able as interest.&nbsp; Besides, he that clears at once will



<P>relapse; for finding himself out of straits, he will



<P>revert to his custom: but he that cleareth by de-



<P>grees, induceth a habit of frugality, and gaineth



<P>as well upon his mind, as upon his estate.&nbsp; Cer-



<P>tainly, who hath a state to repair, may not despise



<P>small things; and commonly it is less dishonor-



<P>able, to abridge petty charges, than to stoop to



<P>petty gettings.&nbsp; A man ought warily to begin



<P>charges which once begun will continue; but in



<P>matters that return not, he may be more magnificent.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Cunning</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Cunning</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>WE TAKE cunning for a sinister or crooked



<P>wisdom.&nbsp; And certainly there is a great dif-



<P>ference, between a cunning man, and a wise man;



<P>not only in point of honesty, but in point of ability.



<P>There be, that can pack the cards, and yet cannot



<P>play well; so there are some that are good in can-



<P>vasses and factions, that are otherwise weak men.



<P>Again, it is one thing to understand persons, and



<P>another thing to understand matters; for many



<P>are perfect in men's humors, that are not greatly



<P>capable of the real part of business; which is the



<P>constitution of one that hath studied men, more



<P>than books.&nbsp; Such men are fitter for practice, than



<P>for counsel; and they are good, but in their own



<P>alley: turn them to new men, and they have lost



<P>their aim; so as the old rule, to know a fool from a



<P>wise man, Mitte ambos nudos ad ignotos, et vide-



<P>bis, doth scarce hold for them.&nbsp; And because these



<P>cunning men, are like haberdashers of small



<P>wares, it is not amiss to set forth their shop.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is a point of cunning, to wait upon him with



<P>whom you speak, with your eye; as the Jesuits give



<P>it in precept: for there be many wise men, that



<P>have secret hearts, and transparent countenances.



<P>Yet this would be done with a demure abasing of



<P>your eye, sometimes, as the Jesuits also do use.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Another is, that when you have anything to



<P>obtain, of present despatch, you entertain and



<P>amuse the party, with whom you deal, with some



<P>other discourse; that he be not too much awake to



<P>make objections.&nbsp; I knew a counsellor and secre-



<P>tary, that never came to Queen Elizabeth of Eng-



<P>land, with bills to sign, but he would always first



<P>put her into some discourse of estate, that she



<P>mought the less mind the bills.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The like surprise may be made by moving



<P>things, when the party is in haste, and cannot stay



<P>to consider advisedly of that is moved.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>If a man would cross a business, that he doubts



<P>some other would handsomely and effectually



<P>move, let him pretend to wish it well, and move it



<P>himself in such sort as may foil it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The breaking off, in the midst of that one was



<P>about to say, as if he took himself up, breeds a



<P>greater appetite in him with whom you confer, to



<P>know more.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And because it works better, when anything



<P>seemeth to be gotten from you by question, than



<P>if you offer it of yourself, you may lay a bait for a



<P>question, by showing another visage, and counte-



<P>nance, than you are wont; to the end to give occa-



<P>sion, for the party to ask, what the matter is of the



<P>change? As Nehemias did; And I had not before



<P>that time, been sad before the king.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In things that are tender and unpleasing, it is



<P>good to break the ice, by some whose words are of



<P>less weight, and to reserve the more weighty voice,



<P>to come in as by chance, so that he may be asked



<P>the question upon the other's speech: as Narcissus



<P>did, relating to Claudius the marriage of Messa-



<P>lina and Silius.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>In things that a man would not be seen in him-



<P>self, it is a point of cunning, to borrow the name of



<P>the world; as to say, The world says, or There is a



<P>speech abroad.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I knew one that, when he wrote a letter, he



<P>would put that, which was most material, in the



<P>postscript, as if it had been a by-matter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I knew another that, when he came to have



<P>speech, he would pass over that, that he intended



<P>most; and go forth, and come back again, and



<P>speak of it as of a thing, that he had almost forgot.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some procure themselves, to be surprised, at



<P>such times as it is like the party that they work



<P>upon, will suddenly come upon them; and to be



<P>found with a letter in their hand, or doing some-



<P>what which they are not accustomed; to the end,



<P>they may be apposed of those things, which of



<P>themselves they are desirous to utter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is a point of cunning, to let fall those words in



<P>a man's own name, which he would have another



<P>man learn, and use, and thereupon take advan-



<P>tage.&nbsp; I knew two, that were competitors for the



<P>secretary's place in Queen Elizabeth's time, and



<P>yet kept good quarter between themselves; and



<P>would confer, one with another, upon the busi-



<P>ness; and the one of them said, That to be a secre-



<P>tary, in the declination of a monarchy, was a



<P>ticklish thing, and that he did not affect it: the



<P>other straight caught up those words, and dis-



<P>coursed with divers of his friends, that he had no



<P>reason to desire to be secretary, in the declination



<P>of a monarchy.&nbsp; The first man took hold of it, and



<P>found means it was told the Queen; who, hearing



<P>of a declination of a monarchy, took it so ill, as she



<P>would never after hear of the other's suit.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There is a cunning, which we in England call,



<P>the turning of the cat in the pan; which is, when



<P>that which a man says to another, he lays it as if



<P>another had said it to him.&nbsp; And to say truth, it is



<P>not easy, when such a matter passed between two,



<P>to make it appear from which of them it first



<P>moved and began.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is a way that some men have, to glance and



<P>dart at others, by justifying themselves by nega-



<P>tives; as to say, This I do not; as Tigellinus did



<P>towards Burrhus, Se non&nbsp; diversas&nbsp; spes,&nbsp; sed&nbsp; incolu-



<P>mitatem imperatoris simpliciter spectare.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Some have in readiness so many tales and



<P>stories, as there is nothing they would insinuate,



<P>but they can wrap it into a tale; which serveth both



<P>to keep themselves more in guard, and to make



<P>others carry it with more pleasure.&nbsp; It is a good



<P>point of cunning, for a man to shape the answer



<P>he would have, in his own words and propositions;



<P>for it makes the other party stick the less.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is strange how long some men will lie in wait



<P>to speak somewhat they desire to say; and how far



<P>about they will fetch; and how many other mat-



<P>ters they will beat over, to come near it.&nbsp; It is a thing



<P>of great patience, but yet of much use.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A sudden, bold, and unexpected question doth



<P>many times surprise a man, and lay him open.



<P>Like to him that , having changed his name, and



<P>walking in Paul's, another suddenly came behind



<P>him, and called him by his true name, whereat



<P>straightways he looked back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But these small wares, and petty points, of cun-



<P>ning, are infinite; and it were a good deed to make



<P>a list of them; for that nothing doth more hurt in



<P>a state, than that cunning men pass for wise.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But certainly some there are that know the re-



<P>sorts and falls of business, that cannot sink into



<P>the main of it; like a house that hath convenient



<P>stairs and entries, but never a fair room.&nbsp; Therefore,



<P>you shall see them find out pretty looses in the con-



<P>clusion, but are no ways able to examine or debate



<P>matters.&nbsp; And yet commonly they take advantage



<P>of their inability, and would be thought wits of



<P>direction.&nbsp; Some build rather upon the abusing of



<P>others, and (as we now say) putting tricks upon



<P>them, than upon soundness of their own proceed-



<P>ings.&nbsp; But Solomon saith, Prudens advertit ad gres-



<P>sus suos; stultus divertit ad dolos.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Envy</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Envy</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THERE be none of the affections, which have



<P>been noted to fascinate or bewitch, but love



<P>and envy.&nbsp; They both have vehement wishes; they



<P>frame themselves readily into imaginations and



<P>suggestions; and they come easily into the eye,



<P>especially upon the present of the objects; which



<P>are the points that conduce to fascination, if any



<P>such thing there be.&nbsp; We see likewise, the Scripture



<P>calleth envy an evil eye; and the astrologers, call



<P>the evil influences of the stars, evil aspects; so that



<P>still there seemeth to be acknowledged, in the act



<P>of envy, an ejaculation or irradiation of the eye.



<P>Nay, some have been so curious, as to note, that



<P>the times when the stroke or percussion of an envi-



<P>ous eye doth most hurt, are when the party envied



<P>is beheld in glory or triumph; for that sets an edge



<P>upon envy: and besides, at such times the spirits



<P>of the person envied, do come forth most into the



<P>outward parts, and so meet the blow.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But leaving these curiosities (though not un-



<P>worthy to be thought on, in fit place), we will



<P>handle, what persons are apt to envy others; what



<P>persons are most subject to be envied themselves;



<P>and what is the difference between public and



<P>private envy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A man that hath no virtue in himself, ever en-



<P>vieth virtue in others.&nbsp; For men's minds, will either



<P>feed upon their own good, or upon others' evil; and



<P>who wanteth the one, will prey upon the other;



<P>and whoso is out of hope, to attain to another's



<P>virtue, will seek to come at even hand, by depress-



<P>ing another's fortune.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A man that is busy, and inquisitive, is com-



<P>monly envious.&nbsp; For to know much of other men's



<P>matters, cannot be because all that ado may con-



<P>cern his own estate; therefore it must needs be,



<P>that he taketh a kind of play-pleasure, in looking



<P>upon the fortunes of others.&nbsp; Neither can he, that



<P>mindeth but his own business, find much matter



<P>for envy.&nbsp; For envy is a gadding passion, and walk-



<P>eth the streets, and doth not keep home: Non est



<P>curiosus, quin idem sit malevolus.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Men of noble birth, are noted to be envious



<P>towards new men, when they rise.&nbsp; For the distance



<P>is altered, and it is like a deceit of the eye, that



<P>when others come on, they think themselves, go



<P>back.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Deformed persons, and eunuchs, and old men,



<P>and bastards, are envious.&nbsp; For he that cannot pos-



<P>sibly mend his own case, will do what he can, to



<P>impair another's; except these defects light upon



<P>a very brave, and heroical nature, which thinketh



<P>to make his natural wants part of his honor; in that



<P>it should be said, that an eunuch, or a lame man,



<P>did such great matters; affecting the honor of a



<P>miracle; as it was in Narses the eunuch, and Agesi-



<P>laus and Tamberlanes, that were lame men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The same is the case of men, that rise after ca-



<P>lamities and misfortunes.&nbsp; For they are as men



<P>fallen out with the times; and think other men's



<P>harms, a redemption of their own sufferings.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>They that desire to excel in too many matters,



<P>out of levity and vain glory, are ever envious.&nbsp; For



<P>they cannot want work; it being impossible, but



<P>many, in some one of those things, should surpass



<P>them.&nbsp; Which was the character of Adrian the Em-



<P>peror; that mortally envied poets, and painters,



<P>and artificers, in works wherein he had a vein to



<P>excel.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lastly, near kinsfolks, and fellows in office, and



<P>those that have been bred together, are more apt



<P>to envy their equals, when they are raised.&nbsp; For it



<P>doth upbraid unto them their own fortunes, and



<P>pointeth at them, and cometh oftener into their



<P>remembrance, and incurreth likewise more into



<P>the note of others; and envy ever redoubleth from



<P>speech and fame.&nbsp; Cain's envy was the more vile



<P>and malignant, towards his brother Abel, because



<P>when his sacrifice was better accepted, there was



<P>no body to look on.&nbsp; Thus much for those, that are



<P>apt to envy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Concerning those that are more or less subject



<P>to envy: First, persons of eminent virtue, when



<P>they are advanced, are less envied.&nbsp; For their for-



<P>tune seemeth , but due unto them; and no man



<P>envieth the payment of a debt, but rewards and



<P>liberality rather.&nbsp; Again, envy is ever joined with



<P>the comparing of a man's self; and where there is



<P>no comparison, no envy; and therefore kings are



<P>not envied, but by kings.&nbsp; Nevertheless it is to be



<P>noted, that unworthy persons are most envied, at



<P>their first coming in, and afterwards overcome it



<P>better; whereas contrariwise, persons of worth



<P>and merit are most envied, when their fortune



<P>continueth long.&nbsp; For by that time, though their



<P>virtue be the same, yet it hath not the same lustre;



<P>for fresh men grow up that darken it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Persons of noble blood, are less envied in their



<P>rising.&nbsp; For it seemeth but right done to their birth.



<P>Besides, there seemeth not much added to their



<P>fortune; and envy is as the sunbeams, that beat



<P>hotter upon a bank, or steep rising ground, than



<P>upon a flat.&nbsp; And for the same reason, those that are



<P>advanced by degrees, are less envied than those



<P>that are advanced suddenly and per saltum.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Those that have joined with their honor great



<P>travels, cares, or perils, are less subject to envy.



<P>For men think that they earn their honors hardly,



<P>and pity them sometimes; and pity ever healeth



<P>envy.&nbsp; Wherefore you shall observe, that the more



<P>deep and sober sort of politic persons, in their



<P>greataess, are ever bemoaning themselves, what



<P>a life they lead; chanting a quanta patimur! Not



<P>that they feel it so, but only to abate the edge of



<P>envy.&nbsp; But this is to be understood, of business that



<P>is laid upon men, and not such, as they call unto



<P>themselves.&nbsp; For nothing increaseth envy more,



<P>than an unnecessary and ambitious engrossing of



<P>business.&nbsp; And nothing doth extinguish envy more,



<P>than for a great person to preserve all other infe-



<P>rior officers, in their full lights and pre-eminences



<P>of their places.&nbsp; For by that means, there be so



<P>many screens between him and envy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Above all, those are most subject to envy, which



<P>carry the greatness of their fortunes, in an insolent



<P>and proud manner; being never well, but while



<P>they are showing how great they are, either by



<P>outward pomp, or by triumphing over all opposi-



<P>tion or competition; whereas wise men will rather



<P>do sacrifice to envy, in suffering themselves some-



<P>times of purpose to be crossed, and overborne in



<P>things that do not much concern them.&nbsp; Notwith-



<P>standing, so much is true, that the carriage of



<P>greatness, in a plain and open manner (so it be



<P>without arrogancy and vain glory) doth draw less



<P>envy, than if it be in a more crafty and cunning



<P>fashion.&nbsp; For in that course, a man doth but dis-



<P>avow fortune; and seemeth to be conscious of his



<P>own want in worth; and doth but teach others, to



<P>envy him.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lastly, to conclude this part; as we said in the



<P>beginning, that the act of envy had somewhat in



<P>it of witchcraft, so there is no other cure of envy,



<P>but the cure of witchcraft; and that is, to remove



<P>the lot (as they call it) and to lay it upon another.



<P>For which purpose, the wiser sort of great persons,



<P>bring in ever upon the stage somebody upon whom



<P>to derive the envy, that would come upon them-



<P>selves; sometimes upon ministers and servants;



<P>sometimes upon colleagues and associates; and the



<P>like; and for that turn there are never wanting,



<P>some persons of violent and undertaking natures,



<P>who, so they may have power and business, will



<P>take it at any cost.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Now, to speak of public envy.&nbsp; There is yet some



<P>good in public envy, whereas in private, there is



<P>none.&nbsp; For public envy, is as an ostracism, that



<P>eclipseth men, when they grow too great.&nbsp; And



<P>therefore it is a bridle also to great ones, to keep



<P>them within bounds.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This envy, being in the Latin word invidia,



<P>goeth in the modern language, by the name of



<P>discontentment; of which we shall speak, in hand-



<P>ling sedition.&nbsp; It is a disease, in a state, like to infec-



<P>tion.&nbsp; For as infection spreadeth upon that which is



<P>sound, and tainteth it; so when envy is gotten once



<P>into a state, it traduceth even the best actions



<P>thereof, and turneth them into an ill odor.&nbsp; And



<P>therefore there is little won, by intermingling of



<P>plausible actions.&nbsp; For that doth argue but a weak-



<P>ness, and fear of envy, which hurteth so much the



<P>more, as it is likewise usual in infections; which



<P>if you fear them, you call them upon you.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This public envy, seemeth to beat chiefly upon



<P>principal officers or ministers, rather than upon



<P>kings, and estates themselves.&nbsp; But this is a sure



<P>rule, that if the envy upon the minister be great,



<P>when the cause of it in him is small; or if the envy



<P>be general, in a manner upon all the ministers of



<P>an estate; then the envy (though hidden) is truly



<P>upon the state itself.&nbsp; And so much of public envy



<P>or discontentment, and the difference thereof from



<P>private envy, which was handled in the first place.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>We will add this in general, touching the affec-



<P>tion of envy; that of all other affections, it is the



<P>most importune and continual.&nbsp; For of other affec-



<P>tions, there is occasion given, but now and then;



<P>and therefore it was well said, Invidia festos dies



<P>non agit: for it is ever working upon some or other.



<P>And it is also noted, that love and envy do make a



<P>man pine, which other affections do not, because



<P>they are not so continual.&nbsp; It is also the vilest affec-



<P>tion, and the most depraved; for which cause it



<P>is the proper attribute of the devil, who is called,



<P>the envious man, that soweth tares amongst the



<P>wheat by night; as it always cometh to pass, that



<P>envy worketh subtilly, and in the dark, and to the



<P>prejudice of good things, such as is the wheat.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>of death</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Death</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MEN fear death, as children fear to go in the



<P>dark; and as that natural fear in children,



<P>is increased with tales, so is the other.&nbsp; Certainly,



<P>the contemplation of death, as the wages of sin,



<P>and passage to another world, is holy and relig-



<P>ious; but the fear of it, as a tribute due unto nature,



<P>is weak.&nbsp; Yet in religious meditations, there is some-



<P>times mixture of vanity, and of superstition.&nbsp; You



<P>shall read, in some of the friars' books of mortifica-



<P>tion, that a man should think with himself, what



<P>the pain is, if he have but his finger's end pressed,



<P>or tortured, and thereby imagine, what the pains



<P>of death are, when the whole body is corrupted,



<P>and dissolved; when many times death passeth,



<P>with less pain than the torture of a limb; for the



<P>most vital parts, are not the quickest of sense.&nbsp; And



<P>by him that spake only as a philosopher, and nat-



<P>ural man, it was well said, Pompa mortis magis



<P>terret, quam mors ipsa.&nbsp; Groans, and convulsions,



<P>and a discolored face, and friends weeping, and



<P>blacks, and obsequies, and the like, show death



<P>terrible.&nbsp; It is worthy the observing, that there is no



<P>passion in the mind of man, so weak, but it mates,



<P>and masters, the fear of death; and therefore,



<P>death is no such terrible enemy, when a man hath



<P>so many attendants about him, that can win the



<P>combat of him.&nbsp; Revenge triumphs over death; love



<P>slights it; honor aspireth to it; grief flieth to it; fear



<P>preoccupateth it; nay, we read, after Otho the em-



<P>peror had slain himself, pity (which is the tender-



<P>est of affections) provoked many to die, out of mere



<P>compassion to their sovereign, and as the truest



<P>sort of followers.&nbsp; Nay, Seneca adds niceness and



<P>satiety: Cogita quamdiu eadem feceris; mori velle,



<P>non tantum fortis aut miser, sed etiam fastidiosus



<P>potest.&nbsp; A man would die, though he were neither



<P>valiant, nor miserable, only upon a weariness to



<P>do the same thing so oft, over and over.&nbsp; It is no less



<P>worthy, to observe, how little alteration in good



<P>spirits, the approaches of death make; for they



<P>appear to be the same men, till the last instant.



<P>Augustus Caesar died in a compliment; Livia, con-



<P>jugii nostri memor, vive et vale.&nbsp; Tiberius in dissi-



<P>mulation; as Tacitus saith of him, Jam Tiberium



<P>vires et corpus, non dissimulatio, deserebant.&nbsp; Ves-



<P>pasian in a jest, sitting upon the stool; Ut puto deus



<P>fio.&nbsp; Galba with a sentence; Feri, si ex re sit populi



<P>Romani; holding forth his neck.&nbsp; Septimius Severus



<P>in despatch; Adeste si quid mihi restat agendum.



<P>And the like.&nbsp; Certainly the Stoics bestowed too



<P>much cost upon death, and by their great prepara-



<P>tions, made it appear more fearful.&nbsp; Better saith he,



<P>qui finem vitae extremum inter munera ponat



<P>naturae.&nbsp; It is as natural to die, as to be born; and to



<P>a little infant, perhaps, the one is as painful, as the



<P>other.&nbsp; He that dies in an earnest pursuit, is like one



<P>that is wounded in hot blood; who, for the time,



<P>scarce feels the hurt; and therefore a mind fixed,



<P>and bent upon somewhat that is good, doth avert



<P>the dolors of death.&nbsp; But, above all, believe it, the



<P>sweetest canticle is', Nunc dimittis; when a man



<P>hath obtained worthy ends, and expectations.



<P>Death hath this also; that it openeth the gate to



<P>good fame, and extinguisheth envy. - Extinctus



<P>amabitur idem.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Love</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Love</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE stage is more beholding to love, than the



<P>life of man.&nbsp; For as to the stage, love is ever



<P>matter of comedies, and now and then of tragedies;



<P>but in life it doth much mischief; sometimes like a



<P>siren, sometimes like a fury.&nbsp; You may observe, that



<P>amongst all the great and worthy persons (whereof



<P>the memory remaineth, either ancient or recent)



<P>there is not one, that hath been transported to



<P>the mad degree of love: which shows that great



<P>spirits, and great business, do keep out this weak



<P>passion.&nbsp; You must except, nevertheless, Marcus



<P>Antonius, the half partner of the empire of Rome,



<P>and Appius Claudius, the decemvir and lawgiver;



<P>whereof the former was indeed a voluptuous man,



<P>and inordinate; but the latter was an austere and



<P>wise man: and therefore it seems (though rarely)



<P>that love can find entrance, not only into an open



<P>heart, but also into a heart well fortified, if watch



<P>be not well kept.&nbsp; It is a poor saying of Epicurus,



<P>Satis&nbsp; magnum&nbsp; alter&nbsp; alteri&nbsp; theatrum&nbsp; sumus;&nbsp;

as&nbsp;&nbsp; if



<P>man,&nbsp; made&nbsp; for&nbsp; the&nbsp; contemplation&nbsp; of&nbsp;&nbsp;

heaven,&nbsp;&nbsp; and



<P>all noble objects, should do nothing but kneel be-



<P>fore a little idol, and make himself a subject,



<P>though not of the mouth (as beasts are), yet of the



<P>eye; which was given him for higher purposes.&nbsp; It



<P>is a strange thing, to note the excess of this passion,



<P>and how it braves the nature, and value of things,



<P>by this; that the speaking in a perpetual hyper-



<P>bole, is comely in nothing but in love.&nbsp; Neither is it



<P>merely in the phrase; for whereas it hath been



<P>well said, that the arch-flatterer, with whom all



<P>the petty flatterers have intelligence, is a man's



<P>self; certainly the lover is more.&nbsp; For there was



<P>never proud man thought so absurdly well of him-



<P>self, as the lover doth of the person loved; and



<P>therefore it was well said, That it is impossible to



<P>love, and to be wise.&nbsp; Neither doth this weakness



<P>appear to others only, and not to the party loved;



<P>but to the loved most of all, except the love be reci-



<P>proque.&nbsp; For it is a true rule, that love is ever re-



<P>warded, either with the reciproque, or with an



<P>inward and secret contempt.&nbsp; By how much the



<P>more, men ought to beware of this passion, which



<P>loseth not only other things, but itself! As for the



<P>other losses, the poet's relation doth well figure



<P>them: that he that preferred Helena, quitted the



<P>gifts of Juno and Pallas.&nbsp; For whosoever esteemeth



<P>too much of amorous affection, quitteth both riches



<P>and wisdom.&nbsp; This passion hath his floods, in very



<P>times of weakness; which are great prosperity, and



<P>great adversity; though this latter hath been less



<P>observed: both which times kindle love, and make



<P>it more fervent, and therefore show it to be the



<P>child of folly.&nbsp; They do best, who if they cannot but



<P>admit love, yet make it keep quarters; and sever it



<P>wholly from their serious affairs, and actions, of



<P>life; for if it check once with business, it troubleth



<P>men's fortunes, and maketh men, that they can no



<P>ways be true to their own ends.&nbsp; I know not how,



<P>but martial men are given to love: I think, it is but



<P>as they are given to wine; for perils commonly ask



<P>to be paid in pleasures.&nbsp; There is in man's nature, a



<P>secret inclination and motion, towards love of



<P>others, which if it be not spent upon some one or a



<P>few, doth naturally spread itself towards many,



<P>and maketh men become humane and charitable;



<P>as it is seen sometime in friars.&nbsp; Nuptial love maketh



<P>mankind; friendly love perfecteth it; but wanton



<P>love corrupteth, and embaseth it.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Prophecies</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Prophecies</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>I MEAN not to speak of divine prophecies; nor



<P>of heathen oracles; nor of natural predictions;



<P>but only of prophecies that have been of cer-



<P>tain memory, and from hidden causes.&nbsp; Saith the



<P>Pythonissa to Saul, To-morrow thou and thy son



<P>shall be with me.&nbsp; Homer hath these verses:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>At domus AEneae cunctis dominabitur oris,



<P>Et nati natorum, et qui nascentur ab illis.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A prophecy, as it seems, of the Roman empire.



<P>Seneca the tragedian hath these verses:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>&nbsp; --Venient annis



<P>Saecula seris, quibus Oceanus



<P>Vincula rerum laxet, et ingens



<P>Pateat Tellus, Tiphysque novos



<P>Detegat orbes; nec sit terris



<P>Ultima Thule:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>a prophecy of the discovery of America.&nbsp; The daugh-



<P>ter of Polycrates, dreamed that Jupiter bathed her



<P>father, and Apollo anointed him; and it came to



<P>pass, that he was crucified in an open place, where



<P>the sun made his body run with sweat, and the



<P>rain washed it.&nbsp; Philip of Macedon dreamed, he



<P>sealed up bis wife's belly; whereby he did expound



<P>it, that his wife should be barren; but Aristander



<P>the soothsayer, told him his wife was with child,



<P>because men do not use to seal vessels, that are



<P>empty.&nbsp; A phantasm that appeared to M. Brutus, in



<P>his tent, said to him, Philippis iterum me videbis.



<P>Tiberius said to Galba, Tu quoque, Galba, degusta-



<P>bis imperium.&nbsp; In Vespasian's time, there went a



<P>prophecy in the East, that those that should come



<P>forth of Judea, should reign over the world:



<P>which though it may be was meant of our Savior;



<P>yet Tacitus expounds it of Vespasian.&nbsp; Domitian



<P>dreamed, the night before he was slain, that a



<P>golden head was growing, out of the nape of his



<P>neck: and indeed, the succession that followed him



<P>for many years, made golden times.&nbsp; Henry the



<P>Sixth of England, said of Henry the Seventh, when



<P>he was a lad, and gave him water, This is the lad



<P>that shall enjoy the crown, for which we strive.



<P>When I was in France, I heard from one Dr. Pena,



<P>that the Queen Mother, who was given to curious



<P>arts, caused the King her husband's nativity to be



<P>calculated, under a false name; and the astrologer



<P>gave a judgment, that he should be killed in a duel;



<P>at which the Queen laughed, thinking her hus-



<P>band to be above challenges and duels: but he was



<P>slain upon a course at tilt, the splinters of the staff



<P>of Montgomery going in at his beaver.&nbsp; The trivial



<P>prophecy, which I heard when I was a child, and



<P>Queen Elizabeth was in the flower of her years,



<P>was,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>When hempe is spun

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>England's done:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>whereby it was generally conceived, that after the



<P>princes had reigned, which had the principal



<P>letters of that word hempe (which were Henry,



<P>Edward, Mary, Philip, and Elizabeth), England



<P>should come to utter confusion; which, thanks be



<P>to God, is verified only in the change of the name;



<P>for that the King's style, is now no more of Eng-



<P>land but of Britain.&nbsp; There was also another proph-



<P>ecy, before the year of '88, which I do not well



<P>understand.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>There shall be seen upon a day,



<P>Between the Baugh and the May,



<P>The black fleet of Norway.



<P>When that that is come and gone,



<P>England build houses of lime and stone,



<P>For after wars shall you have none.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It was generally conceived to be meant, of the



<P>Spanish fleet that came in '88: for that the king of



<P>Spain's surname, as they say, is Norway.&nbsp; The pre-



<P>diction of Regiomontanus,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Octogesimus octavus mirabilis annus,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>was thought likewise accomplished in the sending



<P>of that great fleet, being the greatest in strength,



<P>though not in number, of all that ever swam upon



<P>the sea.&nbsp; As for Cleon's dream, I think it was a jest.



<P>It was, that he was devoured of a long dragon; and



<P>it was expounded of a maker of sausages, that



<P>troubled him exceedingly.&nbsp; There are numbers of



<P>the like kind; especially if you include dreams, and



<P>predictions of astrology.&nbsp; But I have set down these



<P>few only, of certain credit, for example.&nbsp; My judg-



<P>ment is, that they ought all to be despised; and



<P>ought to serve but for winter talk by the fireside.



<P>Though when I say despised, I mean it as for be-



<P>lief; for otherwise, the spreading, or publishing,



<P>of them, is in no sort to be despised.&nbsp; For they have



<P>done much mischief; and I see many severe laws



<P>made, to suppress them.&nbsp; That that hath given them



<P>grace, and some credit, consisteth in three things.



<P>First, that men mark when they hit, and never



<P>mark when they miss; as they do generally also of



<P>dreams.&nbsp; The second is, that probable conjectures,



<P>or obscure traditions, many times turn themselves



<P>into prophecies; while the nature of man, which



<P>coveteth divination, thinks it no peril to foretell



<P>that which indeed they do but collect.&nbsp; As that of



<P>Seneca's verse.&nbsp; For so much was then subject to



<P>demonstration, that the globe of the earth had



<P>great parts beyond the Atlantic, which mought



<P>be probably conceived not to be all sea: and adding



<P>thereto the tradition in Plato's Timaeus, and his



<P>Atlanticus, it mought encourage one to turn it to



<P>a prediction.&nbsp; The third and last (which is the great



<P>one) is, that almost all of them, being infinite in



<P>number, have been impostures, and by idle and



<P>crafty brains merely contrived and feigned, after



<P>the event past.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Anger</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Anger</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>TO SEEK to extinguish anger utterly, is but a



<P>bravery of the Stoics.&nbsp; We have better oracles:



<P>Be angry, but sin not.&nbsp; Let not the sun go down



<P>upon your anger.&nbsp; Anger must be limited and con-



<P>fined, both in race and in time.&nbsp; We will first speak



<P>how the natural inclination and habit to be angry,



<P>may be attempted and calmed.&nbsp; Secondly, how the



<P>particular motions of anger may be repressed, or



<P>at least refrained from doing mischief.&nbsp; Thirdly,



<P>how to raise anger, or appease anger in another.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the first; there is no other way but to medi-



<P>tate, and ruminate well upon the effects of anger,



<P>how it troubles man's life.&nbsp; And the best time to do



<P>this, is to look back upon anger, when the fit is



<P>thoroughly over.&nbsp; Seneca saith well, That anger is



<P>like ruin, which breaks itself upon that it falls.



<P>The Scripture exhorteth us to possess our souls in



<P>patience.&nbsp; Whosoever is out of patience, is out of



<P>possession of his soul.&nbsp; Men must not turn bees;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>...&nbsp; animasque in vulnere ponunt.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Anger is certainly a kind of baseness; as it ap-



<P>pears well in the weakness of those subjects in



<P>whom it reigns; children, women, old folks, sick



<P>folks.&nbsp; Only men must beware, that they carry



<P>their anger rather with scorn, than with fear; so



<P>that they may seem rather to be above the injury,



<P>than below it; which is a thing easily done, if a



<P>man will give law to himself in it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the second point; the causes and motives of



<P>anger, are chiefly three.&nbsp; First, to be too sensible of



<P>hurt; for no man is angry, that feels not himself



<P>hurt; and therefore tender and delicate persons



<P>must needs be oft angry; they have so many things



<P>to trouble them, which more robust natures have



<P>little sense of.&nbsp; The next is, the apprehension and



<P>construction of the injury offered, to be, in the cir-



<P>cumstances thereof, full of contempt: for contempt



<P>is that, which putteth an edge upon anger, as much



<P>or more than the hurt itself.&nbsp; And therefore, when



<P>men are ingenious in picking out circumstances of



<P>contempt, they do kindle their anger much.&nbsp; Lastly,



<P>opinion of the touch of a man's reputation, doth



<P>multiply and sharpen anger.&nbsp; Wherein the remedy



<P>is, that a man should have, as Consalvo was wont



<P>to say, telam honoris crassiorem.&nbsp; But in all refrain-



<P>ings of anger, it is the best remedy to win time;



<P>and to make a man's self believe, that the oppor-



<P>tunity of his revenge is not yet come, but that he



<P>foresees a time for it; and so to still himself in the



<P>meantime, and reserve it.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To contain anger from mischief, though it take



<P>hold of a man, there be two things, whereof you



<P>must have special caution.&nbsp; The one, of extreme bit-



<P>terness of words, especially if they be aculeate and



<P>proper; for cummunia maledicta are nothing so



<P>much; and again, that in anger a man reveal no



<P>secrets; for that, makes him not fit for society.&nbsp; The



<P>other, that you do not peremptorily break off, in



<P>any business, in a fit of anger; but howsoever you



<P>show bitterness, do not act anything, that is not



<P>revocable.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For raising and appeasing anger in another; it



<P>is done chiefly by choosing of times, when men



<P>are frowardest and worst disposed, to incense



<P>them.&nbsp; Again, by gathering (as was touched before)



<P>all that you can find out, to aggravate the con-



<P>tempt.&nbsp; And the two remedies are by the contraries.



<P>The former to take good times, when first to relate



<P>to a man an angry business; for the first impres-



<P>sion is much; and the other is, to sever, as much as



<P>may be, the construction of the injury from the



<P>point of contempt; imputing it to misunderstand-



<P>ing, fear, passion, or what you will.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Boldness</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Boldness</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>IT IS a trivial grammar-school text, but yet



<P>worthy a wise man's consideration.&nbsp; Question



<P>was asked of Demosthenes, what was the chief



<P>part of an orator? he answered, action; what next?



<P>action; what next again? action.&nbsp; He said it, that



<P>knew it best, and had, by nature, himself no ad-



<P>vantage in that he commended.&nbsp; A strange thing,



<P>that that part of an orator, which is but superficial,



<P>and rather the virtue of a player, should be placed



<P>so high, above those other noble parts, of invention,



<P>elocution, and the rest; nay, almost alone, as if it



<P>were all in all.&nbsp; But the reason is plain.&nbsp; There is in



<P>human nature generally, more of the fool than of



<P>the wise; and therefore those faculties, by which



<P>the foolish part of men's minds is taken, are most



<P>potent.&nbsp; Wonderful like is the case of boldness in



<P>civil business: what first? boldness; what second



<P>and third? boldness.&nbsp; And yet boldness is a child of



<P>ignorance and baseness, far inferior to other parts.



<P>But nevertheless it doth fascinate, and bind hand



<P>and foot, those that are either shallow in judg-



<P>ment, or weak in courage, which are the greatest



<P>part; yea and prevaileth with wise men at weak



<P>times.&nbsp; Therefore we see it hath done wonders, in



<P>popular states; but with senates, and princes less;



<P>and more ever upon the first entrance of bold per-



<P>sons into action, than soon after; for boldness is an



<P>ill keeper of promise.&nbsp; Surely, as there are mounte-



<P>banks for the natural body, so are there mounte-



<P>banks for the politic body; men that undertake



<P>great cures, and perhaps have been lucky, in two



<P>or three experiments, but want the grounds of



<P>science, and therefore cannot hold out.&nbsp; Nay, you



<P>shall see a bold fellow many times do Mahomet's



<P>miracle.&nbsp; Mahomet made the people believe that



<P>he would call an hill to him, and from the top of it



<P>offer up his prayers, for the observers of his law.



<P>The people assembled; Mahomet called the hill to



<P>come to him, again and again; and when the hill



<P>stood still, he was never a whit abashed, but said,



<P>If the hill will not come to Mahomet, Mahomet



<P>will go to the hill.&nbsp; So these men, when they have



<P>promised great matters, and failed most shame-



<P>fully, yet (if they have the perfection of boldness)



<P>they will but slight it over, and make a turn, and



<P>no more ado.&nbsp; Certainly to men of great judgment,



<P>bold persons are a sport to behold; nay, and to the



<P>vulgar also, boldness has somewhat of the ridicu-



<P>lous.&nbsp; For if absurdity be the subject of laughter,



<P>doubt you not but great boldness is seldom without



<P>some absurdity.&nbsp; Especially it is a sport to see, when



<P>a bold fellow is out of countenance; for that puts



<P>his face into a most shrunken, and wooden pos-



<P>ture; as needs it must; for in bashfulness, the spirits



<P>do a little go and come; but with bold men, upon



<P>like occasion, they stand at a stay; like a stale at



<P>chess, where it is no mate, but yet the game cannot



<P>stir.&nbsp; But this last were fitter for a satire than for a



<P>serious observation.&nbsp; This is well to be weighed;



<P>that boldness is ever blind; for it seeth not danger,



<P>and inconveniences.&nbsp; Therefore it is ill in counsel,



<P>good in execution; so that the right use of bold per-



<P>sons is, that they never command in chief, but be



<P>seconds, and under the direction of others.&nbsp; For in



<P>counsel, it is good to see dangers; and in execution,



<P>not to see them, except they be very great.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Regiment OF HEALTH</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Regiment OF HEALTH</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THERE is a wisdom in this; beyond the rules of



<P>physic: a man's own observation, what he



<P>finds good of, and what he finds hurt of, is the best



<P>physic to preserve health.&nbsp; But it is a safer conclu-



<P>sion to say, This agreeth not well with me, there-



<P>fore, I will not continue it; than this, I find no



<P>offence of this, therefore I may use it.&nbsp; For strength



<P>of nature in youth, passeth over many excesses,



<P>which are owing a man till his age.&nbsp; Discern of the



<P>coming on of years, and think not to do the same



<P>things still; for age will not be defied.&nbsp; Beware of



<P>sudden change, in any great point of diet, and, if



<P>necessity enforce it, fit the rest to it.&nbsp; For it is a secret



<P>both in nature and state, that it is safer to change



<P>many things, than one.&nbsp; Examine thy customs of



<P>diet, sleep, exercise, apparel, and the like; and try,



<P>in any thing thou shalt judge hurtful, to discon-



<P>tinue it, by little and little; but so, as if thou dost



<P>find any inconvenience by the change, thou come



<P>back to it again: for it is hard to distinguish that



<P>which is generally held good and wholesome,



<P>from that which is good particularly, and fit for



<P>thine own body.&nbsp; To be free-minded and cheerfully



<P>disposed, at hours of meat, and of sleep, and of



<P>exercise, is one of the best precepts of long lasting.



<P>As for the passions, and studies of the mind; avoid



<P>envy, anxious fears; anger fretting inwards;



<P>subtle and knotty inquisitions; joys and exhilara-



<P>tions in excess; sadness not communicated.&nbsp; Enter-



<P>tain hopes; mirth rather than joy; variety of



<P>delights, rather than surfeit of them; wonder and



<P>admiration, and therefore novelties; studies that



<P>fill the mind with splendid and illustrious objects,



<P>as histories, fables, and contemplations of nature.



<P>If you fly physic in health altogether, it will be too



<P>strange for your body, when you shall need it.&nbsp; If



<P>you make it too familiar, it will work no extra-



<P>ordinary effect, when sickness cometh.&nbsp; I commend



<P>rather some diet for certain seasons, than frequent



<P>use of physic, except it be grown into a custom.&nbsp; For



<P>those diets alter the body more, and trouble it less.



<P>Despise no new accident in your body, but ask



<P>opinion of it.&nbsp; In sickness, respect health prin-



<P>cipally; and in health, action.&nbsp; For those that put



<P>their bodies to endure in health, may in most sick-



<P>nesses, which are not very sharp, be cured only



<P>with diet, and tendering.&nbsp; Celsus could never have



<P>spoken it as a physician, had he not been a wise



<P>man withal, when he giveth it for one of the great



<P>precepts of health and lasting, that a man do vary,



<P>and interchange contraries, but with an inclina-



<P>tion to the more benign extreme: use fasting and



<P>full eating, but rather full eating; watching and



<P>sleep, but rather sleep; sitting and exercise, but



<P>rather exercise; and the like.&nbsp; So shall nature be



<P>cherished, and yet taught masteries.&nbsp; Physicians



<P>are, some of them, so pleasing and conformable to



<P>the humor of the patient, as they press not the true



<P>cure of the disease; and some other are so regular,



<P>in proceeding according to art for the disease, as



<P>they respect not sufficiently the condition of the



<P>patient.&nbsp; Take one of a middle temper; or if it may



<P>not be found in one man, combine two of either



<P>sort; and forget not to call as well, the best ac-



<P>quainted with your body, as the best reputed of



<P>for his faculty.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Friendship</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Friendship</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>IT HAD been hard for him that spake it to have



<P>put more truth and untruth together in few



<P>words, than in that speech, Whatsoever is delighted



<P>in solitude, is either a wild beast or a god.&nbsp; For it is



<P>most true, that a natural and secret hatred, and



<P>aversation towards society, in any man, hath



<P>somewhat of the savage beast; but it is most un-



<P>true, that it should have any character at all, of the



<P>divine nature; except it proceed, not out of a pleas-



<P>ure in solitude, but out of a love and desire to



<P>sequester a man's self, for a higher conversation:



<P>such as is found to have been falsely and feignedly



<P>in some of the heathen; as Epimenides the Can-



<P>dian, Numa the Roman, Empedocles the Sicilian,



<P>and Apollonius of Tyana; and truly and really, in



<P>divers of the ancient hermits and holy fathers of



<P>the church.&nbsp; But little do men perceive what soli-



<P>tude is, and how far it extendeth.&nbsp; For a crowd is



<P>not company; and faces are but a gallery of pic-



<P>tures; and talk but a tinkling cymbal, where



<P>there is no love.&nbsp; The Latin adage meeteth with it a



<P>little: Magna civitas, magna solitudo; because in



<P>a great town friends are scattered; so that there is



<P>not that fellowship, for the most part, which is in



<P>less neighborhoods.&nbsp; But we may go further, and



<P>affirm most truly, that it is a mere and miserable



<P>solitude to want true friends; without which the



<P>world is but a wilderness; and even in this sense



<P>also of solitude, whosoever in the frame of his



<P>nature and affections, is unfit for friendship, he



<P>taketh it of the beast, and not from humanity.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>A principal fruit of friendship, is the ease and



<P>discharge of the fulness and swellings of the heart,



<P>which passions of all kinds do cause and induce.



<P>We know diseases of stoppings, and suffocations,



<P>are the most dangerous in the body; and it is not



<P>much otherwise in the mind; you may take sarza



<P>to open the liver, steel to open the spleen, flowers



<P>of sulphur for the lungs, castoreum for the brain;



<P>but no receipt openeth the heart, but a true friend;



<P>to whom you may impart griefs, joys, fears, hopes,



<P>suspicions, counsels, and whatsoever lieth upon



<P>the heart to oppress it, in a kind of civil shrift or



<P>confession.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is a strange thing to observe, how high a rate



<P>great kings and monarchs do set upon this fruit of



<P>friendship, whereof we speak: so great, as they



<P>purchase it, many times, at the hazard of their



<P>own safety and greatness.&nbsp; For princes, in regard



<P>of the distance of their fortune from that of their



<P>subjects and servants, cannot gather this fruit, ex-



<P>cept (to make themselves capable thereof) they



<P>raise some persons to be, as it were, companions



<P>and almost equals to themselves, which many



<P>times sorteth to inconvenience.&nbsp; The modern lan-



<P>guages give unto such persons the name of favor-



<P>ites, or privadoes; as if it were matter of grace, or



<P>conversation.&nbsp; But the Roman name attaineth the



<P>true use and cause thereof, naming them parti-



<P>cipes curarum; for it is that which tieth the knot.



<P>And we see plainly that this hath been done, not



<P>by weak and passionate princes only, but by the



<P>wisest and most politic that ever reigned; who



<P>have oftentimes joined to themselves some of



<P>their servants; whom both themselves have called



<P>friends, and allowed other likewise to call them in



<P>the same manner; using the word which is re-



<P>ceived between private men.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>L. Sylla, when he commanded Rome, raised



<P>Pompey (after surnamed the Great) to that height,



<P>that Pompey vaunted himself for Sylla's over-



<P>match.&nbsp; For when he had carried the consulship for



<P>a friend of his, against the pursuit of Sylla, and



<P>that Sylla did a little resent thereat, and began to



<P>speak great, Pompey turned upon him again, and



<P>in effect bade him be quiet; for that more men



<P>adored the sun rising, than the sun setting.&nbsp; With



<P>Julius Caesar, Decimus Brutus had obtained that



<P>interest as he set him down in his testament, for



<P>heir in remainder, after his nephew.&nbsp; And this was



<P>the man that had power with him, to draw him



<P>forth to his death.&nbsp; For when Caesar would have



<P>discharged the senate, in regard of some ill pres-



<P>ages, and specially a dream of Calpurnia; this



<P>man lifted him gently by the arm out of his chair,



<P>telling him he hoped he would not dismiss the



<P>senate, till his wife had dreamt a better dream.



<P>And it seemeth his favor was so great, as Antonius,



<P>in a letter which is recited verbatim in one of



<P>Cicero's Philippics, calleth him venefica, witch;



<P>as if he had enchanted Caesar.&nbsp; Augustus raised



<P>Agrippa (though of mean birth) to that height, as



<P>when he consulted with Maecenas, about the mar-



<P>riage of his daughter Julia, Maecenas took the



<P>liberty to tell him, that he must either marry his



<P>daughter to Agrippa, or take away his life; there



<P>was no third way, he had made him so great.&nbsp; With



<P>Tiberius Caesar, Sejanus had ascended to that



<P>height, as they two were termed, and reckoned, as



<P>a pair of friends.&nbsp; Tiberius in a letter to him saith,



<P>Haec pro amicitia nostra non occultavi; and the



<P>whole senate dedicated an altar to Friendship, as



<P>to a goddess, in respect of the great dearness of



<P>friendship, between them two.&nbsp; The like, or more,



<P>was between Septimius Severus and Plautianus.



<P>For he forced his eldest son to marry the daughter



<P>of Plautianus; and would often maintain Plau-



<P>tianus, in doing affronts to his son; and did write



<P>also in a letter to the senate, by these words: I love



<P>the man so well, as I wish he may over-live me.



<P>Now if these princes had been as a Trajan, or a



<P>Marcus Aurelius, a man might have thought that



<P>this had proceeded of an abundant goodness of



<P>nature; but being men so wise, of such strength



<P>and severity of mind, and so extreme lovers of



<P>themselves, as all these were, it proveth most



<P>plainly that they found their own felicity (though



<P>as great as ever happened to mortal men) but as



<P>an half piece, except they mought have a friend,



<P>to make it entire; and yet, which is more, they



<P>were princes that had wives, sons, nephews; and



<P>yet all these could not supply the comfort of friend-



<P>ship.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is not to be forgotten, what Comineus observ-



<P>eth of his first master, Duke Charles the Hardy,



<P>namely, that he would communicate his secrets



<P>with none; and least of all, those secrets which



<P>troubled him most.&nbsp; Whereupon he goeth on, and



<P>saith that towards his latter time, that closeness



<P>did impair, and a little perish his understanding.



<P>Surely Comineus mought have made the same



<P>judgment also, if it had pleased him, of his second



<P>master, Lewis the Eleventh, whose closeness was



<P>indeed his tormentor.&nbsp; The parable of Pythagoras



<P>is dark, but true; Cor ne edito; Eat not the heart.



<P>Certainly, if a man would give it a hard phrase,



<P>those that want friends, to open themselves unto,



<P>are carnnibals of their own hearts.&nbsp; But one thing



<P>is most admirable (wherewith I will conclude this



<P>first fruit of friendship), which is, that this com-



<P>municating of a man's self to his friend, works



<P>two contrary effects; for it redoubleth joys, and



<P>cutteth griefs in halves.&nbsp; For there is no man, that



<P>imparteth his joys to his friend, but he joyeth the



<P>more; and no man that imparteth his griefs to his



<P>friend, but he grieveth the less.&nbsp; So that it is in truth,



<P>of operation upon a man's mind, of like virtue as



<P>the alchemists use to attribute to their stone, for



<P>man's body; that it worketh all contrary effects,



<P>but still to the good and benefit of nature.&nbsp; But yet



<P>without praying in aid of alchemists, there is a



<P>manifest image of this, in the ordinary course of



<P>nature.&nbsp; For in bodies, union strengtheneth and



<P>cherisheth any natural action; and on the other



<P>side, weakeneth and dulleth any violent impres-



<P>sion: and even so it is of minds.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The second fruit of friendship, is healthful and



<P>sovereign for the understanding, as the first is for



<P>the affections.&nbsp; For friendship maketh indeed a fair



<P>day in the affections, from storm and tempests; but



<P>it maketh daylight in the understanding, out of



<P>darkness, and confusion of thoughts.&nbsp; Neither is



<P>this to be understood only of faithful counsel,



<P>which a man receiveth from his friend; but before



<P>you come to that, certain it is, that whosoever hath



<P>his mind fraught with many thoughts, his wits



<P>and understanding do clarify and break up, in the



<P>communicating and discoursing with another; he



<P>tosseth his thoughts more easily; he marshalleth



<P>them more orderly, he seeth how they look when



<P>they are turned into words: finally, he waxeth



<P>wiser than himself; and that more by an hour's



<P>discourse, than by a day's meditation.&nbsp; It was well



<P>said by Themistocles, to the king of Persia, That



<P>speech was like cloth of Arras, opened and put



<P>abroad; whereby the imagery doth appear in



<P>figure; whereas in thoughts they lie but as in



<P>packs.&nbsp; Neither is this second fruit of friendship, in



<P>opening the understanding, restrained only to



<P>such friends as are able to give a man counsel;



<P>(they indeed are best;) but even without that, a



<P>man learneth of himself, and bringeth his own



<P>thoughts&nbsp; to light, and whetteth his wits as against



<P>a stone, which itself cuts not.&nbsp; In a word, a man



<P>were better relate himself to a statua, or picture,



<P>than to suffer his thoughts to pass in smother.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Add now, to make this second fruit of friendship



<P>complete, that other point, which lieth more open,



<P>and falleth within vulgar observation; which is



<P>faithful counsel from a friend.&nbsp; Heraclitus saith



<P>well in one of his enigmas, Dry light is ever the



<P>best.&nbsp; And certain it is, that the light that a man



<P>receiveth by counsel from another, is drier and



<P>purer, than that which cometh from his own



<P>understanding and judgment; which is ever in-



<P>fused, and drenched, in his affections and customs.



<P>So as there is as much difference between the coun-



<P>sel, that a friend giveth, and that a man giveth



<P>himself, as there is between the counsel of a friend,



<P>and of a flatterer.&nbsp; For there is no such flatterer as



<P>is a man's self; and there is no such remedy against



<P>flattery of a man's self, as the liberty of a friend.



<P>Counsel is of two sorts: the one concerning man-



<P>ners, the other concerning business.&nbsp; For the first,



<P>the best preservative to keep the mind in health, is



<P>the faithful admonition of a friend.&nbsp; The calling of



<P>a man's self to a strict account, is a medicine, some-



<P>time too piercing and corrosive.&nbsp; Reading good



<P>books of morality, is a little flat and dead.&nbsp; Observ-



<P>ing our faults in others, is sometimes improper for



<P>our case.&nbsp; But the best receipt (best, I say, to work,



<P>and best to take) is the admonition of a friend.



<P>It is a strange thing to behold, what gross errors



<P>and extreme absurdities many (especially of the



<P>greater sort) do commit, for want of a friend to tell



<P>them of them; to the great damage both of their



<P>fame and fortune: for, as St. James saith, they are



<P>as men that look sometimes into a glass, and pres-



<P>ently forget their own shape and favor.&nbsp; As for



<P>business, a man may think, if he win, that two



<P>eyes see no more than one; or that a gamester seeth



<P>always more than a looker-on; or that a man in



<P>anger, is as wise as he that hath said over the four



<P>and twenty letters; or that a musket may be shot



<P>off as well upon the arm, as upon a rest; and such



<P>other fond and high imaginations, to think him-



<P>self all in all.&nbsp; But when all is done, the help of good



<P>counsel, is that which setteth business straight.



<P>And if any man think that he will take counsel,



<P>but it shall be by pieces; asking counsel in one



<P>business, of one man, and in another business, of



<P>another man; it is well (that is to say, better, per-



<P>haps, than if he asked none at all); but he runneth



<P>two dangers: one, that he shall not be faithfully



<P>counselled; for it is a rare thing, except it be from



<P>a perfect and entire friend, to have counsel given,



<P>but such as shall be bowed and crooked to some



<P>ends, which he hath, that giveth it.&nbsp; The other, that



<P>he shall have counsel given, hurtful and unsafe



<P>(though with good meaning), and mixed partly of



<P>mischief and partly of remedy; even as if you



<P>would call a physician, that is thought good for



<P>the cure of the disease you complain of, but is unac-



<P>quainted with your body; and therefore may put



<P>you in way for a present cure, but overthroweth



<P>your health in some other kind; and so cure the



<P>disease, and kill the patient.&nbsp; But a friend that is



<P>wholly acquainted with a man's estate, will be-



<P>ware, by furthering any present business, how he



<P>dasheth upon other inconvenience.&nbsp; And therefore



<P>rest not upon scattered counsels; they will rather



<P>distract and mislead, than settle and direct.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>After these two noble fruits of friendship (peace



<P>in the affections, and support of the judgment),



<P>followeth the last fruit; which is like the pome-



<P>granate, full of many kernels; I mean aid, and



<P>bearing a part, in all actions and occasions.&nbsp; Here



<P>the best way to represent to life the manifold use



<P>of friendship, is to cast and see how many things



<P>there are, which a man cannot do himself; and



<P>then it will appear, that it was a sparing speech of



<P>the ancients, to say, that a friend is another him-



<P>self; for that a friend is far more than himself.



<P>Men have their time, and die many times, in de-



<P>sire of some things which they principally take to



<P>heart; the bestowing of a child, the finishing of a



<P>work, or the like.&nbsp; If a man have a true friend, he



<P>may rest almost secure that the care of those things



<P>will continue after him.&nbsp; So that a man hath, as it



<P>were, two lives in his desires.&nbsp; A man hath a body,



<P>and that body is confined to a place; but where



<P>friendship is, all offices of life are as it were granted



<P>to him, and his deputy.&nbsp; For he may exercise them



<P>by his friend.&nbsp; How many things are there which



<P>a man cannot, with any face or comeliness, say or



<P>do himself?&nbsp; A man can scarce allege his own



<P>merits with modesty, much less extol them; a man



<P>cannot sometimes brook to supplicate or beg; and



<P>a number of the like.&nbsp; But all these things are grace-



<P>ful, in a friend's mouth, which are blushing in a



<P>man's own.&nbsp; So again, a man's person hath many



<P>proper relations, which he cannot put off.&nbsp; A man



<P>cannot speak to his son but as a father; to his wife



<P>but as a husband; to his enemy but upon terms:



<P>whereas a friend may speak as the case requires,



<P>and not as it sorteth with the person.&nbsp; But to enu-



<P>merate these things were endless; I have given the



<P>rule, where a man cannot fitly play his own part;



<P>if he have not a friend, he may quit the stage.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Marriage AND SINGLE LIFE</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Marriage AND SINGLE LIFE</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>HE THAT hath wife and children hath given



<P>hostages to fortune; for they are impedi-



<P>ments to great enterprises, either of virtue or mis-



<P>chief.&nbsp; Certainly&nbsp; the&nbsp; best&nbsp; works,&nbsp; and&nbsp;

of&nbsp; greatest



<P>merit for the&nbsp; public,&nbsp; have&nbsp; proceeded&nbsp; from&nbsp;

the&nbsp; un-



<P>married or childless men; which both in affection



<P>and means, have married and endowed the public.



<P>Yet it were great reason that those that have chil-



<P>dren, should have greatest care of future times;



<P>unto which they know they must transmit their



<P>dearest pledges.&nbsp; Some there are, who though they



<P>lead a single life, yet their thoughts do end with



<P>themselves, and account future times imperti-



<P>nences.&nbsp; Nay, there are some other, that account



<P>wife and children, but as bills of charges.&nbsp; Nay



<P>more, there are some foolish rich covetous men,



<P>that take a pride, in having no children, because



<P>they may be thought so much the richer.&nbsp; For per-



<P>haps they have heard some talk, Such an one is a



<P>great rich man, and another except to it, Yea, but



<P>he hath a great charge of children; as if it were an



<P>abatement to his riches.&nbsp; But the most ordinary



<P>cause of a single life, is liberty, especially in certain



<P>self-pleasing and humorous minds, which are so



<P>sensible of every restraint, as they will go near to



<P>think their girdles and garters, to be bonds and



<P>shackles.&nbsp; Unmarried men are best friends, best



<P>masters, best servants; but not always best sub-



<P>jects; for they are light to run away; and almost



<P>all fugitives, are of that condition.&nbsp; A single life



<P>doth well with churchmen; for charity will hardly



<P>water the ground, where it must first fill a pool.&nbsp; It



<P>is indifferent for judges and magistrates; for if



<P>they be facile and corrupt, you shall have a ser-



<P>vant, five times worse than a wife.&nbsp; For soldiers, I



<P>find the generals commonly in their hortatives,



<P>put men in mind of their wives and children; and



<P>I think the despising of marriage amongst the



<P>Turks, maketh the vulgar soldier more base.&nbsp; Cer-



<P>tainly wife and children are a kind of discipline



<P>of humanity; and single men, though they may



<P>be many times more charitable, because their



<P>means are less exhaust, yet, on the other side, they



<P>are more cruel and hardhearted (good to make



<P>severe inquisitors), because their tenderness is not



<P>so oft called upon.&nbsp; Grave natures, led by custom,



<P>and therefore constant, are commonly loving hus-



<P>bands, as was said of Ulysses, vetulam suam praetu-



<P>lit immortalitati.&nbsp; Chaste women are often proud



<P>and froward, as presuming upon the merit of their



<P>chastity.&nbsp; It is one of the best bonds, both of chastity



<P>and obedience, in the wife, if she think her hus-



<P>band wise; which she will never do, if she find him



<P>jealous.&nbsp; Wives are young men's mistresses; com-



<P>panions for middle age; and old men's nurses.&nbsp; So



<P>as a man may have a quarrel to marry, when he



<P>will.&nbsp; But yet he was reputed one of the wise men,



<P>that made answer to the question, when a man



<P>should marry, - A young man not yet, an elder



<P>man not at all.&nbsp; It is often seen that bad husbands,



<P>have very good wives; whether it be, that it raiseth



<P>the price of their husband's kindness, when it



<P>comes; or that the wives take a pride in their



<P>patience.&nbsp; But this never fails, if the bad husbands



<P>were of their own choosing, against their friends'



<P>consent; for then they will be sure to make good



<P>their own folly.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Parents AND CHILDREN</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Parents AND CHILDREN</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>THE joys of parents are secret; and so are their



<P>griefs and fears.&nbsp; They cannot utter the one;



<P>nor they will not utter the other.&nbsp; Children sweeten



<P>labors; but they make misfortunes more bitter.



<P>They increase the cares of life; but they mitigate



<P>the remembrance of death.&nbsp; The perpetuity by



<P>generation is common to beasts; but memory,



<P>merit, and noble works, are proper to men.&nbsp; And



<P>surely a man shall see the noblest works and foun-



<P>dations have proceeded from childless men; which



<P>have sought to express the images of their minds,



<P>where those of their bodies have failed.&nbsp; So the care



<P>of posterity is most in them, that have no posterity.



<P>They that are the first raisers of their houses, are



<P>most indulgent towards their children; beholding



<P>them as the continuance, not only of their kind, but



<P>of their work; and so both children and creatures.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The difference in affection, of parents towards



<P>their several children, is many times unequal; and



<P>sometimes unworthy; especially in the mothers;



<P>as Solomon saith, A wise son rejoiceth the father,



<P>but an ungracious son shames the mother.&nbsp; A man



<P>shall see, where there is a house full of children,



<P>one or two of the eldest respected, and the young-



<P>est made wantons; but in the midst, some that



<P>are as it were forgotten, who many times, never-



<P>theless, prove the best.&nbsp; The illiberality of parents,



<P>in allowance towards their children, is an harmful



<P>error; makes them base; acquaints them with



<P>shifts; makes them sort with mean company; and



<P>makes them surfeit more when they come to



<P>plenty.&nbsp; And therefore the proof is best, when men



<P>keep their authority towards the children, but not



<P>their purse.&nbsp; Men have a foolish manner (both par-



<P>ents and schoolmasters and servants) in creating



<P>and breeding an emulation between brothers, dur-



<P>ing childhood, which many times sorteth to dis-



<P>cord when they are men, and disturbeth families.



<P>The Italians make little difference between chil-



<P>dren, and nephews or near kinsfolks; but so they



<P>be of the lump, they care not though they pass not



<P>through their own body.&nbsp; And, to say truth, in



<P>nature it is much a like matter; insomuch that we



<P>see a nephew sometimes resembleth an uncle, or



<P>a kinsman, more than his own parent; as the blood



<P>happens.&nbsp; Let parents choose betimes, the vocations



<P>and courses they mean their children should take;



<P>for then they are most flexible; and let them not



<P>too much apply themselves to the disposition of



<P>their children, as thinking they will take best to
