<P>janizaries, and pretorian bands of Rome; but train-



<P>ings of men, and arming them in several places,



<P>and under several commanders, and without



<P>donatives, are things of defence, and no danger.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Princes are like to heavenly bodies, which cause



<P>good or evil times; and which have much venera-



<P>tion, but no rest.&nbsp; All precepts concerning kings,



<P>are in effect comprehended in those two remem-



<P>brances: memento quod es homo; and memento



<P>quod es Deus, or vice Dei; the one bridleth their



<P>power, and the other their will.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Riches</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Riches</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>I CANNOT call riches better than the baggage



<P>of virtue.&nbsp; The Roman word is better, impedi-



<P>menta.&nbsp; For as the baggage is to an army, so is riches



<P>to virtue.&nbsp; It cannot be spared, nor left behind, but



<P>it hindereth the march; yea, and the care of it,



<P>sometimes loseth or disturbeth the victory.&nbsp; Of



<P>great riches there is no real use, except it be in the



<P>distribution; the rest is but conceit.&nbsp; So saith Solo-



<P>mon, Where much is, there are many to consume



<P>it; and what hath the owner, but the sight of it



<P>with his eyes? The personal fruition in any man,



<P>cannot reach to feel great riches: there is a custody



<P>of them; or a power of dole, and donative of them;



<P>or a fame of them; but no solid use to the owner.



<P>Do you not see what feigned prices, are set upon



<P>little stones and rarities? and what works of osten-



<P>tation are undertaken, because there might seem



<P>to be some use of great riches? But then you will



<P>say, they may be of use, to buy men out of dangers



<P>or troubles.&nbsp; As Solomon saith, Riches are as a



<P>strong hold, in the imagination of the rich man.



<P>But this is excellently expressed, that it is in imagi-



<P>nation, and not always in fact.&nbsp; For certainly great



<P>riches, have sold more men, than they have bought



<P>out.&nbsp; Seek not proud riches, but such as thou mayest



<P>get justly, use soberly, distribute cheerfully, and



<P>leave contentedly.&nbsp; Yet have no abstract nor friarly



<P>contempt of them.&nbsp; But distinguish, as Cicero saith



<P>well of Rabirius Posthumus, In studio rei ampli-



<P>ficandae apparebat, non avaritiae praedam, sed



<P>instrumentum bonitati quaeri.&nbsp; Harken also to



<P>Solomon, and beware of hasty gathering of riches;



<P>Qui festinat ad divitias, non erit insons.&nbsp; The poets



<P>feign, that when Plutus (which is Riches) is sent



<P>from Jupiter, he limps and goes slowly; but when



<P>he is sent from Pluto, he runs, and is swift of foot.



<P>Meaning that riches gotten by good means, and



<P>just labor, pace slowly; but when they come by



<P>the death of others (as by the course of inheritance,



<P>testaments, and the like), they come tumbling



<P>upon a man.&nbsp; But it mought be applied likewise to



<P>Pluto, taking him for the devil.&nbsp; For when riches



<P>come from the devil (as by fraud and oppression,



<P>and unjust means), they come upon speed.&nbsp; The



<P>ways to enrich are many, and most of them foul.



<P>Parsimony is one of the best, and yet is not inno-



<P>cent; for it withholdeth men from works of liberal-



<P>ity and charity.&nbsp; The improvement of the ground,



<P>is the most natural obtaining of riches; for it is our



<P>great mother's blessing, the earth's; but it is slow.



<P>And yet where men of great wealth do stoop to



<P>husbandry, it multiplieth riches exceedingly.&nbsp; I



<P>knew a nobleman in England, that had the great-



<P>est audits of any man in my time; a great grazier,



<P>a great sheep-master, a great timber man, a great



<P>collier, a great corn-master, a great lead-man, and



<P>so of iron, and a number of the like points of hus-



<P>bandry.&nbsp; So as the earth seemed a sea to him, in



<P>respect of the perpetual importation.&nbsp; It was truly



<P>observed by one, that himself came very hardly,



<P>to a little riches, and very easily, to great riches.



<P>For when a man's stock is come to that, that he can



<P>expect the prime of markets, and overcome those



<P>bargains, which for their greatness are few men's



<P>money, and be partner in the industries of younger



<P>men, he cannot but increase mainly.&nbsp; The gains of



<P>ordinary trades and vocations are honest; and



<P>furthered by two things chiefly: by diligence, and



<P>by a good name, for good and fair dealing.&nbsp; But the



<P>gains of bargains, are of a more doubtful nature;



<P>when men shall wait upon others' necessity, broke



<P>by servants and instruments to draw them on, put



<P>off others cunningly, that would be better chap-



<P>men, and the like practices, which are crafty and



<P>naught.&nbsp; As for the chopping of bargains, when a



<P>man buys not to hold but to sell over again, that



<P>commonly grindeth double, both upon the seller,



<P>and upon the buyer.&nbsp; Sharings do greatly enrich,



<P>if the hands be well chosen, that are trusted.&nbsp; Usury



<P>is the certainest means of gain, though one of the



<P>worst; as that whereby a man doth eat his bread,



<P>in sudore vultus alieni; and besides, doth plough



<P>upon Sundays.&nbsp; But yet certain though it be, it hath



<P>flaws; for that the scriveners and brokers do value



<P>unsound men, to serve their own turn.&nbsp; The fortune



<P>in being the first, in an invention or in a privilege,



<P>doth cause sometimes a wonderful overgrowth in



<P>riches; as it was with the first sugar man, in the



<P>Canaries.&nbsp; Therefore if a man can play the true



<P>logician, to have as well judgment, as invention,



<P>he may do great matters; especially if the times be



<P>fit.&nbsp; He that resteth upon gains certain, shall hardly



<P>grow to great riches; and he that puts all upon



<P>adventures, doth oftentimes break and come to



<P>poverty: it is good, therefore, to guard adventures



<P>with certainties, that may uphold losses.&nbsp; Monopo-



<P>lies, and coemption of wares for re-sale, where



<P>they are not restrained, are great means to enrich;



<P>especially if the party have intelligence, what



<P>things are like to come into request, and so store



<P>himself beforehand.&nbsp; Riches gotten by service,



<P>though it be of the best rise, yet when they are



<P>gotten by flattery, feeding humors, and other serv-



<P>ile conditions, they may be placed amongst the



<P>worst.&nbsp; As for fishing for testaments and executor-



<P>ships (as Tacitus saith of Seneca, testamenta et



<P>orbos tamquam indagine capi), it is yet worse; by



<P>how much men submit themselves to meaner per-



<P>sons, than in service.&nbsp; Believe not much, them that



<P>seem to despise riches; for they despise them, that



<P>despair of them; and none worse, when they come



<P>to them.&nbsp; Be not penny-wise; riches have wings,



<P>and sometimes they fly away of themselves, some-



<P>times they must be set flying, to bring in more.



<P>Men leave their riches, either to their kindred, or



<P>to the public; and moderate portions, prosper best



<P>in both.&nbsp; A great state left to an heir, is as a lure to



<P>all the birds of prey round about, to seize on him, if



<P>he be not the better stablished in years and judg-



<P>ment.&nbsp; Likewise glorious gifts and foundations, are



<P>like sacrifices without salt; and but the painted



<P>sepulchres of alms, which soon will putrefy, and



<P>corrupt inwardly.&nbsp; Therefore measure not thine



<P>advancements, by quantity, but frame them by



<P>measure: and defer not charities till death; for,



<P>certainly, if a man weigh it rightly, he that doth



<P>so, is rather liberal of another man's, than of his



<P>own.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Dispatch</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Dispatch</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>AFFECTED dispatch is one of the most danger-



<P>ous things to business that can be.&nbsp; It is like



<P>that, which the physicians call predigestion, or



<P>hasty digestion; which is sure to fill the body full of



<P>crudities, and secret seeds of diseases.&nbsp; Therefore



<P>measure not dispatch, by the times of sitting, but



<P>by the advancement of the business.&nbsp; And as in



<P>races it is not the large stride or high lift that makes



<P>the speed; so in business, the keeping close to the



<P>matter, and not taking of it too much at once, pro-



<P>cureth dispatch.&nbsp; It is the care of some, only to come



<P>off speedily for the time; or to contrive some false



<P>periods of business, because they may seem men



<P>of dispatch.&nbsp; But it is one thing, to abbreviate by



<P>contracting, another by cutting off . And business



<P>so handled, at several sittings or meetings, goeth



<P>commonly backward and forward in an unsteady



<P>manner.&nbsp; I knew a wise man that had it for a by-



<P>word, when he saw men hasten to a conclusion,



<P>Stay a little, that we may&nbsp; make&nbsp; an&nbsp; end&nbsp; the&nbsp;

sooner.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>On the other side, true dispatch is a rich thing.



<P>For time is the measure of business, as money is



<P>of wares; and business is bought at a dear hand,



<P>where there is small dispatch.&nbsp; The Spartans and



<P>Spaniards have been noted to be of small dispatch;



<P>Mi venga la muerte de Spagna; Let my death come



<P>from Spain; for then it will be sure to be long in



<P>coming.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Give good hearing to those, that give the first



<P>information in business; and rather direct them



<P>in the beginning, than interrupt them in the con-



<P>tinuance of their speeches; for he that is put out of



<P>his own order, will go forward and backward, and



<P>be more tedious, while he waits upon his memory,



<P>than he could have been, if he had gone on in his



<P>own course.&nbsp; But sometimes it is seen, that the



<P>moderator is more troublesome, than the actor.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Iterations are commonly loss of time.&nbsp; But there



<P>is no such gain of time, as to iterate often the state



<P>of the question; for it chaseth away many a frivo-



<P>lous speech, as it is coming forth.&nbsp; Long and curious



<P>speeches, are as fit for dispatch, as a robe or mantle,



<P>with a long train, is for race.&nbsp; Prefaces and pas-



<P>sages, and excusations, and other speeches of refer-



<P>ence to the person, are great wastes of time; and



<P>though they seem to proceed of modesty, they are



<P>bravery.&nbsp; Yet beware of being too material, when



<P>there is any impediment or obstruction in men's



<P>wills; for pre-occupation of mind ever requireth



<P>preface of speech; like a fomentation to make the



<P>unguent enter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Above all things, order, and distribution, and



<P>singling out of parts, is the life of dispatch; so as the



<P>distribution be not too subtle: for he that doth not



<P>divide, will never enter well into business; and he



<P>that divideth too much, will never come out of it



<P>clearly.&nbsp; To choose time, is to save time; and an un-



<P>seasonable motion, is but beating the air.&nbsp; There be



<P>three parts of business; the preparation, the debate



<P>or examination, and the perfection.&nbsp; Whereof, if



<P>you look for dispatch, let the middle only be the



<P>work of many, and the first and last the work of



<P>few.&nbsp; The proceeding upon somewhat conceived in



<P>writing, doth for the most part facilitate dispatch:



<P>for though it should be wholly rejected, yet that



<P>negative is more pregnant of direction, than an



<P>indefinite; as ashes are more generative than dust.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>of revenge</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Revenge</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>REVENGE is a kind of wild justice; which the



<P>more man' s nature runs to, the more ought



<P>law to weed it out.&nbsp; For as for the first wrong, it



<P>doth but offend the law; but the revenge of that



<P>wrong, putteth the law out of office.&nbsp; Certainly, in



<P>taking revenge, a man is but even with his enemy;



<P>but in passing it over, he is superior; for it is a



<P>prince's part to pardon.&nbsp; And Solomon, I am sure,



<P>saith, It is the glory of a man, to pass by an offence.



<P>That which is past is gone, and irrevocable; and



<P>wise men have enough to do, with things present



<P>and to come; therefore they do but trifle with



<P>themselves, that labor in past matters.&nbsp; There is no



<P>man doth a wrong, for the wrong's sake; but



<P>thereby to purchase himself profit, or pleasure, or



<P>honor, or the like.&nbsp; Therefore why should I be



<P>angry with a man, for loving himself better than



<P>me? And if any man should do wrong, merely out



<P>of ill-nature, why, yet it is but like the thorn or



<P>briar, which prick and scratch, because they can



<P>do no other.&nbsp; The most tolerable sort of revenge, is



<P>for those wrongs which there is no law to remedy;



<P>but then let a man take heed, the revenge be such



<P>as there is no law to punish; else a man's enemy is



<P>still before hand, and it is two for one.&nbsp; Some, when



<P>they take revenge, are desirous, the party should



<P>know, whence it cometh.&nbsp; This is the more gener-



<P>ous.&nbsp; For the delight seemeth to be, not so much in



<P>doing the hurt, as in making the party repent.&nbsp; But



<P>base and crafty cowards, are like the arrow that



<P>flieth in the dark.&nbsp; Cosmus, duke of Florence, had a



<P>desperate saying against perfidious or neglecting



<P>friends, as if those wrongs were unpardonable;



<P>You shall read (saith he) that we are commanded



<P>to forgive our enemies; but you never read, that we



<P>are commanded to forgive our friends.&nbsp; But yet the



<P>spirit of Job was in a better tune: Shall we (saith



<P>he) take good at God's hands, and not be content to



<P>take evil also? And so of friends in a proportion.



<P>This is certain, that a man that studieth revenge,



<P>keeps his own wounds green, which otherwise



<P>would heal, and do well.&nbsp; Public revenges are for



<P>the most part fortunate; as that for the death of



<P>Caesar; for the death of Pertinax; for the death of



<P>Henry the Third of France; and many more.&nbsp; But



<P>in private revenges, it is not so.&nbsp; Nay rather, vindic-



<P>tive persons live the life of witches; who, as they



<P>are mischievous, so end they infortunate.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of the True GREATNESS OF KINGDOMS AND ESTATES</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of the True GREATNESS OF KINGDOMS AND ESTATES</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE speech of Themistocles the Athenian,



<P>which was haughty and arrogant, in taking



<P>so much to himself, had been a grave and wise



<P>observation and censure, applied at large to others.



<P>Desired at a feast to touch a lute, he said, He could



<P>not fiddle, but yet he could make a small town, a



<P>great city.&nbsp; These words (holpen a little with a



<P>metaphor) may express two differing abilities, in



<P>those that deal in business of estate.&nbsp; For if a true



<P>survey be taken of counsellors and statesmen,



<P>there may be found (though rarely) those which



<P>can make a small state great, and yet cannot fid-



<P>dle; as on the other side, there will be found a great



<P>many, that can fiddle very cunningly, but yet are



<P>so far from being able to make a small state great,



<P>as their gift lieth the other way; to bring a great



<P>and flourishing estate, to ruin and decay.&nbsp; And cer-



<P>tainly whose degenerate arts and shifts, whereby



<P>many counsellors and governors gain both favor



<P>with their masters, and estimation with the vulgar,



<P>deserve no better name than fiddling; being things



<P>rather pleasing for the time, and graceful to them-



<P>selves only, than tending to the weal and advance-



<P>ment of the state which they serve.&nbsp; There are also



<P>(no doubt) counsellors and governors which may



<P>be held sufficient (negotiis pares), able to manage



<P>affairs, and to keep them from precipices and



<P>manifest inconveniences; which nevertheless are



<P>far from the ability to raise and amplify an estate



<P>in power, means, and fortune.&nbsp; But be the workmen



<P>what they may be, let us speak of the work; that



<P>is, the true greatness of kingdoms and estates, and



<P>the means thereof.&nbsp; An argument fit for great and



<P>mighty princes to have in their hand; to the end



<P>that neither by over-measuring their forces, they



<P>leese themselves in vain enterprises; nor on the



<P>other side, by undervaluing them, they descend to



<P>fearful and pusillanimous counsels.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The greatness of an estate, in bulk and territory,



<P>doth fall under measure; and the greatness of



<P>finances and revenue, doth fall under computa-



<P>tion.&nbsp; The population may appear by musters; and



<P>the number and greatness of cities and towns by



<P>cards and maps.&nbsp; But yet there is not any thing



<P>amongst civil affairs more subject to error, than



<P>the right valuation and true judgment concerning



<P>the power and forces of an estate.&nbsp; The kingdom of



<P>heaven is compared, not to any great kernel or nut,



<P>but to a grain of mustard-seed: which is one of the



<P>least grains, but hath in it a property and spirit



<P>hastily to get up and spread.&nbsp; So are there states,



<P>great in territory, and yet not apt to enlarge or



<P>command; and some that have but a small dimen-



<P>sion of stem, and yet apt to be the foundations of



<P>great monarchies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Walled towns, stored arsenals and armories,



<P>goodly races of horse, chariots of war, elephants,



<P>ordnance, artillery, and the like; all this is but a



<P>sheep in a lion's skin, except the breed and disposi-



<P>tion of the people, be stout and warlike.&nbsp; Nay, num-



<P>ber (itself) in armies importeth not much, where



<P>the people is of weak courage; for (as Virgil saith)



<P>It never troubles a wolf, how many the sheep be.



<P>The army of the Persians, in the plains of Arbela,



<P>was such a vast sea of people, as it did somewhat



<P>astonish the commanders in Alexander's army;



<P>who came to him therefore, and wished him to set



<P>upon them by night; and he answered, He would



<P>not pilfer the victory.&nbsp; And the defeat was easy.



<P>When Tigranes the Armenian, being encamped



<P>upon a hill with four hundred thousand men, dis-



<P>covered the army of the Romans, being not above



<P>fourteen thousand, marching towards him, he



<P>made himself merry with it, and said, Yonder men



<P>are too many for an embassage, and too few for a



<P>fight.&nbsp; But before the sun set, he found them enow



<P>to give him the chase with infinite slaughter.



<P>Many are the examples of the great odds, between



<P>number and courage; so that a man may truly



<P>make a judgment, that the principal point of great-



<P>ness in any state, is to have a race of military men.



<P>Neither is money the sinews of war (as it is trivially



<P>said), where the sinews of men's arms, in base and



<P>effeminate people, are failing.&nbsp; For Solon said well



<P>to Croesus (when in ostentation he showed him his



<P>gold), Sir, if any other come, that hath better iron,



<P>than you, he will be master of all this gold.&nbsp; There-



<P>fore let any prince or state think solely of his forces,



<P>except his militia of natives be of good and valiant



<P>soldiers.&nbsp; And let princes, on the other side, that



<P>have subjects of martial disposition, know their



<P>own strength; unless they be otherwise wanting



<P>unto themselves.&nbsp; As for mercenary forces (which



<P>is the help in this case), all examples show, that



<P>whatsoever estate or prince doth rest upon them,



<P>he may spread his feathers for a time, but he will



<P>mew them soon after.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The blessing of Judah and Issachar will never



<P>meet; that the same people, or nation, should be



<P>both the lion's whelp and the ass between bur-



<P>thens; neither will it be, that a people overlaid



<P>with taxes, should ever become valiant and mar-



<P>tial.&nbsp; It is true that taxes levied by consent of the



<P>estate, do abate men's courage less: as it hath been



<P>seen notably, in the excises of the Low Countries;



<P>and, in some degree, in the subsidies of England.



<P>For you must note, that we speak now of the heart,



<P>and not of the purse.&nbsp; So that although the same



<P>tribute and tax, laid by consent or by imposing, be



<P>all one to the purse, yet it works diversely upon the



<P>courage.&nbsp; So that you may conclude, that no people



<P>overcharged with tribute, is fit for empire.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Let states that aim at greatness, take heed how



<P>their nobility and gentlemen do multiply too fast.



<P>For that maketh the common subject, grow to be a



<P>peasant and base swain, driven out of heart, and in



<P>effect but the gentleman's laborer.&nbsp; Even as you



<P>may see in coppice woods; if you leave your stad-



<P>dles too thick, you shall never have clean under-



<P>wood, but shrubs and bushes.&nbsp; So in countries,&nbsp; if the



<P>gentlemen be too many, the commons will be base;



<P>and you will bring it to that, that not the hundred



<P>poll, will be fit for an helmet; especially as to the



<P>infantry, which is the nerve of an army; and so



<P>there will be great population, and little strength.



<P>This which I speak of, hath been nowhere better



<P>seen, than by comparing of England and France;



<P>whereof England, though far less in territory and



<P>population, hath been (nevertheless) an over-



<P>match; in regard the middle people of England



<P>make good soldiers, which the peasants of France



<P>do not.&nbsp; And herein the device of king Henry the



<P>Seventh (whereof I have spoken largely in the



<P>History of his Life) was profound and admirable;



<P>in making farms and houses of husbandry of a



<P>standard; that is, maintained with such a propor-



<P>tion of land unto them, as may breed a subject to



<P>live in convenient plenty and no servile condition;



<P>and to keep the plough in the hands of the owners,



<P>and not mere hirelings.&nbsp; And thus indeed you shall



<P>attain to Virgil's character which he gives to an-



<P>cient Italy:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Terra potens armis atque ubere glebae.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Neither is that state (which, for any thing I know,



<P>is almost peculiar to England, and hardly to be



<P>found anywhere else, except it be perhaps in



<P>Poland) to be passed over; I mean the state of free



<P>servants, and attendants upon noblemen and



<P>gentlemen; which are no ways inferior unto the



<P>yeomanry for arms.&nbsp; And therefore out of all ques-



<P>tions, the splendor and magnificence, and great



<P>retinues and hospitality, of noblemen and gentle-



<P>men, received into custom, doth much conduce



<P>unto martial greatness.&nbsp; Whereas, contrariwise, the



<P>close and reserved living of noblemen and gentle-



<P>men, causeth a penury of military forces.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>By all means it is to be procured, that the trunk



<P>of Nebuchadnezzar's tree of monarchy, be great



<P>enough to bear the branches and the boughs; that



<P>is, that the natural subjects of the crown or state,



<P>bear a sufficient proportion to the stranger sub-



<P>jects, that they govern.Therefore all states that are



<P>liberal of naturalization towards strangers, are fit



<P>for empire.&nbsp; For to think that an handful of people



<P>can, with the greatest courage and policy in the



<P>world, embrace too large extent of dominion, it



<P>may hold for a time, but it will fail suddenly.&nbsp; The



<P>Spartans were a nice people in point of naturaliza-



<P>tion; whereby, while they kept their compass,



<P>they stood firm; but when they did spread, and



<P>their boughs were becomen too great for their



<P>stem, they became a windfall, upon the sudden.



<P>Never any state was in this point so open to receive



<P>strangers into their body, as were the Romans.



<P>Therefore it sorted with them accordingly; for



<P>they grew to the greatest monarchy.&nbsp; Their manner



<P>was to grant naturalization (which they called jus



<P>civitatis), and to grant it in the highest degree; that



<P>is, not only jus commercii, jus connubii, jus haere-



<P>ditatis; but also jus suffragii, and jus honorum.



<P>And this not to singular persons alone, but likewise



<P>to whole families; yea to cities, and sometimes to



<P>nations.&nbsp; Add to this their custom of plantation of



<P>colonies; whereby the Roman plant was removed



<P>into the soil of other nations.&nbsp; And putting both



<P>constitutions together, you will say that it was not



<P>the Romans that spread upon the world, but it was



<P>the world that spread upon the Romans; and that



<P>was the sure way of greatness.&nbsp; I have marvelled,



<P>sometimes, at Spain, how they clasp and contain



<P>so large dominions, with so few natural Spaniards;



<P>but sure the whole compass of Spain, is a very great



<P>body of a tree; far above Rome and Sparta at the



<P>first.&nbsp; And besides, though they have not had that



<P>usage, to naturalize liberally, yet they have that



<P>which is next to it; that is, to employ, almost indif-



<P>ferently, all nations in their militia of ordinary



<P>soldiers; yea, and sometimes in their highest com-



<P>mands.&nbsp; Nay, it seemeth at this instant they are



<P>sensible, of this want of natives; as by the Prag-



<P>matical Sanction, now published, appeareth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is certain that sedentary, and within-door



<P>arts, and delicate manufactures (that require



<P>rather the finger than the arm), have, in their na-



<P>ture, a contrariety to a military disposition.&nbsp; And



<P>generally, all warlike people are a little idle, and



<P>love danger better than travail.&nbsp; Neither must they



<P>be too much broken of it, if they shall be preserved



<P>in vigor.&nbsp; Therefore it was great advantage, in the



<P>ancient states of Sparta, Athens, Rome, and others,



<P>that they had the use of slaves, which commonly



<P>did rid those manufactures.&nbsp; But that is abolished,



<P>in greatest part, by the Christian law.&nbsp; That which



<P>cometh nearest to it, is to leave those arts chiefly to



<P>strangers (which, for that purpose, are the more



<P>easily to be received), and to contain the principal



<P>bulk of the vulgar natives, within those three



<P>kinds, - tillers of the ground; free servants; and



<P>handicraftsmen of strong and manly arts, as



<P>smiths, masons, carpenters, etc.; not reckoning



<P>professed soldiers.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>But above all, for empire and greatness, it im-



<P>porteth most, that a nation do profess arms, as their



<P>principal honor, study, and occupation.&nbsp; For the



<P>things which we formerly have spoken of, are but



<P>habilitations towards arms; and what is habilita-



<P>tion without intention and act? Romulus, after his



<P>death (as they report or feign), sent a present to the



<P>Romans, that above all, they should intend arms;



<P>and then they should prove the greatest empire of



<P>the world.&nbsp; The fabric of the state of Sparta was



<P>wholly (though not wisely) framed and composed,



<P>to that scope and end.&nbsp; The Persians and Macedo-



<P>nians had it for a flash.&nbsp; The Gauls, Germans,



<P>Goths, Saxons, Normans, and others, had it for a



<P>time.&nbsp; The Turks have it at this day, though in great



<P>declination.&nbsp; Of Christian Europe, they that have it



<P>are, in effect, only the Spaniards.&nbsp; But it is so



<P>plain, that every man profiteth in that, he most



<P>intendeth, that it needeth not to be stood upon.&nbsp; It



<P>is enough to point at it; that no nation which doth



<P>not directly profess arms, may look to have great-



<P>ness fall into their mouths.&nbsp; And on the other side,



<P>it is a most certain oracle of time, that those states



<P>that continue long in that profession (as the Ro-



<P>mans and Turks principally have done) do won-



<P>ders.&nbsp; And those that have professed arms but for



<P>an age, have, notwithstanding, commonly at-



<P>tained that greatness, in that age, which main-



<P>tained them long after, when their profession and



<P>exercise of arms hath grown to decay.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Incident to this point is, for a state to have those



<P>laws or customs, which may reach forth unto them



<P>just occasions (as may be pretended) of war.&nbsp; For



<P>there is that justice, imprinted in the nature of



<P>men, that they enter not upon wars (whereof so



<P>many calamities do ensue) but upon some, at the



<P>least specious, grounds and quarrels.&nbsp; The Turk



<P>hath at hand, for cause of war, the propagation of



<P>his law or sect; a quarrel that he may always com-



<P>mand.&nbsp; The Romans, though they esteemed the



<P>extending the limits of their empire, to be great



<P>honor to their generals, when it was done, yet they



<P>never rested upon that alone, to begin a war.&nbsp; First,



<P>therefore, let nations that pretend to greatness



<P>have this; that they be sensible of wrongs, either



<P>upon borderers, merchants, or politic ministers;



<P>and that they sit not too long upon a provocation.



<P>Secondly, let them be prest, and ready to give aids



<P>and succors, to their confederates; as it ever was



<P>with the Romans; insomuch, as if the confederate



<P>had leagues defensive, with divers other states,



<P>and, upon invasion offered, did implore their aids



<P>severally, yet the Romans would ever be the fore-



<P>most, and leave it to none other to have the honor.



<P>As for the wars which were anciently made, on



<P>the behalf of a kind of party, or tacit conformity of



<P>estate, I do not see how they may be well justified:



<P>as when the Romans made a war, for the liberty of



<P>Grecia; or when the Lacedaemonians and Athe-



<P>nians, made wars to set up or pull down democ-



<P>racies and oligarchies; or when wars were made



<P>by foreigners, under the pretence of justice or pro-



<P>tection, to deliver the subjects of others, from



<P>tyranny and oppression; and the like.&nbsp; Let it suf-



<P>fice, that no estate expect to be great, that is not



<P>awake upon any just occasion of arming.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>No body can be healthful without exercise,



<P>neither natural body nor politic; and certainly to



<P>a kingdom or estate, a just and honorable war, is



<P>the true exercise.&nbsp; A civil war, indeed, is like the



<P>heat of a fever; but a foreign war is like the heat of



<P>exercise, and serveth to keep the body in health;



<P>for in a slothful peace, both courages will effemi-



<P>nate, and manners corrupt.&nbsp; But howsoever it be



<P>for happiness, without all question, for greatness,



<P>it maketh to be still for the most part in arms; and



<P>the strength of a veteran army (though it be a



<P>chargeable business) always on foot, is that which



<P>commonly giveth the law, or at least the reputa-



<P>tion, amongst all neighbor states; as may well be



<P>seen in Spain, which hath had, in one part or other,



<P>a veteran army almost continually, now by the



<P>space of six score years.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To be master of the sea, is an abridgment of a



<P>monarchy.&nbsp; Cicero, writing to Atticus of Pompey



<P>his preparation against Caesar, saith, Consilium



<P>Pompeii plane Themistocleum est; putat enim,



<P>qui mari potitur, eum rerum potiri.&nbsp; And, without



<P>doubt, Pompey had tired out Caesar, if upon vain



<P>confidence, he had not left that way.&nbsp; We see the



<P>great effects of battles bv sea.&nbsp; The battle of Actium,



<P>decided the empire of the world.&nbsp; The battle of Le-



<P>panto, arrested the greatness of the Turk.&nbsp; There be



<P>many examples, where sea-fights have been final



<P>to the war; but this is when princes or states have



<P>set up their rest, upon the battles.&nbsp; But thus much



<P>is certain, that he that commands the sea, is at



<P>great liberty, and may take as much, and as little,



<P>of the war as he will.&nbsp; Whereas those that be strong-



<P>est by land, are many times nevertheless in great



<P>straits.&nbsp; Surely, at this day, with us of Europe, the



<P>vantage of strength at sea (which is one of the prin-



<P>cipal dowries of this kingdom of Great Britain) is



<P>great; both because most of the kingdoms of Eu-



<P>rope, are not merely inland, but girt with the sea



<P>most part of their compass; and because the wealth



<P>of both Indies seems in great part, but an accessory



<P>to the command of the seas.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The wars of latter ages seem to be made in the



<P>dark, in respect of the glory, and honor, which



<P>reflected upon men from the wars, in ancient time.



<P>There be now, for martial encouragement, some



<P>degrees and orders of chivalry; which nevertheless



<P>are conferred promiscuously, upon soldiers and



<P>no soldiers; and some remembrance perhaps, upon



<P>the scutcheon; and some hospitals for maimed sol-



<P>diers; and such like things.&nbsp; But in ancient times,



<P>the trophies erected upon the place of the victory;



<P>the funeral laudatives and monuments for those



<P>that died in the wars; the crowns and garlands per-



<P>sonal; the style of emperor, which the great kings



<P>of the world after borrowed; the triumphs of the



<P>generals, upon their return; the great donatives



<P>and largesses, upon the disbanding of the armies;



<P>were things able to inflame all men's courages.



<P>But above all, that of the triumph, amongst the



<P>Romans, was not pageants or gaudery, but one of



<P>the wisest and noblest institutions, that ever was.



<P>For it contained three things: honor to the general;



<P>riches to the treasury out of the spoils; and dona-



<P>tives to the army.&nbsp; But that honor, perhaps were not



<P>fit for monarchies; except it be in the person of the



<P>monarch himself, or his sons; as it came to pass in



<P>the times of the Roman emperors, who did impro-



<P>priate the actual triumphs to themselves, and their



<P>sons, for such wars as they did achieve in person;



<P>and left only, for wars achieved by subjects, some



<P>triumphal garments and ensigns to the general.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To conclude: no man can by care taking (as the



<P>Scripture saith) add a cubit to his stature, in this



<P>little model of a man's body; but in the great frame



<P>of kingdoms and commonwealths, it is in the



<P>power of princes or estates, to add amplitude and



<P>greatness to their kingdoms; for by introducing



<P>such ordinances, constitutions, and customs, as we



<P>have now touched, they may sow greatness to



<P>their posterity and succession.&nbsp; But these things are



<P>commonly not observed, but left to take their



<P>chance.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Counsel</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Counsel</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

<BR>THE greatest trust, between man and man, is



<P>the trust of giving counsel.&nbsp; For in other con-



<P>fidences, men commit the parts of life; their lands,



<P>their goods, their children, their credit, some par-



<P>ticular affair; but to such as they make their coun-



<P>sellors, they commit the whole: by how much the



<P>more, they are obliged to all faith and integrity.



<P>The wisest princes need not think it any diminu-



<P>tion to their greatness, or derogation to their suf-



<P>ficiency, to rely upon counsel.&nbsp; God himself is not



<P>without, but hath made it one of the great names



<P>of his blessed Son: The Counsellor.&nbsp; Solomon hath



<P>pronounced, that in counsel is stability.&nbsp; Things



<P>will have their first, or second agitation: if they be



<P>not tossed upon the arguments of counsel, they



<P>will be tossed upon the waves of fortune; and be



<P>full of inconstancy, doing and undoing, like the



<P>reeling of a drunken man.&nbsp; Solomon's son found



<P>the force of counsel, as his father saw the necessity



<P>of it.&nbsp; For the beloved kingdom of God, was first



<P>rent, and broken, by ill counsel; upon which coun-



<P>sel, there are set for our instruction, the two marks



<P>whereby bad counsel is for ever best discerned;



<P>that it was young counsel, for the person; and



<P>violent counsel, for the matter.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The ancient times, do set forth in figure, both



<P>the incorporation, and inseparable conjunction, of



<P>counsel with kings, and the wise and politic use of



<P>counsel by kings: the one, in that they say Jupi-



<P>ter did marry Metis, which signifieth counsel;



<P>whereby they intend that Sovereignty, is married



<P>to Counsel: the other in that which followeth,



<P>which was thus: They say, after Jupiter was mar-



<P>ried to Metis, she conceived by him, and was with



<P>child, but Jupiter suffered her not to stay, till she



<P>brought forth, but eat her up; whereby he became



<P>himself with child, and was delivered of Pallas



<P>armed, out of his head.&nbsp; Which monstrous fable



<P>containeth a secret of empire; how kings are to



<P>make use of their counsel of state.&nbsp; That first, they



<P>ought to refer matters unto them, which is the first



<P>begetting, or impregnation; but when they are



<P>elaborate, moulded, and shaped in the womb of



<P>their counsel, and grow ripe, and ready to be



<P>brought forth, that then they suffer not their coun-



<P>sel to go through with the resolution and direc-



<P>tion, as if it depended on them; but take the matter



<P>back into their own hands, and make it appear to



<P>the world, that the decrees and final directions



<P>(which, because they come forth, with prudence



<P>and power, are resembled to Pallas armed) pro-



<P>ceeded from themselves; and not only from their



<P>authority, but (the more to add reputation to them-



<P>selves) from their head and device.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Let us now speak of the inconveniences of coun-



<P>sel, and of the remedies.&nbsp; The inconveniences that



<P>have been noted, in calling and using counsel, are



<P>three.&nbsp; First, the revealing of affairs, whereby they



<P>become less secret.&nbsp; Secondly, the weakening of the



<P>authority of princes, as if they were less of them-



<P>selves.&nbsp; Thirdly, the danger of being unfaithfully



<P>counselled, and more for the good of them that



<P>counsel, than of him that is counselled.&nbsp; For which



<P>inconveniences, the doctrine of Italy, and practice



<P>of France, in some kings' times, hath introduced



<P>cabinet counsels; a remedy worse than the disease.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As to secrecy; princes are not bound to commu-



<P>nicate all matters, with all counsellors; but may



<P>extract and select.&nbsp; Neither is it necessary, that he



<P>that consulteth what he should do, should declare



<P>what he will do.&nbsp; But let princes beware, that the



<P>unsecreting of their affairs, comes not from them-



<P>selves.&nbsp; And as for cabinet counsels, it may be their



<P>motto, plenus rimarum sum: one futile person,



<P>that maketh it his glory to tell, will do more hurt



<P>than many, that know it their duty to conceal.&nbsp; It is



<P>true there be some affairs, which require extreme



<P>secrecy, which will hardly go beyond one or two



<P>persons, besides the king: neither are those coun-



<P>sels unprosperous; for, besides the secrecy, they



<P>conunonly go on constantly, in one spirit of direc-



<P>tion, without distraction.&nbsp; But then it must be a



<P>prudent king, such as is able to grind with a hand-



<P>mill; and those inward counsellors had need also



<P>be wise men, and especially true and trusty to the



<P>king's ends; as it was with King Henry the Seventh



<P>of England, who, in his great business, imparted



<P>himself to none, except it were to Morton and Fox.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For weakening of authority; the fable showeth



<P>the remedy.&nbsp; Nay, the majesty of kings, is rather



<P>exalted than diminished, when they are in the



<P>chair of counsel; neither was there ever prince, be-



<P>reaved of his dependences, by his counsel, except



<P>where there hath been, either an over-greatness



<P>in one counsellor, or an over-strict combination in



<P>divers; which are things soon found, and holpen.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the last inconvenience, that men will coun-



<P>sel, with an eye to themselves; certainly, non



<P>inveniet fidem super terram is meant, of the na-



<P>ture of times, and not of all particular persons.



<P>There be, that are in nature faithful, and sincere,



<P>and plain, and direct; not crafty and involved; let



<P>princes, above all, draw to themselves such na-



<P>tures.&nbsp; Besides, counsellors are not commonly so



<P>united, but that one counsellor, keepeth sentinel



<P>over another; so that if any do counsel out of fac-



<P>tion or private ends, it commonly comes to the



<P>king's ear.&nbsp; But the best remedy is, if princes know



<P>their counsellors, as well as their counsellors



<P>know them:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Principis est virtus maxima nosse suos.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>And on the other side, counsellors should not be



<P>too speculative into their sovereign's person.&nbsp; The



<P>true composition of a counsellor, is rather to be



<P>skilful in their master's business, than in his na-



<P>ture; for then he is like to advise him, and not feed



<P>his humor.&nbsp; It is of singular use to princes, if they



<P>take the opinions of their counsel, both separately



<P>and together.&nbsp; For private opinion is more free;



<P>but opinion before others, is more reverent.&nbsp; In



<P>private, men are more bold in their own humors;



<P>and in consort, men are more obnoxious to others'



<P>humors; therefore it is good to take both; and of



<P>the inferior sort, rather in private, to preserve free-



<P>dom; of the greater, rather in consort, to preserve



<P>respect.&nbsp; It is in vain for princes, to take counsel



<P>concerning matters, if they take no counsel like-



<P>wise concerning persons; for all matters are as



<P>dead images; and the life of the execution of af-



<P>fairs, resteth in the good choice of persons.&nbsp; Neither



<P>is it enough, to consult concerning persons secun-



<P>dum genera, as in an idea, or mathematical de-



<P>scription, what the kind and character of the



<P>person should be; for the greatest errors are com-



<P>mitted, and the most judgment is shown, in the



<P>choice of individuals.&nbsp; It was truly said, optimi con-



<P>siliarii mortui: books will speak plain, when coun-



<P>sellors blanch.Therefore it is good to be conversant



<P>in them, specially the books of such as themselves



<P>have been actors upon the stage.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The counsels at this day, in most places, are but



<P>familiar meetings, where matters are rather talked



<P>on, than debated.&nbsp; And they run too swift, to the



<P>order, or act, of counsel.&nbsp; It were better that in



<P>causes of weight, the matter were propounded one



<P>day, and not spoken to till the next day; in nocte



<P>consilium.&nbsp; So was it done in the Commission of



<P>Union, between England and Scotland; which



<P>was a grave and orderly assembly.&nbsp; I commend set



<P>days for petitions; for both it gives the sudtors more



<P>certainty for their attendance, and it frees the



<P>meetings for matters of estate, that they may hoc



<P>agere.&nbsp; In choice of committees; for ripening busi-



<P>ness for the counsel, it is better to choose indifferent



<P>persons, than to make an indifferency, by putting



<P>in those, that are strong on both sides.&nbsp; I commend



<P>also standing commissions; as for trade, for treas-



<P>ure, for war, for suits, for some provinces; for



<P>where there be divers particular counsels, and but



<P>one counsel of estate (as it is in Spain), they are, in



<P>effect, no more than standing commissions: save



<P>that they have greater authority.&nbsp; Let such as are



<P>to inform counsels, out of their particular profes-



<P>sions (as lawyers, seamen, mintmen, and the like)



<P>be first heard before committees; and then, as oc-



<P>casion serves, before the counsel.&nbsp; And let them not



<P>come in multitudes, or in a tribunitious manner;



<P>for that is to clamor counsels, not to inform them.



<P>A long table and a square table, or seats about the



<P>walls, seem things of form, but are things of sub-



<P>stance; for at a long table a few at the upper end, in



<P>effect, sway all the business; but in the other form,



<P>there is more use of the counsellors' opinions, that



<P>sit lower.&nbsp; A king, when he presides in counsel, let



<P>him beware how he opens his own inclination too



<P>much, in that which he propoundeth; for else



<P>counsellors will but take the wind of him, and in-



<P>stead of giving free counsel, sing him a song of



<P>placebo.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Vain-glory</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Vain-glory</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>IT WAS prettily devised of AEsop, The fly sat



<P>upon the axle-tree of the chariot wheel, and



<P>said, What a dust do I raise! So are there some vain



<P>persons, that whatsoever goeth alone, or moveth



<P>upon greater means, if they have never so little



<P>hand in it, they think it is they that carry it.&nbsp; They



<P>that are glorious, must needs be factious; for all



<P>bravery stands upon comparisons.&nbsp; They must



<P>needs be violent, to make good their own vaunts.



<P>Neither can they be secret, and therefore not ef-



<P>fectual; but according to the French proverb,



<P>Beaucoup de bruit, peu de fruit; Much bruit little



<P>fruit.&nbsp; Yet certainly, there is use of this quality in



<P>civil affairs.&nbsp; Where there is an opinion and fame to



<P>be created, either of virtue or greatness, these men



<P>are good trumpeters.&nbsp; Again, as Titus Livius noteth,



<P>in the case of Antiochus and the AEtolians, There



<P>are sometimes great effects, of cross lies; as if a



<P>man, that negotiates between two princes, to draw



<P>them to join in a war against the third, doth extol



<P>the forces of either of them, above measure, the



<P>one to the other: and sometimes he that deals be-



<P>tween man and man, raiseth his own credit with



<P>both, by pretending greater interest than he hath



<P>in either.&nbsp; And in these and the like kinds, it often



<P>falls out, that somewhat is produced of nothing;



<P>for lies are sufficient to breed opinion, and opinion



<P>brings on substance.&nbsp; In militar commanders and



<P>soldiers, vain-glory is an essential point; for as



<P>iron sharpens iron, so by glory, one courage sharp-



<P>eneth another.&nbsp; In cases of great enterprise upon



<P>charge and adventure, a composition of glorious



<P>natures, doth put life into business; and those that



<P>are of solid and sober natures, have more of the



<P>ballast, than of the sail.&nbsp; In fame of learning, the



<P>flight will be slow without some feathers of osten-



<P>tation.&nbsp; Qui de contemnenda gloria libros scri-



<P>bunt, nomen, suum inscribunt.&nbsp; Socrates, Aristotle,



<P>Galen, were men full of ostentation.&nbsp; Certainly



<P>vain-glory helpeth to perpetuate a man's memory;



<P>and virtue was never so beholding to human na-



<P>ture, as it received his due at the second hand.



<P>Neither had the fame of Cicero, Seneca, Plinius



<P>Secundus, borne her age so well, if it had not been



<P>joined with some vanity in themselves; like unto



<P>varnish, that makes ceilings not only shine but



<P>last.&nbsp; But all this while, when I speak of vain-glory,



<P>I mean not of that property, that Tacitus doth at-



<P>tribute to Mucianus; Omnium quae dixerat fece-



<P>ratque arte quadam ostentator: for that proceeds



<P>not of vanity, but of natural magnanimity and



<P>discretion; and in some persons, is not only comely,



<P>but gracious.&nbsp; For excusations, cessions, modesty



<P>itself well governed, are but arts of ostentation.



<P>And amongst those arts, there is none better than



<P>that which Plinius Secundus speaketh of, which is



<P>to be liberal of praise and commendation to others,



<P>in that, wherein a man's self hath any perfection.



<P>For saith Pliny, very wittily, In commending



<P>another, you do yourself right; for he that you



<P>commend, is either superior to you in that you



<P>commend, or inferior.&nbsp; If he be inferior, if he be to



<P>be commended, you much more; if he be superior,



<P>if he be not to be commended, you much less.



<P>Glorious men are the scorn of wise men, the ad-



<P>miration of fools, the idols of parasites, and the



<P>slaves of their own vaunts.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Youth AND AGE</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Youth AND AGE</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>A MAN that is young in years, may be old in



<P>hours, if he have lost no time.&nbsp; But that hap-



<P>peneth rarely.&nbsp; Generally, youth is like the first



<P>cogitations, not so wise as the second.&nbsp; For there is



<P>a youth in thoughts, as well as in ages.&nbsp; And yet the



<P>invention of young men, is more lively than that



<P>of old; and imaginations stream into their minds



<P>better, and, as it were, more divinely.&nbsp; Natures that



<P>have much heat, and great and violent desires and



<P>perturbations, are not ripe for action, till they have



<P>passed the meridian of their years; as it was with



<P>Julius Caesar and Septimius Severus.&nbsp; Of the latter,



<P>of whom it is said, Juventutem egit erroribus, imo



<P>furoribus, plenam.&nbsp; And yet he was the ablest em-



<P>peror, almost, of all the list.&nbsp; But reposed natures



<P>may do well in youth.&nbsp; As it is seen in Augustus



<P>Caesar, Cosmus Duke of Florence, Gaston de Foix,



<P>and others.&nbsp; On the other side, heat and vivacity in



<P>age, is an excellent composition for business.



<P>Young men are fitter to invent, than to judge; fitter



<P>for execution, than for counsel; and fitter for new



<P>projects, than for settled business.&nbsp; For the experi-



<P>ence of age, in things that fall within the compass



<P>of it, directeth them; but in new things, abuseth



<P>them.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The errors of young men, are the ruin of busi-



<P>ness; but the errors of aged men, amount but to



<P>this, that more might have been done, or sooner.



<P>Young men, in the conduct and manage of actions,



<P>embrace more than they can hold; stir more than



<P>they can quiet; fly to the end, without considera-



<P>tion of the means and degrees; pursue some



<P>few principles, which they have chanced upon



<P>absurdly; care not to innovate, which draws un-



<P>known inconveniences; use extreme remedies at



<P>first; and, that which doubleth all errors, will not



<P>acknowledge or retract them; like an unready



<P>horse, that will neither stop nor turn.&nbsp; Men of age



<P>object too much, consult too long, adventure too



<P>little, repent too soon, and seldom drive business



<P>home to the full period, but content themselves



<P>with a mediocrity of success.&nbsp; Certainly it is good to



<P>compound employments of both; for that will be



<P>good for the present, because the virtues of either



<P>age, may correct the defects of both; and good for



<P>succession, that young men may be learners, while



<P>men in age are actors; and, lastly, good for extern



<P>accidents, because authority followeth old men,



<P>and favor and popularity, youth.&nbsp; But for the moral



<P>part, perhaps youth will have the pre-eminence, as



<P>age hath for the politic.&nbsp; A certain rabbin, upon the



<P>text, Your young men shall see visions, and your



<P>old men shall dream dreams, inferreth that young



<P>men, are admitted nearer to God than old, because



<P>vision, is a clearer revelation, than a dream.&nbsp; And



<P>certainly, the more a man drinketh of the world,



<P>the more it intoxicateth; and age doth profit rather



<P>in the powers of understanding, than in the virtues



<P>of the will and affections.&nbsp; There be some, have an



<P>over-early ripeness in their years, which fadeth



<P>betimes.&nbsp; These are, first, such as have brittle wits,



<P>the edge whereof is soon turned; such as was Her-



<P>mogenes the rhetorician, whose books are exceed-



<P>ing subtle; who afterwards waxed stupid.&nbsp; A second



<P>sort, is of those that have some natural dispositions



<P>which have better grace in&nbsp; youth, than in age;



<P>such as is a fluent and luxuriant speech; which



<P>becomes youth well, but not age: so Tully saith of



<P>Hortensius, Idem manebat, neque idem decebat.



<P>The third is of such, as take too high a strain at the



<P>first, and are magnanimous, more than tract of



<P>years can uphold.&nbsp; As was Scipio Africanus, of



<P>whom Livy saith in effect, Ultima primis cedebant.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>

   <TITLE>glossary</TITLE>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>A Glossary OF ARCHAIC WORDS AND PHRASES</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>Abridgment: miniature



<P>Absurd: stupid, unpolished



<P>Abuse: cheat, deceive



<P>Aculeate: stinging



<P>Adamant: loadstone



<P>Adust: scorched



<P>Advoutress: adulteress



<P>Affect: like, desire



<P>Antic: clown



<P>Appose: question



<P>Arietation: battering-ram



<P>Audit: revenue



<P>Avoidance: secret outlet



<P>Battle: battalion



<P>Bestow: settle in life



<P>Blanch: flatter, evade



<P>Brave: boastful



<P>Bravery: boast, ostentation



<P>Broke: deal in brokerage



<P>Broken: shine by comparison

<P><B><FONT SIZE=+1>The End</FONT></B>

<BR>
