</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Nature IN MEN</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Nature IN MEN</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>NATURE is often hidden; sometimes over-



<P>come; seldom extinguished.&nbsp; Force, maketh



<P>nature more violent in the return; doctrine and dis-



<P>course, maketh nature less importune; but custom



<P>only doth alter and subdue nature.&nbsp; He that seeketh



<P>victory over his nature, let him not set himself too



<P>great, nor too small tasks; for the first will make



<P>him dejected by often failings; and the second will



<P>make him a small proceeder, though by often pre-



<P>vailings.&nbsp; And at the first let him practise with



<P>helps, as swimmers do with bladders or rushes;



<P>but after a time let him practise with disadvan-



<P>tages, as dancers do with thick shoes.&nbsp; For it breeds



<P>great perfection, if the practice be harder than the



<P>use.&nbsp; Where nature is mighty, and therefore the



<P>victory hard, the degrees had need be, first to stay



<P>and arrest nature in time; like to him that would



<P>say over the four and twenty letters when he was



<P>angry; then to go less in quantity; as if one should,



<P>in forbearing wine, come from drinking healths,



<P>to a draught at a meal; and lastly, to discontinue



<P>altogether.&nbsp; But if a man have the fortitude, and



<P>resolution, to enfranchise himself at once, that is



<P>the best:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Optimus ille animi vindex laedentia pectus



<P>Vincula qui rupit, dedoluitque semel.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Neither is the ancient rule amiss, to bend nature,



<P>as a wand, to a contrary extreme, whereby to set it



<P>right, understanding it, where the contrary ex-



<P>treme is no vice.&nbsp; Let not a man force a habit upon



<P>himself, with a perpetual continuance, but with



<P>some intermission.&nbsp; For both the pause reinforceth



<P>the new onset; and if a man that is not perfect, be



<P>ever in practice, he shall as well practise his errors,



<P>as his abilities, and induce one habit of both; and



<P>there is no means to help this, but by seasonable



<P>intermissions.&nbsp; But let not a man trust his victory



<P>over his nature, too far; for nature will lay buried



<P>a great time, and yet revive, upon the occasion or



<P>temptation.&nbsp; Like as it was with AEsop's damsel,



<P>turned from a cat to a woman, who sat very de-



<P>mutely at the board's end, till a mouse ran before



<P>her.&nbsp; Therefore, let a man either avoid the occasion



<P>altogether; or put himself often to it, that he may



<P>be little moved with it.&nbsp; A man's nature is best per-



<P>ceived in privateness, for there is no affectation;



<P>in passion, for that putteth a man out of his pre-



<P>cepts; and in a new case or experiment, for there



<P>custom leaveth him.&nbsp; They are happy men, whose



<P>natures sort with their vocations; otherwise they



<P>may say, multum incola fuit anima mea; when



<P>they converse in those things, they do not affect.



<P>In studies, whatsoever a man commandeth upon



<P>himself, let him set hours for it; but whatsoever is



<P>agreeable to his nature, let him take no care for



<P>any set times; for his thoughts will fly to it, of



<P>themselves; so as the spaces of other business, or



<P>studies, will suffice.&nbsp; A man's nature, runs either to



<P>herbs or weeds; therefore let him seasonably water



<P>the one, and destroy the other.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Seeming Wise</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Seeming Wise</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>IT HATH been an opinion, that the French are



<P>wiser than they seem, and the Spaniards seem



<P>wiser than they are.&nbsp; But howsoever it be between



<P>nations, certainly it is so between man and man.



<P>For as the Apostle saith of godliness, Having a



<P>show of godliness, but denying the power thereof;



<P>so certainly there are, in point of wisdom and suf-



<P>ficiently, that do nothing or little very solemnly:



<P>magno conatu nugas.&nbsp; It is a ridiculous thing, and



<P>fit for a satire to persons of judgment, to see what



<P>shifts these formalists have, and what prospectives



<P>to make superficies to seem body, that hath depth



<P>and bulk.&nbsp; Some are so close and reserved, as they



<P>will not show their wares, but by a dark light; and



<P>seem always to keep back somewhat; and when



<P>they know within themselves, they speak of that



<P>they do not well know, would nevertheless seem



<P>to others, to know of that which they may not well



<P>speak.&nbsp; Some help themselves with countenance



<P>and gesture, and are wise by signs; as Cicero saith



<P>of Piso, that when he answered him, he fetched



<P>one of his brows up to his forehead, and bent the



<P>other down to his chin; Respondes, altero ad fron-



<P>tem sublato,&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; altero ad mentum depresso super-



<P>cilio, crudelitatem tibi non placere.&nbsp; Some think



<P>to bear it by speaking a great word, and being per-



<P>emptory; and go on, and take by admittance, that



<P>which they cannot make good.&nbsp; Some, whatsoever



<P>is beyond their reach, will seem to despise, or make



<P>light of it, as impertinent or curious; and so would



<P>have their ignorance seem judgment.&nbsp; Some are



<P>never without a difference, and commonly by



<P>amusing men with a subtilty, blanch the matter;



<P>of whom A. Gellius saith, Hominem delirum, qui



<P>verborum minutiis rerum&nbsp; frangit pondera.&nbsp; Of



<P>which kind also, Plato, in his Protagoras, bringeth



<P>in Prodius in scorn, and maketh him make a



<P>speech, that consisteth of distinction from the be-



<P>ginning to the end.&nbsp; Generally, such men in all



<P>deliberations find ease to be of the negative side,



<P>and affect a credit to object and foretell difficul-



<P>ties; for when propositions are denied, there is an



<P>end of them; but if they be allowed, it requireth a



<P>new work; which false point of wisdom is the bane



<P>of business.&nbsp; To conclude, there is no decaying mer-



<P>chant, or inward beggar, hath so many tricks to



<P>uphold the credit of their wealth, as these empty



<P>persons have, to maintain the credit of their suf-



<P>ficiency.&nbsp; Seeming wise men may make shift to get



<P>opinion; but let no man choose them for employ-



<P>ment; for certainly you were better take for busi-



<P>ness, a man somewhat absurd, than over-formal.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Plantations</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Plantations</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>PLANTATIONS are amongst ancient, primi-



<P>tive, and heroical works.&nbsp; When the world was



<P>young, it begat more children; but now it is old, it



<P>begets fewer: for I may justly account new plan-



<P>tations, to be the children of former kingdoms.&nbsp; I



<P>like a plantation in a pure soil; that is, where



<P>people are not displanted, to the end, to plant in



<P>others.&nbsp; For else it is rather an extirpation, than a



<P>plantation.&nbsp; Planting of countries, is like planting



<P>of woods; for you must make account to leese al-



<P>most twenty years' profit, and expect your recom-



<P>pense in the end.&nbsp; For the principal thing, that hath



<P>been the destruction of most plantations, hath



<P>been the base and hasty drawing of profit, in the



<P>first years.&nbsp; It is true, speedy profit is not to be neg-



<P>lected, as far as may stand with the good of the



<P>plantation, but no further.&nbsp; It is a shameful and



<P>unblessed thing, to take the scum of people, and



<P>wicked condemned men, to be the people with



<P>whom you plant; and not only so, but it spoileth



<P>the plantation; for they will ever live like rogues,



<P>and not fall to work, but be lazy, and do mischief,



<P>and spend victuals, and be quickly weary, and



<P>then certify over to their country, to the discredit



<P>of the plantation.&nbsp; The people wherewith you



<P>plant ought to be gardeners, ploughmen, laborers,



<P>smiths, carpenters, joiners, fishermen, fowlers,



<P>with some few apothecaries, surgeons, cooks, and



<P>bakers.&nbsp; In a country of plantation, first look about,



<P>what kind of victual the country yields of itself to



<P>hand; as chestnuts, walnuts, pineapples, olives,



<P>dates, plums, cherries, wild honey, and the like;



<P>and make use of them.&nbsp; Then consider what victual



<P>or esculent things there are, which grow speedily,



<P>and within the year; as parsnips, carrots, turnips,



<P>onions, radish, artichokes of Hierusalem, maize,



<P>and the like.&nbsp; For wheat, barley, and oats, they ask



<P>too much labor; but with pease and beans you may



<P>begin, both because they ask less labor, and be-



<P>cause they serve for meat, as well as for bread.&nbsp; And



<P>of rice, likewise cometh a great increase, and it is



<P>a kind of meat.&nbsp; Above all, there ought to be brought



<P>store of biscuit, oat-meal, flour, meal, and the like,



<P>in the beginning, till bread may be had.&nbsp; For beasts,



<P>or birds, take chiefly such as are least subject to



<P>diseases, and multiply fastest; as swine, goats,



<P>cocks, hens, turkeys, geese, house-doves, and the



<P>like.&nbsp; The victual in plantations, ought to be ex-



<P>pended almost as in a besieged town; that is, with



<P>certain allowance.&nbsp; And let the main part of the



<P>ground, employed to gardens or corn, be to a com-



<P>mon stock; and to be laid in, and stored up, and



<P>then delivered out in proportion; besides some



<P>spots of ground, that any particular person will



<P>manure for his own private.&nbsp; Consider likewise



<P>what commodities, the soil where the plantation



<P>is, doth naturally yield, that they may some way



<P>help to defray the charge of the plantation (so it be



<P>not, as was said, to the untimely prejudice of the



<P>main business), as it hath fared with tobacco in



<P>Virginia.&nbsp; Wood commonly aboundeth but too



<P>much; and therefore timber is fit to be one.&nbsp; If there



<P>be iron ore, and streams whereupon to set the mills,



<P>iron is a brave commodity where wood aboundeth.



<P>Making of bay-salt, if the climate be proper for it,



<P>would be put in experience.&nbsp; Growing silk likewise,



<P>if any be, is a likely commodity.&nbsp; Pitch and tar,



<P>where store of firs and pines are, will not fail.&nbsp; So



<P>drugs and sweet woods, where they are, cannot



<P>but yield great profit.&nbsp; Soap-ashes likewise, and



<P>other things that may be thought of.&nbsp; But moil not



<P>too much under ground; for the hope of mines is



<P>very uncertain, and useth to make the planters



<P>lazy, in other things.&nbsp; For government; let it be in



<P>the hands of one, assisted with some counsel; and



<P>let them have commission to exercise martial laws,



<P>with some limitation.&nbsp; And above all, let men make



<P>that profit, of being in the wilderness, as they have



<P>God always, and his service, before their eyes.&nbsp; Let



<P>not the government of the plantation, depend



<P>upon too many counsellors, and undertakers, in



<P>the country that planteth, but upon a temperate



<P>number; and let those be rather noblemen and



<P>gentlemen, than merchants; for they look ever to



<P>the present gain.&nbsp; Let there be freedom from cus-



<P>tom, till the plantation be of strength; and not



<P>only freedom from custom, but freedom to carry



<P>their commodities, where they may make their



<P>best of them, except there be some special cause of



<P>caution.&nbsp; Cram not in people, by sending too fast



<P>company after company; but rather harken how



<P>they waste, and send supplies proportionably; but



<P>so, as the number may live well in the plantation,



<P>and not by surcharge be in penury.&nbsp; It hath been a



<P>great endangering to the health of some planta-



<P>tions, that they have built along the sea and rivers,



<P>in marish and unwholesome grounds.&nbsp; Therefore,



<P>though you begin there, to avoid carriage and



<P>like discommodities, yet build still rather upwards



<P>from the streams, than along.&nbsp; It concerneth like-



<P>wise the health of the plantation, that they have



<P>good store of salt with them, that they may use it



<P>in their victuals, when it shall be necessary.&nbsp; If you



<P>plant where savages are, do not only entertain



<P>them, with trifles and gingles, but use them justly



<P>and graciously, with sufficient guard nevertheless;



<P>and do not win their favor, by helping them to in-



<P>vade their enemies, but for their defence it is not



<P>amiss; and send oft of them, over to the country



<P>that plants, that they may see a better condition



<P>than their own, and commend it when they re-



<P>turn.&nbsp; When the plantation grows to strength, then



<P>it is time to plant with women, as well as with



<P>men; that the plantation may spread into genera-



<P>tions, and not be ever pieced from without.&nbsp; It is the



<P>sinfullest thing in the world, to forsake or destitute



<P>a plantation once in forwardness; for besides the



<P>dishonor, it is the guiltiness of blood of many com-



<P>miserable persons.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>of unity</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Unity IN RELIGION</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>RELIGION being the chief band of human so-



<P>ciety, it is a happy thing, when itself is well



<P>contained within the true band of unity.&nbsp; The



<P>quarrels, and divisions about religion, were evils



<P>unknown to the heathen.&nbsp; The reason was, because



<P>the religion of the heathen, consisted rather in



<P>rites and ceremonies, than in any constant belief.



<P>For you may imagine, what kind of faith theirs



<P>was, when the chief doctors, and fathers of their



<P>church, were the poets.&nbsp; But the true God hath this



<P>attribute, that he is a jealous God; and therefore,



<P>his worship and religion, will endure no mixture,



<P>nor partner.We shall therefore speak a few words,



<P>concerning the unity of the church; what are the



<P>fruits thereof ; what the bounds; and what the



<P>means.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The fruits of unity (next unto the well pleasing



<P>of God, which is all in all) are two: the one, towards



<P>those that are without the church, the other,



<P>towards those that are within.&nbsp; For the former; it is



<P>certain, that heresies, and schisms, are of all others



<P>the greatest scandals; yea, more than corruption



<P>of manners.&nbsp; For as in the natural body, a wound,



<P>or solution of continuity, is worse than a corrupt



<P>humor; so in the spiritual.&nbsp; So that nothing, doth so



<P>much keep men out of the church, and drive men



<P>out of the church, as breach of unity.&nbsp; And there-



<P>fore, whensoever it cometh to that pass, that one



<P>saith, Ecce in deserto, another saith, Ecce in pene-



<P>tralibus; that is, when some men seek Christ, in the



<P>conventicles of heretics, and others, in an outward



<P>face of a church, that voice had need continually



<P>to sound in men's ears, Nolite exire, - Go not out.



<P>The doctor of the Gentiles (the propriety of whose



<P>vocation, drew him to have a special care of those



<P>without) saith, if an heathen come in, and hear



<P>you speak with several tongues, will he not say



<P>that you are mad? And certainly it is little better,



<P>when atheists, and profane persons, do hear of



<P>so many discordant, and contrary opinions in re-



<P>ligion; it doth avert them from the church, and



<P>maketh them, to sit down in the chair of the



<P>scorners. It is but a light thing, to be vouched in so



<P>serious a matter, but yet it expresseth well the



<P>deformity.&nbsp; There is a master of scoffing, that in his



<P>catalogue of books of a feigned library, sets down



<P>this title of a book, The Morris-Dance of Heretics.



<P>For indeed, every sect of them, hath a diverse pos-



<P>ture, or cringe by themselves, which cannot but



<P>move derision in worldlings, and depraved politics,



<P>who are apt to contemn holy things.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As for the fruit towards those that are within; it



<P>is peace; which containeth infinite blessings.&nbsp; It



<P>establisheth faith; it kindleth charity; the outward



<P>peace of the church, distilleth into peace of con-



<P>science; and it turneth the labors of writing, and



<P>reading of controversies, into treaties of mortifica-



<P>tion and devotion.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Concerning the bounds of unity; the true plac-



<P>ing of them, importeth exceedingly.&nbsp; There appear



<P>to be two extremes.&nbsp; For to certain zealants, all



<P>speech of pacification is odious.&nbsp; Is it peace, Jehu,?



<P>What hast thou to do with peace? turn thee be-



<P>hind me.&nbsp; Peace is not the matter, but following,



<P>and party.&nbsp; Contrariwise, certain Laodiceans, and



<P>lukewarm persons, think they may accommodate



<P>points of religion, by middle way, and taking part



<P>of both, and witty reconcilements; as if they would



<P>make an arbitrament between God and man.&nbsp; Both



<P>these extremes are to be avoided; which will be



<P>done, if the league of Christians, penned by our



<P>Savior himself, were in two cross clauses thereof,



<P>soundly and plainly expounded: He that is not



<P>with us, is against us; and again, He that is not



<P>against us, is with us; that is, if the points funda-



<P>mental and of substance in religion, were truly



<P>discerned and distinguished, from points not



<P>merely of faith, but of opinion, order, or good in-



<P>tention.&nbsp; This is a thing may seem to many a matter



<P>trivial, and done already.&nbsp; But if it were done less



<P>partially, it would be embraced more generally.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Of this I may give only this advice, according to



<P>my small model.&nbsp; Men ought to take heed, of rend-



<P>ing God's church, by two kinds of controversies.



<P>The one is, when the matter of the point contro-



<P>verted, is too small and light, not worth the heat



<P>and strife about it, kindled only by contradiction.



<P>For, as it is noted, by one of the fathers, Christ's



<P>coat indeed had no seam, but the church's vesture



<P>was of divers colors; whereupon he saith, In veste



<P>varietas sit, scissura non sit; they be two things,



<P>unity and uniformity.&nbsp; The other is, when the



<P>matter of the point controverted, is great, but it is



<P>driven to an over-great subtilty, and obscurity; so



<P>that it becometh a thing rather ingenious, than



<P>substantial.&nbsp; A man that is of judgment and under-



<P>standing, shall sometimes hear ignorant men dif-



<P>fer, and know well within himself, that those



<P>which so differ, mean one thing, and yet they



<P>themselves would never agree.&nbsp; And if it come so



<P>to pass, in that distance of judgment, which is be-



<P>tween man and man, shall we not think that God



<P>above, that knows the heart, doth not discern that



<P>frail men, in some of their contradictions, intend



<P>the same thing; and accepteth of both? The nature



<P>of such controversies is excellently expressed, by



<P>St. Paul, in the warning and precept, that he giveth



<P>concerning the same, Devita profanas vocum novi-



<P>tates, et oppositiones falsi nominis scientiae.&nbsp; Men



<P>create oppositions, which are not; and put them



<P>into new terms, so fixed, as whereas the meaning



<P>ought to govern the term, the term in effect gov-



<P>erneth the meaning.There be also two false peaces,



<P>or unities: the one, when the peace is grounded,



<P>but upon an implicit ignorance; for all colors will



<P>agree in the dark: the other, when it is pieced up,



<P>upon a direct admission of contraries, in funda-



<P>mental points.&nbsp; For truth and falsehood, in such



<P>things, are like the iron and clay, in the toes of



<P>Nebuchadnezzar's image; they may cleave, but



<P>they will not incorporate.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Concerning the means of procuring unity; men



<P>must beware, that in the procuring, or reuniting,



<P>of religious unity, they do not dissolve and deface



<P>the laws of charity, and of human society.&nbsp; There



<P>be two swords amongst Christians, the spiritual



<P>and temporal; and both have their due office and



<P>place, in the maintenance of religion.&nbsp; But we may



<P>not take up the third sword, which is Mahomet's



<P>sword, or like unto it; that is, to propagate religion



<P>by wars, or by sanguinary persecutions to force



<P>consciences; except it be in cases of overt scandal,



<P>blasphemy, or intermixture of practice against



<P>the state; much less to nourish seditions; to author-



<P>ize conspiracies and rebellions; to put the sword



<P>into the people's hands; and the like; tending to



<P>the subversion of all government, which is the



<P>ordinance of God.&nbsp; For this is but to dash the first



<P>table against the second; and so to consider men



<P>as Christians, as we forget that they are men.



<P>Lucretius the poet, when he beheld the act of Aga-



<P>memnon, that could endure the sacrificing of his



<P>own daughter, exclaimed: Tantum Religio potuit



<P>suadere malorum.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>What would he have said, if he had known of



<P>the massacre in France, or the powder treason of



<P>England? He would have been seven times more



<P>Epicure, and atheist, than he was.&nbsp; For as the tem-



<P>poral sword is to be drawn with great circumspec-



<P>tion in cases of religion; so it is a thing monstrous



<P>to put it into the hands of the common people.&nbsp; Let



<P>that be left unto the Anabaptists, and other furies.



<P>It was great blasphemy, when the devil said, I will



<P>ascend, and be like the highest; but it is greater



<P>blasphemy, to personate God, and bring him in



<P>saying, I will descend, and be like the prince of



<P>darkness; and what is it better, to make the cause



<P>of religion to descend, to the cruel and execrable



<P>actions of murthering princes, butchery of people,



<P>and subversion of states and governments? Surely



<P>this is to bring down the Holy Ghost, instead of the



<P>likeness of a dove, in the shape of a vulture or



<P>raven; and set, out of the bark of a Christian



<P>church, a flag of a bark of pirates, and assassins.



<P>Therefore it is most necessary, that the church, by



<P>doctrine and decree, princes by their sword, and



<P>all learnings, both Christian and moral, as by their



<P>Mercury rod, do damn and send to hell for ever,



<P>those facts and opinions tending to the support of



<P>the same; as hath been already in good part done.



<P>Surely in counsels concerning religion, that coun-



<P>sel of the apostle would be prefixed, Ira hominis



<P>non implet justitiam Dei.&nbsp; And it was a notable



<P>observation of a wise father, and no less ingenu-



<P>ously confessed; that those which held and per-



<P>suaded pressure of consciences, were commonly



<P>interested therein., themselves, for their own ends.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>of truth</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Truth</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>WHAT is truth? said jesting Pilate,and would



<P>not stay for an answer.&nbsp; Certainly there be,



<P>that delight in giddiness, and count it a bondage to



<P>fix a belief; affecting free-will in thinking, as well



<P>as in acting.&nbsp; And though the sects of philosophers



<P>of that kind be gone, yet there remain certain dis-



<P>coursing wits, which are of the same veins, though



<P>there be not so much blood in them, as was in those



<P>of the ancients.&nbsp; But it is not only the difficulty and



<P>labor, which men take in finding out of truth, nor



<P>again, that when it is found, it imposeth upon



<P>men's thoughts, that doth bring lies in favor; but



<P>a natural, though corrupt love, of the lie itself.&nbsp; One



<P>of the later school of the Grecians, examineth the



<P>matter, and is at a stand, to think what should be



<P>in it, that men should love lies; where neither they



<P>make for pleasure, as with poets, nor for advan-



<P>tage, as with the merchant; but for the lie's sake.



<P>But I cannot tell; this same truth, is a naked, and



<P>open day-light, that doth not show the masks, and



<P>mummeries, and triumphs, of the world, half so



<P>stately and daintily as candle-lights.&nbsp; Truth may



<P>perhaps come to the price of a pearl, that showeth



<P>best by day; but it will not rise to the price of a



<P>diamond, or carbuncle, that showeth best in varied



<P>lights.&nbsp; A mixture of a lie doth ever add pleasure.



<P>Doth any man doubt, that if there were taken out



<P>of men's minds, vain opinions, flattering hopes,



<P>false valuations, imaginations as one would, and



<P>the like, but it would leave the minds, of a number



<P>of men, poor shrunken things, full of melancholy



<P>and indisposition, and unpleasing to themselves?

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>One of the fathers, in great severity, called poesy



<P>vinum daemonum, because it fireth the imagina-



<P>tion; and yet, it is but with the shadow of a lie.



<P>But it is not the lie that passeth through the mind,



<P>but the lie that sinketh in, and settleth in it, that



<P>doth the hurt; such as we spake of before.&nbsp; But how-



<P>soever these things are thus in men's depraved



<P>judgments, and affections, yet truth, which only



<P>doth judge itself, teacheth that the inquiry of truth,



<P>which is the love-making, or wooing of it, the



<P>knowledge of truth, which is the presence of it, and



<P>the belief of truth, which is the enjoying of it, is



<P>the sovereign good of human nature.&nbsp; The first



<P>creature of God, in the works of the days, was the



<P>light of the sense; the last, was the light of reason;



<P>and his sabbath work ever since, is the illumina-



<P>tion of his Spirit.&nbsp; First he breathed light, upon the



<P>face of the matter or chaos; then he breathed light,



<P>into the face of man; and still he breatheth and in-



<P>spireth light, into the face of his chosen.&nbsp; The poet,



<P>that beautified the sect, that was otherwise in-



<P>ferior to the rest, saith yet excellently well: It is a



<P>pleasure, to stand upon the shore, and to see ships



<P>tossed upon the sea; a pleasure, to stand in the win-



<P>dow of a castle, and to see a battle, and the adven-



<P>tures thereof below: but no pleasure is comparable



<P>to the standing upon the vantage ground of truth



<P>(a hill not to be commanded, and where the air is



<P>always clear and serene), and to see the errors, and



<P>wanderings, and mists, and tempests, in the vale



<P>below; so always that this prospect be with pity,



<P>and not with swelling, or pride.&nbsp; Certainly, it is



<P>heaven upon earth, to have a man's mind move in



<P>charity, rest in providence, and turn upon the



<P>poles of truth.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>To pass from theological, and philosophical



<P>truth, to the truth of civil business; it will be ac-



<P>knowledged, even by those that practise it not, that



<P>clear, and round dealing, is the honor of man's



<P>nature; and that mixture of falsehoods, is like alloy



<P>in coin of gold and silver, which may make the



<P>metal work the better, but it embaseth it.&nbsp; For these



<P>winding, and crooked courses, are the goings of the



<P>serpent; which goeth basely upon the belly, and



<P>not upon the feet.&nbsp; There is no vice, that doth so



<P>cover a man with shame, as to be found false and



<P>perfidious.&nbsp; And therefore Montaigne saith pret-



<P>tily, when he inquired the reason, why the word



<P>of the lie should be such a disgrace, and such an



<P>odious charge? Saith he, If it be well weighed, to



<P>say that a man lieth, is as much to say, as that he is



<P>brave towards God, and a coward towards men.



<P>For a lie faces God, and shrinks from man.&nbsp; Surely



<P>the wickedness of falsehood, and breach of faith,



<P>cannot possibly be so highly expressed, as in that



<P>it shall be the last peal, to call the judgments of God



<P>upon the generations of men; it being foretold,



<P>that when Christ cometh, he shall not find faith



<P>upon the earth.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Beauty</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Beauty</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>VIRTUE is like a rich stone, best plain set; and



<P>surely virtue is best, in a body that is comely,



<P>though not of delicate features; and that hath



<P>rather dignity of presence, than beauty of aspect.



<P>Neither is it almost seen, that very beautiful per-



<P>sons are otherwise of great virtue; as if nature were



<P>rather busy, not to err, than in labor to produce



<P>excellency.&nbsp; And therefore they prove accom-



<P>plished, but not of great spirit; and study rather



<P>behavior, than virtue.&nbsp; But this holds not always:



<P>for Augustus Caesar, Titus Vespasianus, Philip le



<P>Belle of France, Edward the Fourth of England,



<P>Alcibiades of Athens, Ismael the Sophy of Persia,



<P>were all high and great spirits; and yet the most



<P>beautiful men of their times.&nbsp; In beauty, that of



<P>favor, is more than that of color; and that of decent



<P>and gracious motion, more than that of favor.&nbsp; That



<P>is the best part of beauty, which a picture cannot



<P>express; no, nor the first sight of the life.&nbsp; There is no



<P>excellent beauty, that hath not some strangeness



<P>in the proportion.&nbsp; A man cannot tell whether



<P>Apelles, or Albert Durer, were the more trifler;



<P>whereof the one, would make a personage by geo-



<P>metrical proportions; the other, by taking the best



<P>parts out of divers faces, to make one excellent.



<P>Such personages, I think, would please nobody,



<P>but the painter that made them.&nbsp; Not but I think a



<P>painter may make a better face than ever was; but



<P>he must do it by a kind of felicity (as a musician



<P>that maketh an excellent air in music), and not by



<P>rule.&nbsp; A man shall see faces, that if you examine



<P>them part by part, you shall find never a good;



<P>and yet altogether do well.&nbsp; If it be true that the



<P>principal part of beauty is in decent motion, cer-



<P>tainly it is no marvel, though persons in years



<P>seem many times more amiable; pulchrorum



<P>autumnus pulcher; for no youth can be comely



<P>but by pardon, and considering the youth, as to



<P>make up the comeliness.&nbsp; Beauty is as summer



<P>fruits,) which are easy to corrupt, and cannot last;



<P>and for the most part it makes a dissolute youth,



<P>and an age a little out of countenance; but yet cer-



<P>tainly again, if it light well, it maketh virtue shine,



<P>and vices blush.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Honor AND REPUTATION</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Honor AND REPUTATION</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>THE winning of honor, is but the revealing of



<P>a man,s virtue and worth, without disadvan-



<P>tage.&nbsp; For some in their actions, do woo and effect



<P>honor and reputation, which sort of men, are



<P>commonly much talked of, but inwardly little



<P>admired.&nbsp; And some, contrariwise, darken their



<P>virtue in the show of it; so as they be undervalued



<P>in opinion.&nbsp; If a man perform that, which hath not



<P>been attempted before; or attempted and given



<P>over; or hath been achieved, but not with so good



<P>circumstance; he shall purchase more honor, than



<P>by effecting a matter of greater difficulty or virtue,



<P>wherein he is but a follower.&nbsp; If a man so temper



<P>his actions, as in some one of them he doth content



<P>every faction, or combination of people, the music



<P>will be the fuller.&nbsp; A man is an ill husband of bis



<P>honor, that entereth into any action, the failing



<P>wherein may disgrace him, more than the carry-



<P>ing of it through, can honor him.&nbsp; Honor that is



<P>gained and broken upon another, hath the quick-



<P>est reflection, like diamonds cut with facets.&nbsp; And



<P>therefore, let a man contend to excel any competi-



<P>tors of his in honor, in outshooting them, if he can,



<P>in their own bow.&nbsp; Discreet followers and servants,



<P>help much to reputation.&nbsp; Omnis fama a domesticis



<P>emanat.&nbsp; Envy, which is the canker of honor, is



<P>best extinguished by declaring a man's self in



<P>his ends, rather to seek merit than fame; and by



<P>attributing a man's successes, rather to divine



<P>Providence and felicity, than to his own virtue or



<P>policy.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The true marshalling of the degrees of sovereign



<P>honor, are these: In the first place are conditores



<P>imperiorum, founders of states and common-



<P>wealths; such as were Romulus, Cyrus, Caesar,



<P>Ottoman, Ismael.&nbsp; In the second place are legis-



<P>latores, lawgivers; which are also called second



<P>founders, or perpetui principes, because they gov-



<P>ern by their ordinances after they are gone; such



<P>were Lycurgus, Solon, Justinian, Eadgar, Alphon-



<P>sus of Castile, the Wise, that made the Siete Parti-



<P>das.&nbsp; In the third place are liberatores, or salvatores,



<P>such as compound the long miseries of civil



<P>wars, or deliver their countries from servitude of



<P>strangers or tyrants; as Augustus Caesar, Vespasi-



<P>anus, Aurelianus, Theodoricus, King Henry the



<P>Seventh of England, King Henry the Fourth of



<P>France.&nbsp; In the fourth place are propagatores or



<P>propugnatores imperii; such as in honorable wars



<P>enlarge their territories, or make noble defence



<P>against invaders.&nbsp; And in the last place are patres



<P>patriae; which reign justly, and make the times



<P>good wherein they live.&nbsp; Both which last kinds need



<P>no examples, they are in such number.&nbsp; Degrees of



<P>honor, in subjects, are, first participes curarum,



<P>those upon whom, princes do discharge the great-



<P>est weight of their affairs; their right hands, as



<P>we call them.&nbsp; The next are duces belli, great leaders



<P>in war; such as are princes' lieutenants, and do



<P>them notable services in the wars.&nbsp; The third are



<P>gratiosi, favorites; such as exceed not this scant-



<P>ling, to be solace to the sovereign, and harmless to



<P>the people.&nbsp; And the fourth, negotiis pares; such as



<P>have great places under princes, and execute their



<P>places, with sufficiency.&nbsp; There is an honor, like-



<P>wise, which may be ranked amongst the greatest,



<P>which happeneth rarely; that is, of such as sacri-



<P>fice themselves to death or danger for the good of



<P>their country; as was M. Regulus, and the two



<P>Decii.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Travel</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Travel</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>TRAVEL, in the younger sort, is a part of edu-



<P>cation, in the elder, a part of experience.&nbsp; He



<P>that travelleth into a country, before he hath some



<P>entrance into the language, goeth to school, and



<P>not to travel.&nbsp; That young men travel under some



<P>tutor, or grave servant, I allow well; so that he be



<P>such a one that hath the language, and hath been



<P>in the country before; whereby he may be able



<P>to tell them what things are worthy to be seen, in



<P>the country where they go; what acquaintances



<P>they are to seek; what exercises, or discipline, the



<P>place yieldeth.&nbsp; For else, young men shall go



<P>hooded, and look abroad little.&nbsp; It is a strange thing,



<P>that in sea voyages, where there is nothing to be



<P>seen, but sky and sea, men should make diaries;



<P>but in land-travel, wherein so much is to be ob-



<P>served, for the most part they omit it; as if chance



<P>were fitter to be registered, than observation.&nbsp; Let



<P>diaries, therefore, be brought in use.&nbsp; The things to



<P>be seen and observed are: the courts of princes,



<P>especially when they give audience to ambassa-



<P>dors; the courts of justice, while they sit and hear



<P>causes; and so of consistories ecclesiastic; the



<P>churches and monasteries, with the monuments



<P>which are therein extant; the walls and fortifica-



<P>tions of cities, and towns, and so the heavens and



<P>harbors; antiquities and ruins; libraries; colleges,



<P>disputations, and lectures, where any are; ship-



<P>ping and navies; houses and gardens of state and



<P>pleasure, near great cities; armories; arsenals;



<P>magazines; exchanges; burses; warehouses; exer-



<P>cises of horsemanship, fencing, training of sol-



<P>diers, and the like; comedies, such whereunto the



<P>better sort of persons do resort; treasuries of jewels



<P>and robes; cabinets and rarities; and, to conclude,



<P>whatsoever is memorable, in the places where



<P>they go.&nbsp; After all which, the tutors, or servants,



<P>ought to make diligent inquiry.&nbsp; As for triumphs,



<P>masks, feasts, weddings, funerals, capital execu-



<P>tions, and such shows, men need not to be put in



<P>mind of them; yet are they not to be neglected.&nbsp; If



<P>you will have a young man to put his travel into a



<P>little room, and in short time to gather much, this



<P>you must do.&nbsp; First, as was said, he must have some



<P>entrance into the language before he goeth.&nbsp; Then



<P>he must have such a servant, or tutor, as knoweth



<P>the country, as was likewise said.&nbsp; Let him carry



<P>with him also, some card or book, describing the



<P>country where he travelleth; which will be a good



<P>key to his inquiry.&nbsp; Let him keep also a diary.&nbsp; Let



<P>him not stay long, in one city or town; more or less



<P>as the place deserveth, but not long; nay, when he



<P>stayeth in one city or town, let him change his



<P>lodging from one end and part of the town, to an-



<P>other; which is a great adamant of acquaintance.



<P>Let him sequester himself, from the company of



<P>his countrymen, and diet in such places, where



<P>there is good company of the nation where he



<P>travelleth.&nbsp; Let him, upon his removes from one



<P>place to another, procure recommendation to some



<P>person of quality, residing in the place whither he



<P>removeth; that he may use his favor, in those



<P>things he desireth to see or know.&nbsp; Thus he may



<P>abridge his travel, with much profit.&nbsp; As for the



<P>acquaintance, which is to be sought in travel; that



<P>which is most of all profitable, is acquaintance



<P>with the secretaries and employed men of ambas-



<P>sadors: for so in travelling in one country, he shall



<P>suck the experience of many.&nbsp; Let him also see, and



<P>visit, eminent persons in all kinds, which are of



<P>great name abroad; that he may be able to tell,



<P>how the life agreeth with the fame.&nbsp; For quarrels,



<P>they are with care and discretion to be avoided.



<P>They are commonly for mistresses, healths, place,



<P>and words.&nbsp; And let a man beware,&nbsp; how he keepeth



<P>company with choleric and quarrelsome persons;



<P>for they will engage him into their own quarrels.



<P>When a traveller returneth home, let him not



<P>leave the countries, where he hath travelled, alto-



<P>gether behind him; but maintain a correspond-



<P>ence by letters, with those of his acquaintance,



<P>which are of most worth.&nbsp; And let his travel appear



<P>rather in his discourse, than his apparel or gesture;



<P>and in his discourse, let him be rather advised in



<P>his answers, than forward to tell stories; and let it



<P>appear that he doth not change his country man-



<P>ners, for those of foreign parts; but only prick in



<P>some flowers, of that he hath learned abroad, into



<P>the customs of his own country.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Faction</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Faction</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MANY have an opinion not wise, that for a



<P>prince to govern his estate, or for a great



<P>person to govern his proceedings, according to the



<P>respect of factions, is a principal part of policy;



<P>whereas contrariwise, the chiefest wisdom, is



<P>either in ordering those things which are general,



<P>and wherein men of several factions do neverthe-



<P>less agree; or in dealing with correspondence to



<P>particular persons, one by one.&nbsp; But I say not that



<P>the considerations of factions, is to be neglected.



<P>Mean men, in their rising, must adhere; but



<P>great men, that have strength in themselves, were



<P>better to maintain themselves indifferent, and



<P>neutral.&nbsp; Yet even in beginners, to adhere so moder-



<P>ately, as he be a man of the one faction, which is



<P>most passable with the other, commonly giveth



<P>best way.&nbsp; The lower and weaker faction, is the



<P>firmer in conjunction; and it is often seen, that a



<P>few that are stiff, do tire out a greater number, that



<P>are more moderate.&nbsp; When one of the factions is ex-



<P>tinguished, the remaining subdivideth; as the



<P>faction between Lucullus, and the rest of the



<P>nobles of the senate (which they called Optimates)



<P>held out awhile, against the faction of Pompey



<P>and Caesar; but when the senate's authority was



<P>pulled down, Caesar and Pompey soon after brake.



<P>The faction or party of Antonius and Octavianus



<P>Caesar, against Brutus and Cassius, held out like-



<P>wise for a time; but when Brutus and Cassius were



<P>overthrown, then soon after, Antonius and Octa-



<P>vianus brake and subdivided.&nbsp; These examples are



<P>of wars, but the same holdeth in private factions.



<P>And therefore, those that are seconds in factions,



<P>do many times, when the faction subdivideth,



<P>prove principals; but many times also, they prove



<P>ciphers and cashiered; for many a man's strength



<P>is in opposition; and when that faileth, he groweth



<P>out of use.&nbsp; It is commonly seen, that men, once



<P>placed, take in with the&nbsp; contrary faction, to that



<P>by which they enter: thinking belike, that they



<P>have the first sure, and now are ready for a new



<P>purchase.&nbsp; The traitor in faction, lightly goeth



<P>away with it; for when matters have stuck long in



<P>balancing, the winning of some one man casteth



<P>them, and he getteth all the thanks.&nbsp; The even car-



<P>riage between two factions, proceedeth not always



<P>of moderation, but of a trueness to a man's self,



<P>with end to make use of both.&nbsp; Certainly in Italy,



<P>they hold it a little suspect in popes, when they



<P>have often in their mouth Padre commune: and



<P>take it to be a sign of one, that meaneth to refer all



<P>to the greatness of his own house.&nbsp; Kings had need



<P>beware, how they side themselves, and make



<P>themselves as of a faction or party; for leagues



<P>within the state, are ever pernicious to monarchies:



<P>for they raise an obligation, paramount to obliga-



<P>tion of sovereignty, and make the king tanquam



<P>unus ex nobis; as was to be seen in the League of



<P>France.&nbsp; When factions are carried too high and too



<P>violently, it is a sign of weakness in princes; and



<P>much to the prejudice, both of their authority and



<P>business.&nbsp; The motions of factions under kings



<P>ought to be, like the motions (as the astronomers



<P>speak) of the inferior orbs, which may have their



<P>proper motions, but yet still are quietly carried, by



<P>the higher motion of primum mobile.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Innovations</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Innovations</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>AS THE births of living creatures, at first are ill-



<P>shapen, so are all innovations, which are the



<P>births of time.&nbsp; Yet notwithstanding, as those that



<P>first bring honor into their family, are commonly



<P>more worthy than most that succeed, so the first



<P>precedent (if it be good) is seldom attained by



<P>imitation.&nbsp; For ill, to man's nature, as it stands



<P>perverted, hath a natural motion, strongest in con-



<P>tinuance; but good, as a forced motion, strongest



<P>at first.&nbsp; Surely every medicine is an innovation;



<P>and he that will not apply new remedies, must



<P>expect new evils; for time is the greatest innovator;



<P>and if time of course alter things to the worse, and



<P>wisdom and counsel shall not alter them to the



<P>better, what shall be the end? It is true, that what



<P>is settled by custom, though it be not good, yet at



<P>least it is fit; and those things which have long



<P>gone together, are, as it were, confederate within



<P>themselves; whereas new things piece not so well;



<P>but though they help by their utility, yet they



<P>trouble by their inconformity.&nbsp; Besides, they are



<P>like strangers; more admired, and less favored.&nbsp; All



<P>this is true, if time stood still; which contrariwise



<P>moveth so round, that a froward retention of cus-



<P>tom, is as turbulent a thing as an innovation; and



<P>they that reverence too much old times, are but a



<P>scorn to the new.&nbsp; It were good, therefore, that men



<P>in their innovations would follow the example of



<P>time itself; which indeed innovateth greatly, but



<P>quietly, by degrees scarce to be perceived.&nbsp; For



<P>otherwise, whatsoever is new is unlooked for; and



<P>ever it mends some, and pairs others; and he that



<P>is holpen, takes it for a fortune, and thanks the



<P>time; and he that is hurt, for a wrong, and imput-



<P>eth it to the author.&nbsp; It is good also, not to try experi-



<P>ments in states, except the necessity be urgent, or



<P>the utility evident; and well to beware, that it be



<P>the reformation, that draweth on the change, and



<P>not the desire of change, that pretendeth the refor-



<P>mation.&nbsp; And lastly, that the novelty, though it be



<P>not rejected, yet be held for a suspect; and, as the



<P>Scripture saith, that we make a stand upon the



<P>ancient way, and then look about us, and discover



<P>what is the straight and right way, and so to walk



<P>in it.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Suitors</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>

&nbsp;



<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Suitors</FONT></B>



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>MANY ill matters and projects are under-



<P>taken; and private suits do putrefy the pub-



<P>lic good.&nbsp; Many good matters, are undertaken with



<P>bad minds; I mean not only corrupt minds, but



<P>crafty minds, that intend not performance.&nbsp; Some



<P>embrace suits, which never mean to deal effectu-



<P>ally in them; but if they see there may be life in



<P>the matter, by some other mean, they will be con-



<P>tent to win a thank, or take a second reward, or at



<P>least to make use, in the meantime, of the suitor's



<P>hopes.&nbsp; Some take hold of suits, only for an occa-



<P>sion to cross some other; or to make an informa-



<P>tion, whereof they could not otherwise have apt



<P>pretext; without care what become of the suit,



<P>when that turn is served; or, generally, to make



<P>other men's business a kind of entertainment, to



<P>bring in their own.&nbsp; Nay, some undertake suits,



<P>with a full purpose to let them fall; to the end to



<P>gratify the adverse party, or competitor.&nbsp; Surely



<P>there is in some sort a right in every suit; either a



<P>right of equity, if it be a suit of controversy; or a



<P>right of desert, if it be a suit of petition.&nbsp; If affection



<P>lead a man to favor the wrong side in justice, let



<P>him rather use his countenance to compound the



<P>matter, than to carry it.&nbsp; If affection lead a man



<P>to favor the less worthy in desert, let him do it,



<P>without depraving or disabling the better deserver.



<P>In suits which a man doth not well understand, it



<P>is good to refer them to some friend of trust and



<P>judgment, that may report, whether he may deal



<P>in them with honor: but let him choose well his



<P>referendaries, for else he may be led by the nose.



<P>Suitors are so distasted with delays and abuses,



<P>that plain dealing, in denying to deal in suits at



<P>first, and reporting the success barely, and in chal-



<P>lenging no more thanks than one hath deserved,



<P>is grown not only honorable, but also gracious.&nbsp; In



<P>suits of favor, the first coming ought to take little



<P>place: so far forth, consideration may be had of



<P>his trust, that if intelligence of the matter could



<P>not otherwise have been had, but by him, advan-



<P>tage be not taken of the note, but the party left to



<P>his other means; and in some sort recompensed,



<P>for his discovery.&nbsp; To be ignorant of the value of a



<P>suit, is simplicity; as well as to be ignorant of the



<P>right thereof, is want of conscience.&nbsp; Secrecy in



<P>suits, is a great mean of obtaining; for voicing



<P>them to be in forwardness, may discourage some



<P>kind of suitors, but doth quicken and awake others.



<P>But timing of the suit is the principal.&nbsp; Timing, I



<P>say, not only in respect of the person that should



<P>grant it, but in respect of those, which are like to



<P>cross it.&nbsp; Let a man, in the choice of his mean, rather



<P>choose the fittest mean, than the greatest mean;



<P>and rather them that deal in certain things, than



<P>those that are general.&nbsp; The reparation of a denial,



<P>is sometimes equal to the first grant; if a man



<P>show himself neither dejected nor discontented.



<P>Iniquum petas ut aequum feras is a good rule,



<P>where a man hath strength of favor: but other-



<P>wise, a man were better rise in his suit; for



<P>he, that would have ventured at first to have lost



<P>the suitor, will not in the conclusion lose both the



<P>suitor, and his own former favor.&nbsp; Nothing is



<P>thought so easy a request to a great person, as his



<P>letter; and yet, if it be not in a good cause, it is so



<P>much out of his reputation.&nbsp; There are no worse



<P>instruments, than these general contrivers of suits;



<P>for they are but a kind of poison, and infection, to



<P>public proceedings.



<P>

<HR WIDTH="100%">

</BODY>

</HTML>



<HTML>

<HEAD>

   <META HTTP-EQUIV="Content-Type" CONTENT="text/html; charset=iso-8859-1">

   <META NAME="Author" CONTENT="Yuan Ping">

   <META NAME="GENERATOR" CONTENT="Mozilla/4.04 [en] (Win95; I) [Netscape]">

   <TITLE>Of Seditions AND TROUBLES</TITLE>

</HEAD>

<BODY>





<P><B><FONT SIZE=+2>Of Seditions AND TROUBLES</FONT></B>

<BR>

<HR WIDTH="100%">



<P>SHEPHERDS of people, had need know the



<P>calendars of tempests in state; which are com-



<P>monly greatest, when things grow to equality; as



<P>natural tempests are greatest about the Equinoc-



<P>tia.&nbsp; And as there are certain hollow blasts of wind,



<P>and secret swellings of seas before a tempest, so



<P>are there in states:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>--Ille etiam caecos instare tumultus



<P>Saepe monet, fraudesque et operta tunescere bella.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Libels and licentious discourses against the state,



<P>when they are frequent and open; and in like sort,



<P>false news often running up and down, to the dis-



<P>advantage of the state, and hastily embraced; are



<P>amongst the signs of troubles.&nbsp; Virgil, giving the



<P>pedigree of Fame, saith, she was sister to the Giants:

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Illam Terra parens, irra irritata deorum,



<P>Extremam (ut perhibent) Coeo Enceladoque sororem



<P>Progenuit.-

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>As if fames were the relics of seditions past; but



<P>they are no less, indeed, the preludes of seditions to



<P>come.&nbsp; Howsoever he noteth it right, that seditious



<P>tumults, and seditious fames, differ no more but



<P>as brother and sister, masculine and feminine; es-



<P>pecially if it come to that, that the best actions of



<P>a state, and the most plausible, and which ought



<P>to give greatest contentment, are taken in ill sense,



<P>and traduced: for that shows the envy great, as



<P>Tacitus saith; conflata magna invidia, seu bene



<P>seu male gesta premunt.&nbsp; Neither doth it follow,



<P>that because these fames are a sign of troubles, that



<P>the suppressing of them with too much severity,



<P>should be a remedy of troubles.&nbsp; For the despising



<P>of them, many times checks them best; and the



<P>going about to stop them, doth but make a wonder



<P>long-lived.&nbsp; Also that kind of obedience, which



<P>Tacitus speaketh of, is to be held suspected: Erant



<P>in officio, sed tamen qui mallent mandata impe-



<P>rantium interpretari quam exequi; disputing, ex-



<P>cusing, cavilling upon mandates and directions, is



<P>a kind of shaking off the yoke, and assay of dis-



<P>obedience; especially if in those disputings, they



<P>which are for the direction, speak fearfully and



<P>tenderly, and those that are against it, audaciously.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Also, as Machiavel noteth well, when princes,



<P>that ought to be common parents, make them-



<P>selves as a party, and lean to a side, it is as a boat,



<P>that is overthrown by uneven weight on the one



<P>side; as was well seen, in the time of Henry the



<P>Third of France; for first, himself entered league



<P>for the extirpation of the Protestants; and pres-



<P>ently after, the same league was turned upon him-



<P>self.&nbsp; For when the authority of princes, is made



<P>but an accessory to a cause, and that there be other



<P>bands, that tie faster than the band of sovereignty,



<P>kings begin to be put almost out of possession.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Also, when discords, and quarrels, and factions



<P>are carried openly and audaciously, it is a sign the



<P>reverence of government is lost.&nbsp; For the motions



<P>of the greatest persons in a government, ought to



<P>be as the motions of the planets under primum



<P>mobile; according to the old opinion: which is,



<P>that every of them, is carried swiftly by the



<P>highest motion, and softly in their own motion.



<P>And therefore, when great ones in their own



<P>particular motion, move violently, and, as Tacitus



<P>expresseth it well, liberius quam ut imperan-



<P>tium meminissent; it is a sign the orbs are out



<P>of frame.&nbsp; For reverence is that, wherewith princes



<P>are girt from God; who threateneth the dissolving



<P>thereof; Solvam cingula regum.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>So when any of the four pillars of government,



<P>are mainly shaken, or weakened (which are relig-



<P>ion, justice, counsel, and treasure), men had need



<P>to pray for fair weather.&nbsp; But let us pass from this



<P>part of predictions (concerning which, neverthe-



<P>less, more light may be taken from that which



<P>followeth); and let us speak first, of the materials



<P>of seditions; then of the motives of them; and



<P>thirdly of the remedies.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Concerning the materials of seditions.&nbsp; It is a



<P>thing well to be considered; for the surest way to



<P>prevent seditions (if the times do bear it) is to take



<P>away the matter of them.&nbsp; For if there be fuel pre-



<P>pared, it is hard to tell, whence the spark shall



<P>come, that shall set it on fire.&nbsp; The matter of sedi-



<P>tions is of two kinds: much poverty, and much dis-



<P>contentment.&nbsp; It is certain, so many overthrown



<P>estates, so many votes for troubles.&nbsp; Lucan noteth



<P>well the state of Rome before the Civil War,

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Hinc usura vorax, rapidumque in tempore foenus,



<P>Hinc concussa fides, et multis utile bellum.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>This same multis utile bellum, is an assured and



<P>infallible sign, of a state disposed to seditions and



<P>troubles.&nbsp; And if this poverty and broken estate in



<P>the better sort, be joined with a want and necessity



<P>in the mean people, the danger is imminent and



<P>great.&nbsp; For the rebellions of the belly are the worst.



<P>As for discontentments, they are, in the politic



<P>body, like to humors in the natural, which are apt



<P>to gather a preternatural heat, and to inflame.



<P>And let no prince measure the danger of them by



<P>this, whether they be just or unjust: for that were



<P>to imagine people, to be too reasonable; who do



<P>often spurn at their own good: nor yet by this,



<P>whether the griefs whereupon they rise, be in fact



<P>great or small: for they are the most dangerous



<P>discontentments, where the fear is greater than



<P>the feeling.&nbsp; Dolendi modus, timendi non item.



<P>Besides, in great oppressions, the same things that



<P>provoke the patience, do withal mate the courage;



<P>but in fears it is not so.&nbsp; Neither let any prince, or



<P>state, be secure concerning discontentments, be-



<P>cause they have been often, or have been long, and



<P>yet no peril hath ensued: for as it is true, that every



<P>vapor or fume doth not turn into a storm; so it is



<P>nevertheless true, that storms, though they blow



<P>over divers times, yet may fall at last; and, as the



<P>Spanish proverb noteth well, The cord breaketh at



<P>the last by the weakest pull.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The causes and motives of seditions are, innova-



<P>tion in religion; taxes; alteration of laws and cus-



<P>toms; breaking of privileges; general oppression;



<P>advancement of unworthy persons; strangers;



<P>dearths; disbanded soldiers; factions grown des-



<P>perate; and what soever, in offending people,



<P>joineth and knitteth them in a common cause.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For the remedies; there may be some general



<P>preservatives, whereof we will speak: as for the



<P>just cure, it must answer to the particular disease;



<P>and so be left to counsel, rather than rule.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The first remedy or prevention is to remove, by



<P>all means possible, that material cause of sedition



<P>whereof we spake; which is, want and poverty in



<P>the estate.&nbsp; To which purpose serveth the opening,



<P>and well-balancing of trade; the cherishing of



<P>manufactures; the banishing of idleness; the re-



<P>pressing of waste, and excess, by sumptuary laws;



<P>the improvement and husbanding of the soil; the



<P>regulating of prices of things vendible; the moder-



<P>ating of taxes and tributes; and the like.&nbsp; Generally,



<P>it is to be foreseen that the population of a king-



<P>dom (especially if it be not mown down by wars)



<P>do not exceed the stock of the kingdom, which



<P>should maintain them.&nbsp; Neither is the population



<P>to be reckoned only by number; for a smaller num-



<P>ber, that spend more and earn less, do wear out an



<P>estate sooner, than a greater number that live



<P>lower, and gather more.&nbsp; Therefore the multiply-



<P>ing of nobility, and other degrees of quality, in an



<P>over proportion to the common people, doth speed-



<P>ily bring a state to necessity; and so doth likewise



<P>an overgrown clergy; for they bring nothing to



<P>the stock; and in like manner, when more are bred



<P>scholars, than preferments can take off .

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>It is likewise to be remembered, that forasmuch



<P>as the increase of any estate must be upon the



<P>foreigner (for whatsoever is somewhere gotten, is



<P>somewhere lost), there be but three things, which



<P>one nation selleth unto another; the commodity as



<P>nature yieldeth it; the manufacture; and the vec-



<P>ture, or carriage.&nbsp; So that if these three wheels go,



<P>wealth will flow as in a spring tide.&nbsp; And it cometh



<P>many times to pass, that materiam superabit opus;



<P>that the work and carriage is more worth than the



<P>material, and enricheth a state more; as is notably



<P>seen in the Low-Countrymen, who have the best



<P>mines above ground, in the world.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Above all things, good policy is to be used, that



<P>the treasure and moneys, in a state, be not gath-



<P>ered into few hands.&nbsp; For otherwise a state may



<P>have a great stock, and yet starve.&nbsp; And money is



<P>like muck, not good except it be spread.&nbsp; This is



<P>done, chiefly by suppressing, or at least keeping



<P>a strait hand, upon the devouring trades of usury,



<P>ingrossing great pasturages, and the like.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>For removing discontentments, or at least the



<P>danger of them; there is in every state (as we



<P>know) two portions of subjects; the noblesse and



<P>the commonalty.&nbsp; When one of these is discontent,



<P>the danger is not great; for common people are of



<P>slow motion, if they be not excited by the greater



<P>sort; and the greater sort are of small strength,



<P>except the multitude be apt, and ready to move of



<P>themselves.&nbsp; Then is the danger, when the greater



<P>sort, do but wait for the troubling of the waters



<P>amongst the meaner, that then they may declare



<P>themselves.&nbsp; The poets feign, that the rest of the



<P>gods would have bound Jupiter; which he hearing



<P>of, by the counsel of Pallas, sent for Briareus, with



<P>his hundred hands, to come in to his aid.&nbsp; An em-



<P>blem, no doubt, to show how safe it is for mon-



<P>archs, to make sure of the good will of common



<P>people.&nbsp; To give moderate liberty for griefs and dis-



<P>contentments to evaporate (so it be without too



<P>great insolency or bravery), is a safe way.&nbsp; For he



<P>that turneth the humors back, and maketh the



<P>wound bleed inwards, endangereth malign ulcers,



<P>and pernicious imposthumations.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>The part of Epimetheus mought well become



<P>Prometheus, in the case of discontentments: for



<P>there is not a better provision against them.&nbsp; Epime-



<P>theus, when griefs and evils flew abroad, at last



<P>shut the lid, and kept hope in the bottom of the



<P>vessel.&nbsp; Certainly, the politic and artificial nourish-



<P>ing, and entertaining of hopes, and carrying men



<P>from hopes to hopes, is one of the best antidotes



<P>against the poison of discontentments.&nbsp; And it is a



<P>certain sign of a wise government and proceeding,



<P>when it can hold men's hearts by hopes, when it



<P>cannot by satisfaction; and when it can handle



<P>things, in such manner, as no evil shall appear so



<P>peremptory, but that it hath some outlet of hope;



<P>which is the less hard to do, because both particu-



<P>lar persons and factions, are apt enough to flatter



<P>themselves, or at least to brave that, which they



<P>believe not.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Also the foresight and prevention, that there be



<P>no likely or fit head, whereunto discontented per-



<P>sons may resort, and under whom they may join,



<P>is a known, but an excellent point of caution.&nbsp; I



<P>understand a fit head, to be one that hath great-



<P>ness and reputation; that hath confidence with



<P>the discontented party, and upon whom they turn



<P>their eyes; and that is thought discontented, in his



<P>own particular: which kind of persons, are either



<P>to be won, and reconciled to the state, and that in



<P>a fast and true manner; or to be fronted with some



<P>other, of the same party, that may oppose them,



<P>and so divide the reputation.&nbsp; Generally, the divid-



<P>ing and breaking, of all factions and combinations



<P>that are adverse to the state, and setting them at



<P>distance, or at least distrust, amongst themselves,



<P>is not one of the worst remedies.&nbsp; For it is a desper-



<P>ate case, if those that hold with the proceeding of



<P>the state, be full of discord and faction, and those



<P>that are against it, be entire and united.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>I have noted, that some witty and sharp



<P>speeches, which have fallen from princes, have



<P>given fire to seditions.&nbsp; Caesar did himself infinite



<P>hurt in that speech, Sylla nescivit literas, non po-



<P>tuit dictare; for it did utterly cut off that hope,



<P>which men had entertained, that he would at one



<P>time or other give over his dictatorship.&nbsp; Galba un-



<P>did himself by that speech, legi a se militem, non



<P>emi; for it put the soldiers out of hope of the dona-



<P>tive.&nbsp; Probus likewise, by that speech, Si vixero,



<P>non opus erit amplius Romano imperio militibus;



<P>a speech of great despair for the soldiers.&nbsp; And



<P>many the like.&nbsp; Surely princes had need, in tender



<P>matters and ticklish times, to beware what they



<P>say; especially in these short speeches, which fly



<P>abroad like darts, and are thought to be shot out of



<P>their secret intentions.&nbsp; For as for large&nbsp; discourses,



<P>they are flat things, and not so much noted.

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;

<BR>&nbsp;



<P>Lastly, let princes, against all events, not be



<P>without some great person, one or rather more, of



<P>military valor, near unto them, for the repressing



<P>of seditions in their beginnings.&nbsp; For without that,



<P>there useth to be more trepidation in court upon



<P>the first breaking out of troubles, than were fit.



<P>And the state runneth the danger of that which



<P>Tacitus saith; Atque is habitus animorum fuit, ut



<P>pessimum facinus auderent pauci, plures vellent,



<P>omnes paterentur.&nbsp; But let such military persons be



<P>assured, and well reputed of, rather than factious



<P>and popular; holding also good correspondence



<P>with the other great men in the state; or else the



<P>remedy, is worse than the disease.



<P>
